The RED PAGE
[This seems to be a new form. The
sestina on which it is based is too clumsy
for English verse; but something might be
done with the Sestinette. If there are any
triers, £1 Is. will be paid for the best
verse on the above model, sent in before the

SESTINETTE.
My Lady in her garden threw away
A full-blown rose of richly vivid red
That matched the blood that from my
heart had bled,
What time my Lady fair had said me “Nay,”
When I had asked her for her love alway,
“That she could only lend it me instead.”
That she would onlv lend it me instead,
Was tragedy enow for me. Alway
I wanted her, nor did believe her “Nay”
Until she wrung my soul, when therefrom
bled
My love, as richly hued as that rose red
My Lady in her garden threw away.
My Lady in her warden threw away
A rose as red as from my heart had bled
All love alway, what time she told me
That she could only lend it me instead.”
vie.
E. PERUGINI.
THE OLD TREE.
This is the tree ’neath which we sat together
In the pleasant summer weather long ago ;
In its branches green and gold the birds sang dear
and bold.
While the fair blue sea broke into foam below.
Now is the time of winter, and all the birds are
silent,
Grey sky above, a greyer sea below.
Listless I sit alone, thy tired eyes idly watching
Brown leaves on rain-soaked earth blow to and
fro.
O I know that in the spring the birds will blithely
mu Sing ’
The tree be green, and winter cease to reign;
But what spring has power to cast the old-time
spell, or bring
Lost Helen to my longing arms again?
N.S.W.
M. R. HUNTER.
ACROSS THE STREET.
Across the street a hundred hammers ring,
Day after day, unceasing is their chime;
A common sound of common daily toil,
Yet to my woman’s ear it is sublime.
Why is it that, above all other sounds,
I seem to hear one hammer’s steady beat?
And to my mind there is but one strong form
Bent o’er his labor, out across the street?
And why, when toil seems hard and life
seems wrong,
Should I but catch that hammer’s steady
ring,
Why does my heart with sudden lightness
rise
To see fresh hope and joy in everything?
’Tis love that calls me, calls me all day long,
Over the busy noise and dusty heat:
Dear God! I thank Thee for the happy sound
Of just one hammer, out across the street.
VIC.
MINNIE M. DOUGHENEY.
AUSTRALIAN WRITERS—I.
John Arthur Barry
When a man has written a good deal about
this country, and has lived in it for a num-
ber of years, he is justly claimed as an Aus-
tralian writer, no matter where he happened
to be born. The next best thing to being
born in Australia is being born in'a place
like Devon, which gave Drake and many
famous men to naval history. So J. A.
Barry began well by arriving in this life at
Torquay, in the year 1850. He went the
proper Devonshire way, by taking to ship-
board at the age of 14. Since then he has
had experiences on sea and land that fall
to the lot of few who write, and further on
is printed his own brief summary of his
career to date.
Barry’s family in England knew the
Lockwood Iviplings, and when young Rud-
yard came from India to go to school, an
acquaintance began which resulted, years
afterwards, in Barry’s first book being
launched from a London publisher’s with
a ribbon of Kipling’s verse streaming from
the fore-peak. (A nautical simile comes
naturally after reading so many of Barry’s
yarns.) The book was Steve Brown’s
Bunyip (1893), reprinted six times in Eng-
land, and issued again in a new edition by
the N.S.W. Bookstall Co. in 1905. It was
followed by In the G r cat Deep (1895), The
Luck of the Native Born (1898: reprinted
by N.S.W. Bookstall Co., 1908), A Bon of
the Sea (1899), Against the Tides of Fate
(1899), Red Lion and Blue Star (1902)
and Sea Yarns (1910). Old and New Syd-
ney (1903) represents material grubbed out
of the records, and/ dressed up to make a con-
tinuous history of the city. It was by far
the hardest job of the lot, for, to all good
yarn spinners, the writing of formal history
is something like Writing in a straight-jacket.
All save one of the books above mentioned
contain odd lengths of yarn. In two cases,
The Luck ' of the Native Born and A Bon
of the Sea, it runs as a continuous narra-
tive from cover to cover. These are not
novels; the plots are too slight, and the
characterisation weak: they are episodes
through which the hero moves ,to his des-
tined end, and could be resolved into ele-
ments similar to the short stories in the
other books.
The main thing about all the yarns is
that things happen in them, and happen
quickly. They comprise a large assort-
ment of mining and droving adventures,
piracy, salvage, wrecks, murders, and hilar-
ious comedy. The grim tragedy of “Sojur
Jim” is a few pages from the amusing
“Books at Barracaboo”; the ghastly “Red
Warder of the Reef” rubs shoulders with thJe
comical fight between the Red Lion and
Blue Star captains.
Barry is most at home when his yarns are
at sea, and one deduces from them that he
has met more good captains than bad, that
the men of to-day are as capable as those
of the past, and that it is a great thing to go
to sea, but better to leave it. He does not
attempt “fine writing”; and his yarns de-
pend upon incident rather than style. In
them all will be found a hearty spirit in
vigorous action, and his books have an hon-
orable place in the small company of good
sea stories.
The Author now answers “a call” :
“What did you eome to sea for, you d—d
fool?” was a question often put to me dur-
ing the course of my first voyage, by ship-
mates fore and aft the swift little Australian
trader in which I partly served my time.
And no matter what the reply might be,
it was capped by“ The man who’d come
to sea for pleasure ’d go to Hell for pas-
time.” +
And, certainly, the first voyage was some-
what of a nightmare. But rather than
confess it when one got ashore again among
admiring friends and schoolfellows—gilt
buttons shining on the smart uniform, houser
T. A. BARRY.
flag flying on the goid-braided cap—one
would suffer worse tortures than ever fell
to the lot of green sea-apprentice.
This was in the early ’6o’s; and I was
just 14 when a pig of a second mate, as soon
as I set foot on board, gave me my first
, taste of “a life on the ocean wave” by de-
manding—“ What in Hell’s blazes I was
gawping at?” Adding, as he dipped my
hands into a tar-bpcket, that —“Gentlemen’s
sons on board this packet ain’t no bet-
ter than blackfeller’s dawgs.”
And so we apprentices—“owners’ hard
bargains,” as the men called us—found it.
But the next voyage, in another vessel, was
a pleasanter experience. The third, in my
own estimation, turned me out a hardened
salt. Then, tired of the somewhat monoton-
ous Australian trade, and dreaming of the
‘ Tropics,” I changed over into the same
firm’s West Indian vessels. Rum, sugar,
and molasses —Savannah le Mar, Montego
Bay, and various other ports. Shockingly
slow work on the old droghers, after the
dashing passages made by the beautiful clip-
pers that we ran to and from the. Antipodes
with the first clips of the season.
“Yellow Jack” presently gave me more
“Tropics” than I had bargained for, and
sent me home, just out of my time, for a
spell among the Devonshire orchards.
To China in a “flier” was the next trip-
third of her. Ocean-racing with the early
teas; mending sails by lamplight, and bend-
ing them in a gale of wind ; carrying on in
weather that other ships liove-to in ; leaving
a wake of stuns’l-booms from the River Min
to the Western Islands. Wet, always wet;
decks a-wash. A double crew standing by
to make and take in sail as the big yacht
roared _ across the seas, smothered in seeth-
ing brine. A thousand pounds to the first
ship home ! We came second in one of the
great historic races.
Sometimes a shipmate of those distant
years turns up, and we drink to the memory
of “Ariel,” “Sir Lancelot,” and all their
noble, vanished company; and to the health
of the splendifl'Aailormen who ran them.
Then followed a long spell in Far Eastern
waters on craft of various kinds and of
varying degrees of iniquity, carrying any-
thing from coolies to “general,” and not sel-
dom “contraband.”
By this time I had passed for “first.” But
I never stood on the order of my going—
was as often in the fo’c’s’le as on the poop.
Thus, one day, ' ilyblown” Singapore, I
shipped A.B. on a barque bound for Ade-
laide. She turned out the hungriest sort
of “limejuicer” conceivable. To appease
our clamorous bellies we made a hole in heir
cargo as big as The Bulletin office. On
arrival at the Semaphore, to escape conse-
quences, the same night we pulled ashore
and disappeared into a mangrove swamp,
turning up later on at the Echunga Dig-
gings, and riddling “Sailor Gully” with
shafts of grotesque design. For a few
months it was good fun and bare “tucker.”
Tnen the sea began calling.
Early in the 70’s, after several voyages
to South Africa and the East Indies, I
picked myself up at Brisbane, mate of a
small British ship. The Palmer breaking
out, I left her quite informally, and jour-
neyed up-country with several other ven-
turesome sailormen —some, of whom left
their bones there. And in due course I
crawled back to Cooktown with the sur-
vivors, dead broke and half-blind with sandy
blight.
A few years among the South Sea Islands
and on the coast, and then for the second
time I strayed into the bush, and entered on
a new phase of existence, ranging from
“humping bluey,” droving, and boundary-
riding, to managing a station. “Coffee in
the morning and no fore-royal” bow. All
night in and “full and plenty.” No more
keeping of dreary watches; no more claw-
ing one’s way aloft buffeted by wind, and
drenched with rain. No “bucko” mates and
hazing skippers ; no running foul of hungry
hookers, where the diet consisted mainly of
weevils and limejuice!
It was in the Bush that the making of
stories began. I was a sheep overseer at
the time; and one day, taking a certain
titled personage to inspect a run, the two
of us escaped a bad bush fire by the skin of
our teeth. A letter to literary folk at home
relating the incident, probably, somewhat
embroidered, found its way in guise of a
descriptive article into the columns of a big
London daily newspaper.
And presently a cheque, equal to several
months’ salary, made a simple sailorman
think that a glittering and easy way to
wealth illimitable had suddenly opened out
before him.
Thus, as time passed, many Australian
newspapers, including, of course, The Bul-
letin, heard from me. And, on the whole
—although not financially following the
London daily’s example to any considerable
extent —they treated me very well indeed,
and printed, and paid for, heaps of sea and
bush stories. So did the English and
American magazines. Of some British pub-
lishers, perhaps, the least said the better.
Nevertheless, they have produced seven
books of mine.
For a good many years the settled groove
of uneventful city journalism has claimed
me. But at intervals there comes a desire
to put more sea into printed words. A
continuous story, South Sea Sailormen, is
now on offer in the London markets.
* 1 +
In The Pearl and the Octopus (G. Rob-
ertson and Co.), A. G. Stephens has re-
printed from two of his earlier books and
some newspapers a collection which he aptly
describes as “exercises in prose and verse.”
There are two stories in which fantastic
themes are cleverly handled, though not
cleverly enough to make them convincing.
The best part of the book is in verse, and
for sheer smartness the exercises in tricky
forms, and the parodies of Lawson, Bren-
nan and other Australian poets would en-
title the writer to high marks. An estimate
of Parkes, “our mightiest Son of Thunder,”
is neatly stated, and a sonnet called “The
Last Camp” attracts attention by its quiet
note of pathos. Most of the verses gleam
for a moment like soap-bubbles ; they shine,
but do not sing. Perhaps the finest thing
in the collection —not cleverest, but sincerest
—is the end-piece, “Antsean,” from which
two stanzas are quoted—
So when weary of errors and misconceptions
I go where the Bush fronts grand to the
sky;
And my small weak life is merged in the
Whole,
And the soul of me’s lost in the general
Soul,
And bland airs blow from this hot, tired I
All its worries, and griefs, and pigmy decep-
tions.
And the Bush bends closer and holds my
hands:
And her noons or her nights yearn low to
listen
When I tell her all in her inmost shrine,
And receive absolution for follies o’ mine;
Her mouth may smile, but her grave eyes
glisten—
She is my lover, and understands.
4O
Ivan A. Rosenblum, of Melbourne,
made a mistake in writing The Drama
Eternal (Elliot Stock) as a story in
verse. A narrative poem runs the risk
of dull patches, even when the story,
as such,, is a good one. In this case the
story” is of the flimsiest—a dream which
was real, and a girl who is unreal, with a
philosopher and a Voice making remarks.
The blank verse flows easily, but without
the imaginative lift which would carry it
over the prosiness of the subject matter.
Yet his philosopher gave the author some
idea of what poetry is like:—
Knowledge is twice a power: it adds a charm
lo Beauty, and hath its own grace as well.
Science reveals new secrets in a rose;
But Truth and Poetry are sun and flower
Poetry is not the art of facile phrase
Mere metrical felicity, nor trick
Of dainty syllables. It needs no verse,
Singling in artful rhyme. But it demands
Ihe passionate flow of words sincerely sweet
The billowy tidal wave of bursting thought
Rushing resistless to eternity—
(Hath not the ocean form? Hath not the
sky?)— /
Rosenblum has no mean facility in turning
out melodious lines, and justifies the belief
that he may become a flourishing minor
bard.
A book of verses by Miss Dorothea Mac-
kellar, of Sydney, is announced for publica-
tion in Melbourne. Some of her contribu-
tions to this paper and the London Spec-
tator are of fine quality, and if the book
contains a few like the following it will be
well worth buying:—
Sam Walter Foss, the genial Yankee poet,
died at Somerville, Mass., on the 26th Feb-
ruary. He had had a rough time in youth;
started on the Inky Way at the age of 25,
and stayed on it till 40, when he dropped
into a public librariansliip which he held
up to the time of his death at 53. A back-
yard Longfellow, whose homely wit ranged
him with Whitcomb Riley, he enjoyed con
siderable popularity in his lifetime. Space
will be found for some quotations next week.
Cabled that William Butler Yeats has
been granted a Civil List pension. What
was it that Tennyson said to the poets?—
Dosn’t thou ’ear old Peg’sus’ legs as they
canters awaay?
Poverty, poverty, poverty —that’s what I
’ears ’em saiiy.
* * * *
Amongst the common objects of the sea-
shore which are full of mystery, when
properly considered, are the round pieces of
stone known as pebbles. They had not re-
ceived adequate attention until E. J. Dunn,
F.G.S., of Melbourne, took them up, and the
result oi his exhaustive study is a book —
Peebles (Geo. Robertson and Co.). It might
be thought by the careless that there are no
other uses for pebbles than to make “ducks
and drakes” for idle boys, or missiles for
the Dunn has discovered many other
uses in ancient times and present; he* dwells
upon the variety of their beautiful markings,
explains how pebbles came into being, and
how they have been carried from one place
to another; and he adds 76 plates to illus-
trate his discourse. So far as mortal man
can do it, Dunn has said all there is to say
about pebbles.
CORE OF MY HEART.
The love of field and coppice,
Of green and shaded lanes,
Of ordered woods and gardens
Is running through my veins;
Strong love of grey-blue distance,
Brown streams, and soft, dim skies,
I know, but cannot share it,
My love is otherwise.
I love a sunburnt country,
A land of sweeping plains,
Of ragged mountain ranges,
Of droughts and flooding rains ;
I love her far horizons,
I love her level sea,
Her beauty and her terror —
The wide brown land for me!
The stark white ring-barked forests,
All tragic ’neath the moon,
The sapphire-misted mountains
The hot gold hush of noon;
Green tangle of the bushes,
Where lithe lianas coil,
And orchids deck the tree-tops,
And ferns the crimson soil.
Core of my heart, my country!
Her pitiless blue sky,
When sick at heart around us
We see the cattle die.
And then the grey clouds gather,
And we can bless again
The drumming of an army—
The steady, soaking rain.
Core of my heart, mj r country!
Land of the rainbow gold !
For flood and fire and famine
She pays us back threefold.
Over the thirsty paddocks,
Watch, after many days,
The filmy veil of greenness,
Tnat thickens as you gaze.
An opal-hearted country,
A wilful lavish land:
And you who have not loved her,
You will not understand.
Though earth holds many splendors,
Mherever I may die,
I know to _ what brown country
My homing thoughts will fly.
DOROTHEA MACKELL[?].

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Life and Myself.
Life and myself were ever friends,
And though in some respects it fails me,
Its gifts most amply make amends;
It still abides when woe assails me;
And I would be a graceless debtor
Did I not try to make Life better.
For Life is ill, so some folks say,
And sore oppressed with toil and trouble;
Yet when in joyous ways we stray,
We prick its illness like a bubble:
Life’s ills —they’ve often joys behind them —
Are mostly with the folk who find them.
They gird at Life who never knew
How glorious is simply Irving,
I pity all that cheerless crew
Who miss the bliss Pa Time is giving;
The joy of life in warm veins seething;
A pulse of joy in simply breathing.
Dear Life! that fills my glowing veins
With warmth and "sense Of pulse and feeling,
That stays with me through wounds and pains,
To swiftly give my sorrow healing,
There is no gift that you can give
As glorious as the gift to live!
N.s.w.
HAMER.
The Silent Land.
Were I as dead as Solomon, Stylites-like and calm
I’d rest my graveyard-column on, and sing my
ghostly' psalm 1
Were I nailed perpendicular, to lift a martyr’s
wheeze,
I would not be particular nor bother with M.P.’s
But being one who listeneth unto the daily roar
Where Mace or Rod bedizeneth, I rise and shut the
door!
Let politicians far lament, where Peace and Sense
are banned—
Far off from blaring Parliament I hear the Silent
Land !
I hear Australia reckoning—whilst P.M.G.’s ex-
plain,
And Ministers are beckoning, thoughts flash within
her brain !
Australia Thinks I —eternally I hear her speechless
prayer;
Whilst members rage infernally, she counts the
beads of Care!
The Silent Land is hearkening, no sentence speaketh
she;
And yet with brow swift-darkening she damns each
fool M.P. 1
With eyeballs wildly glistening they hector and
demand;
I see Australia listening—the scornful Silent Land 1
She is the Witness critical who gazes oh the scene;
She watches analytical the paltry souls and mean!
She is the Judge imperious, whom members cannot
see;
The One far-off, mysterious, whose soul-thoughts
speak to me !
When threats are swiftly muttering and members
leap and pace,
I hear her spirit uttering athwart the leagues of
space!
She is the Judge inscrutable, the sword within her
hand ; '
Erect and stern, immutable—behold the Silent
Land !
The dotards and F# palterers she scans with
thoughtful eye—
The failures and the falterers who pass in legions
by! ’
She hears each ancient platitude—incarnate Scorn
she seems
When Johnson strikes an attitude or Willy Kelly
screams!
No word of praise she uttereth, nor yet of doubt
nor blame;
But, lo! the nation muttereth her thoughts of
sweeping flame!
She is the One unperishing whose judgments sting
* and brand ;
The Witness nation-cherishing—hear ye the Silent
Land !
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.
The Sabbath-Breaker.
They are looking at me, good Christian folk,
They are looking at me in scorn,
As they troop to church in their Sabbath dress,
And I lounge here in idleness
This glorious Sunday morn.
They are sniffing at me with Christian sniffs,
As they pass me, garbed in gloom ;
Right glad am I as I sprawl at ease,
With a pipe and a book beneath the trees;
But they’ye marked me down for Doom.
They are gazing at me, good Christian folk,
And their gaze is dour and stern;
And their eyes are hard, and their lips are long,
For they heard me trolling a worldly song,
And they look to see me burn.
Nay, what have I done, good Christian folk?
And how have I earned your scorn ?
May I not be filled with joy to see
The gifts the good God sends to me
On this glad Sabbath morn?
Would ye have me wear a bilious air,
And clothe myself in gloom,
And don my best black Sunday dress,
And walk in mournful righteousness,
And ponder on the Tomb ?
Nay, but all Nature laughs, good folk—
Laughs at your mood austere.
The festive birds, the joyous trees,
The wooing of the wanton breeze,
All bid me tarry here.
They are coming from church, good Christian folk,
And their gloom has deepened thrice.
They are pondering what the preacher said
Of the mouldy grave and the wormy dead;
They are storing his sage advice.
They are looking at me again. God wot
How have I earned such blame!
I feel glad life with ev’ry breath;
I cannot meditate on death
Nor count my joy a shame.
Nay, let me be, good Christian folk.
I pray ye let me rest.
For I cannot join ye here below;
If I join ye not where’er ye go,
I am quite content to have it so ;
For I should be sore oppressed.
VICTORIA.

The Royal Bank of Australia Limited.
Head Office : Melbourne, Victoria.

rOrTy-OIXZn rzcpori general meeting on april 27, ion.
THE Directors have pleasure in submitting: to Shareholders the annexed Balance-Sheet and Statement of Accounts
for the Half-Year ending March 31, duly audited and certified.
The net profits (including £3462 15s. lid. brought from last Half-Year) amount to ..£25,358 1, 1
which it is proposed to deal with as follows, viz.
To pay a dividend at the rate of 7 per cent, per annum .. .. £10,500 0
~ Transfer to the Reserve Fund (making it £150,000) .. .. .. .. .. .. 10,000 0
„• Pay a bonus to the staff « .. .. 1,366 0
And to carry forward the balance .. .. .. .. .. 3,492 1
£25,358 1 1
The Directors are glad to be able to congratulate Shareholders on a successful Half-Year. Trade has been
active, and the volume of business has expanded considerably, leading to a gratifying increase in profits.
Towards the end of the Half-Year a Branch was opened at Footscray, where the Directors are encouraged to
believe, a fair business can be secured in time.
On this occasion the Directors have pleasure in recommending the payment of a bonus to the staff at Head Office
and Branches of 10 per cent, on their salaries, which they trust will meet with the approval of Shareholders.
The assistance of Shareholders in spreading the business of the bank has proved of such value in bygone years
that they are once more urged to use their influence in securing new accounts for the bank.
By Order of the Board, H. T. WILSON, General Manager.

MEDLOW BATH, Blue Mowlams. N.S.W.
MAJESTIC HOTEL.
COMPLETE arrangements have been made by the new proprietor (Mr. W. H. Burgess), late of the Grand
Hotel, Melbourne, for the comfort of visitors during the forthcoming season.
Medlow Bath is an ideal resort. The crisp mountain air is a delight, and the mountains, in their
Spring garb, appeal to lovers of the picturesque. Arrangements can be made at the Hotel for motors,
carriages and riding tours.
Motor parties can be arranged any time to start from the City Office —or your own residence direct
to the Majestic Hotel.
To pedestrians Medlow offers ever-varying attractions. The views in the mountains are superb, and
hours can be spent in walks through the Hotel grounds.
Hot-air pipes are laid in many rooms. Daily Motor trips are made from the Hotel to Jenolan Caves.
Further particulars can be obtained from the Hotel, or from the Sydney Offices, 42 Oastlereagh Street.
W. H. BURGESS, Proprietor.

“THE FIVE NATIONS.”
LONDON, Wednesday.—The TIMES states this morning that the Dominions are being driven at a dozen points into the practical world of politics on their own account, and
are acquiring interests sometimes conflicting with Britain’s. Unless there is a mutual understanding, the five nations will find it impossible to continue the system whereby British
foreign policy remains a sealed book to all but one partner. “It is necessary,” the paper adds, “that our policy be strengthened by becoming Imperial and securing the
conscious and continuous support of the Governments overseas.”

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper {and. to an extent, themselves ) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters {with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them •to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, APRIL 27, 1911.
THE SHOWING-UP OF PARTY
GOVERNMENT.
At the time of writing the jury has, in a
manner of speaking, retired to consider its
verdict on the Referenda questions, and
what the outcome will be no man knows.
All that is certain is that at least one bene-
ficial result must accrue from the campaign,
namely, the further discrediting of our ab-
surd system of Party Government. A
country which has for years endured repre-
sentatives like J. Cook and George Reid
can evidently stomach a lot in the way of
political insincerity, but when it comes to
whole batches of'politicians cheerfully going
back an their oft and loudly expressed con-
victions, at the behest of the Party bosses,
the scandal grows indecently clamorous, and
the moment for a violent kick of protest ap-
proaches.
* * * *
For months Alfred Deakin has been
fighting the Constitutional amendments ask-ed
for by the Government; _ and yet, unless he
prevaricated senselessly in the past, he fa-
vors all of them. The New Protection—
the policy which the Fisher Government’s
Trade and Commerce clause was designed
to make operative —was Deakin’s own
measure, defeated only by a High Court
decision, which came as a complete surprise
to all parties. The present leader of “Lib-
eralism” used not to be the sort of I rotec-
tionist who sees no merit in a tariff unless
it aggrandises the manufacturer, and the
manufacturer alone. In 1907 he, said :
To restrict the powers of the Commonwealth to
the mere imposition of duties,' while the conditions
under which the manufacture ,of protected articles
is carried on differ so widely in the different States,
would be to permit inequality, discrimination and
discord. The ideal of the Commonwealth is equality
and uniformity in all industrial matters. Effective
and useful as .State industrial laws have in many
cases proved, their operation is circumscribed by
State boundaries, and it can hardly be claimed for
them that they either do or can secure uniformity
in the conditions of manufacture throughout Aus-
tralia. No, authority but the Commonwealth Par-
liament can do this.
. And in 190 S he said :
As the power to protect' the manufacturer is
national, it follows that, unless the Parliament of
the Commonwealth also acquires powers to secure
fair and reasonable conditions of employment to
wage-earners, the policy of Protection must remain
incomplete.
Australia has heard nothing like that from
Alfred Deakin of late; yet what was good
logic and sense at the end of 1908 should
have been equally so at the beginning of
1911. And more consistent Anti-Laborites
than Deaktn expressed similar views in the
days when the possibility of a Labor Govern-
ment, with a straight-out majority, was not
entertained by anyone. For example, W.
H. Irvine —the same Irvine who has been
arguing recently that Ruin and Revolution
will smite Australia if the Federal Parlia-
ment’s powers are enlarged as proposed—
said two years ago:—
I say that the people of this country properly
demand that the experiment of attempting to regu-
late wages and conditions of labor shall be honestly
and fairly carried out on all sides. That being the
desire, we ought not to impose technical objections
to prevent its being carried out. Let us give to
that governing body which can effectually and best
carry it out the power to do so. We on this side
of the House should not be afraid to give Parliament
wider powers. ... / for one am, not afraid to
entrust this power to the Parliament. We have a
great jury to appeal to—the people of Australia!
Immense capital was made by Anti-Labor
State Frighters of the Joe Cook stamp
out of the Referenda clause which provided
that State railway servants should be allowed
a right of appeal to the Federal Arbitration
Court. For months Cook has been insist-
ing that, to allow the employees of an Aus-
tralian State to seek what they conceive to
be justice in an Australian High Court,
would bring about the disorganisation of the
railway systems of the Commonwealth, and
create such a panic in foreign pawnshops
that Australia’s loan supply would be cut off
at the meter. In 1904 Cook and a ma-
jority of the Anti-Socialist party (now the
“Lioeral” party) favored the inclusion of
KJiate railway servants in the Federal Arbi-
tration Bill. The Government of the day
was indeed ousted with the assistance of
the party to which Cook then belonged, be-
cause it jibbed at this proposal. A brief
Labor interregnum followed, and then Cook
and his Freetrade, Anti-Socialistic, “Liberal”
colleagues jumped the Treasury benches, and
passed the identical legislation in regard to
railway servants which they have, since the
beginning of the current year, been damning
unitedly as an infamy and an assault on
State Rights.
* * * *
It was not till a Labor Government made
Federal control of monopolies part of its ap-
peal to the country that “Liberalism” dis-
covered anything noxious in the policy.
Some of the bitterest Anfi-Laborites in the
Federal House were agreed last year that
the States could not deal adequately with
injurious monopolies—if only for the fact
that a Trust may register in one State and
operate in another —and that the only means
of scotching such abuses lay in giving the
Commonwealth Parliament power to do so.
W. H. Irvine spoke on the subject on Oc-
• tober 19 last: —
The experience of the United, States, especially
during the last 15 or 20 years, goes to show that to
deal with this growing evil the arm of the States
iR too short, whilst the arm of the Central Govern-
ment is paralysed by the constitutional ligature to
which I have referred. There is no power to deal
with these Trusts iri the United States, and the point
has been madfe by Dr. Haynes in the passage to
which I have referred, that with a Constitution
which is practically incapable of being so amended
as to do away With this ligature there may be found
no other remedy than some form of revolutionary
change.
Sir John Forrest: But the States can deal with
these Trusts? ’
Mr. W. H. IRvine: The States cannot; that is the
whole point.
Sir John Forrest: Not with the operations of a
Trust within a State?
Mr. W. H. Irvine : No; the whole evil of the
position in the United States is that the individual
States are quite powerless. Nothing is better
known than is that fact to anyone who has given
any consideration to the question.
Nevertheless, W. H. Irvine has been fore-
most among those of his party who assured
the electors during the recent campaign (1)
that there were no Trusts in Australia, and
(2) that, in the unlikely event of any mater-
ialising, the States had full power to con-
trol them.
* * * *
So much for the attitude of representative
“Liberals” towards a set of proposals which
might well have been their own. Now consider
the attitude of certain State Labor members
towards the same proposals. There was no
personal reason why the average State mem-
ber, Labor or otherwise, should have en-
thused about the Fisher amendments. The
Government proposals, though they never
threatened the preposterous interferences
with the details of business and so on which
their opponents alleged, were certainly cal-
culated to diminish the powers and prestige
of State Parliaments. The Holmans and
McGowens of N. S. Wales have no hope of
a career in Federal politics. Their whole
future lies in their State. It is understand-
able, therefore, that they should want to
retain all the importance that attaches to
the State politician’s ,job. And, further-
more, there is the consideration that these
men have, for years, been hopelessly out of
touch with the democratic spirit of Austra-
lia. They are peace cranks in a nation
which has declared overwhelmingly for com-
pulsory service: State Frighters in an age
when the rallying cries of the Cabbage Tree
mob are as dead a,s their authors. In other
words, they are inefficiently disguised Tories,
the sort of folks who might be expected to
regard any new legislative departure as
fraught with peril. Not self-interest alone,
but innate Conservatism as well, set them
against the Referenda proposals. But they
did not voice their opinions to any extent.
While the country was seething with argu-
ments for and against the Government’s pro-
posals, these tribunes of the people lay iu-
gloriously low and said nothing.
The silence of these false-alarm Laborites
was due to the same cause as the volubility
of their “Liberal” allies. That is to say, Party
Discipline. In the case of the State Labor-
ites, the Party organisation—that all-power-
ful entity which is responsible to no one —
said in effect: “Whatever hostile views
you hold regarding these questions, you ate
not to air them. If you do, you leave the
Labor party.” And Holman, McGowen
and the rest, putting the lucrative billets
which membership of the party alone per-
mits them to enjoy before their Anti-Refer-
enda convictions, subsided, and were heard
no more. To Deakin, Cook, Irvine and
the rest, the “Liberal” party said: “The
enemy has put forward certain proposals.
You must therefore oppose them. In any
case they are inimical to Vested Interests.
It matters not that you have all, in the
days when you were not pledged definitely
to Anti-Laborism, advocated majority of
these proposals yourselves. Your present
duty is to constitute yourselves special
pleaders against every one of them, that be-
ing the way the party game, as originally
imported from England, always has been
and always must be played.”
* * * *
Tbe struggle is over; the various ethical
sacrifices have been accomplished. Deakin
and his colleagues retain the approval of offi-
cial “Liberalism”; McGowen and his col-
leagues remain in the Labor movement.
Faint moans of protest still issue from the
“Liberal” press, inasmuch as those Labor-
ites who should have attacked the Referenda
proposals were bulldozed into silence by the
Party machine. And from Fremantle drifts
this plaintive cry of surprise from honest
Andrew Fisher :
I think Mr. Deakin has found himself surrounded
by gentlemen who never were Liberal in the true
sense of the term, and he finds himself compelled to
lead a party against proposals which might very well
a few years ago have emanated from himself. I can-
not understand a man of his position and standing
in the Commonwealth stating a few years ago. in
writing, that his opinions were like ours, and then
going back on them.
The general public is so far inarticulate
on the subject. When it gets a chance it,
should surely have a word to say, and a vig-
orous curse to discharge, at a political sys-
tem which, while possessing few points of
merit, operates to produce more liars, turn-
coats, trimmers and spielers than any other
that has been devised by man.
THE STEADFAST MERINO.
i Merinos remain unchanged.—S. M. ITerald.
The whole world changes; Australia, at the
command Of her poets, gets up and makes
a critical examination of the Dawn ; but the
wool-bearing beast, whose bleat pervades the
banks, river-flats, agencies, and drawing-
rooms of this continent, remains as immobile
as the Sphinx, and as unalterable as the cur-
rency of alcohol. Referenda and cyclones
rage; Wowser and Bung mention each
other’s deficiencies; surf-bathing and picture-
shows alter the face of Nature, while hobble
and harem skirts re-disguise her nether
limbs. But the merino browses on, solemn
as Fate, placid as the ocean of Eternity.
* * * .
There is something in the make-up of the
beast which renders him kin to Chaos and
Old Night, and links him in an everlasting
brotherhood with the oldest and most fatigued
star. The spirit of the merino was abroad
when Noaii was: a little boy, learning to
balance himself on the hump of his father’s
least odorous camel ; it rested on the Plain
of Shinar before Nimrod started a. State
brickworks; it slumbered qn the Seven hills
before Romulus erected his liumpy by the
banks of the Tiber pit spread as a hoary fog
over Britain long years before J. C/esar in-
terrupted it with a meat cleaver. In
fact, from the dawn of history to
the public swindle of Yassberra. the
imperturbable essence of the ; merino
has made its influence felt in all human af-
fairs. As a subtle, impalpable vapor its
moribundity has pervaded the brain and con-
science of those who are marked from birth
as its own; and wherever manhood becomes
Fat, through the anointing of the great god
Pluius, its occult power descends with the
weight of Mount Kosciusko and the Ten
Commandments rolled in one.
Merinos remain unchanged. The white
man’s world of 400 years ago was an ex-
ceedingly good place not to live in. In
those grand old times knives were more in
evidence than laws, throat-cutting! was the
most popular form off sport, and the crime
of being merely John Smith was one to be
expiated by life-long servitude. One morn-
ing Progress woke with a start, and saw
that it was time to be at work. She pulled
Freedom’s hair, and bawled in her ear that
it was breakfast time; and the two ladies
started out to organise a conspiracy which
has ever since gone by the name of Democ-
racy. The conspirators altered the world.
Already they have gone nearly half-way to
their goal. Throat-cutting has been slightly
diminished, hunger has become scarcer, soap
and water have come into almost general use,
and all sorts of naw-fangled abominations
have been introduced. Wicked inventions,
such as trains, towels, steamships, politi-
cians, newspapers, doctors, trousers, police-
courts, dinner-forks, theatres, and postage
stamps, have marked the declension from
the high standard of the past; and the
spirit of the merino has been the only bul-
wark of the world against the horror of
moving change. Always a portion of man-
kind, under that subtly-ennobling influence,
has entered its splendid protest, and upheld
' the glories of the great days gone ; and, had
it not- been that the people are easily carried
away by a mere party cry, we should still
be as magnificently hungry as of old, and as
likely to have our throats cut as in the days
of Plantagenet kings or the Saxon Hep-
tarchy.
* * * *
Merinos remain unchanged. For half a
century Australia was divided into a num-
ber of small and jealous colonies, whose peo-
ple regarded one another with the most plea-
sant feelings of hatred, malice and vindic-
tive spite. Tariff walls, foreshadowing the
forts of the future, were built around the
frontiers of each petty nrovince; and a
perpetual war of pin-pricks and small an-
noyances helped to keep the rival settle-
ments mindful of their parochial interests
and prejudices. A dozen years ago a of
enthusiasts, who had long been preaching the
doctrine of a United Australia, succeeded in
forming a Federation of States, under the
title of the Commonwealth. By their action
they practically abolisned the grounds for
unpleasantness between settlement and set-
tlement, gave a fillip to every form of Aus-
tralian industry, introduced a new era of
prosperity, and destroyed the condition of
wrangle and strife which had gladdened the
earlier years. Naturally they were opposed
at every point by the spirit of the merino.
The woolly-minded person regarded the pre-
vious condition of the continent as something
directly and specifically arranged by Provi-
dence, something which it was sacrilege to
seek to improve, and which none but
impious men would attempt to seri-
ously alter. With splendid zeal they
spoke and voted against change • but
the madness of the popular Australian-
ism gained the day, and all that has re-
mained to the stalwarts of holy stagnation
has been to keep the last flicker of tbe old
parish spites alive in the breasts of the faith
ful.
Merinos remain unchanged. Australia to-
day presents the glorious spectacle of four-
teen Houses of Legislature, seven Governors
—all crowned with the cocked hat of semi-
majesty—and an almost uncountable mob
of very ordinary, but specially selected, per-
sons who call themselves Senators, or write
M.P., M.L.A., or M.L.C., after their dis-
tinguished names. In proportion to popu-
lation, this country has more machinery of
government, vice-regal glitter, pomp of of-
fice, and general' political swagger, than any
other white country on earth. And it is
justly proud of the fact. Any person rid-
ing in a pony-carriage drawn by seven horses,
one camel, six mules, six working bullocks,
and an elephant, besides a host of minor
animals, ranging from imported asses to
native bears, would be strangely constituted
indeed if he did not feel "roud of the as-
sorted menagerie in the traces. Yet we are
not allowed to enjoy our glory in peace.
Certain firebrands have arisen who declare
that six out of our seven vice-regal estab-
lishments are crimes against common sense,
and that seven out of our fourteen Cham-
bers of Legislature stand as a perpetual
proof of national insanity. The solid, un-
divided merino mind offers its steadfastness
in defence of our systematised lack of sys-
tem. It presents argument; unanswerable
A populous island in the North Sea has con-
trived to muddle on for centuries with two
Houses of Legislature and one King, and
how should Australia get along without its
fourteen houses and seven imposing king-
lets? Poor common sense has no answer to
give when the merino asks such a question
as that! > : \
* * * ,
Merinos remain unchanged. The city of
Sydney has fallen haphazard oh several
square miles of hills, and is spreading out
arms of brick and mortar in every direction.
It is infested by scores of little municipali-
ties, and countless swarms of more" or less
illiterate aldermen, who fume and fret and
try to preserve their large-waisted dignity
at the expense of pretty well everything a
modern city should possess. The result is
a picturesque, but only somewhat sanitary,
and rather less than moderately convenient,
human ant-heap. Visionaries yell for a
Greater Sydney, under one strong and
capable municipal council. They wquld rob
the suburban alderman of his sacred right
to display his profound incapacity, and at
one sweep bring the whole populous area up
t'- the level of a clean, orderly and health/
city. No well-balanced merino intellect
would ever consent to such a change as that.
London in the days of its fame, before the
Great Plague, had almost as much confusion,
lack of economy, and consequential “dignity”
as the tangled mass of uncombed streets
clustering round Port Jackson, and what bet-
ter precedent should one want? Here at
least a sheepish political intelligence' must
make a stand worthy of; its ancient reputa-
tion. Possessing the finest example! of tri-
umphant municipal muddle on this i planet,
the citadel of splendid incompetence musi
never be yielded while a single truculent al
derman remains to glorify a distended waist
coat. * « ’ *
Merinos remain unchanged. Though th°
heavens fall, and though a Jap invasion fol-
low on an Australian drought, the invincibh
and unalterable merino mind remains the
same. The No. 9 boot of the people eanno'
stir it; the cannon of common sense cannot,
breach its walls; the microbe of reason can-
not quicken in it any fever of'unrest. JSolid
as a Scotchman’s regard for porridge, ear-
nest as the bottomless foundations of Sheol,
strong as the grip of greed, and vast as the
ocean of ignorance, it opposes all change, all
reform, all hope, all movement of any kind
whatever. Merinos are unchanged and un-
changeable.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Party That Does Things.
One of the objects of Australian Federation
was effective defence. Years passed, and
nothing whatever was done. Then the
Labor party came into power, and
universal service and an Australian navy
materialised. The bursting up of big
estates had been talked about for as long
as the Commonwealth couild remember; but
no Government attempted to burst anything.
The Labor party had not been six months
in office before a graduated land-tax arrived
to, do it. Not to harass an obvious proposi-
tion, if there is merit in a political party
briskly and capably doing those things which
ife. has promised to do, the Federal Labor
party good. The N.S..W. State
Labor Government is not above reproach in
a multitude of ways, but it has given no
sign of being less sincere in regard to its
pledges than the Federal Laborites. Last
Thursday night Acting-Premier Holman laid
his Government’s intentions befbre the pub-
lic. They comprise pretty nearly all the
measures that Anti-Labor has enthused about
for the past ten years, and at the same time
has doggedly refrained from touching. The
See. Carrutiiers and Wade Governments
all blithered about the crying necessity of a
harbor bridge or tunnel. To North Shore
deputationists they handed soothing words,
and then either appointed another Commis
sion, or dropped the subject without even


that time-worn sop to public clamor. The
licking of the State’s absurdly invoiycd an
numerous Land Acts and Amendi S
to intelligible shape has been mentioned by
successive Governments since the b g
of things. But none of them attempted to
do it. The principle of a Court of Cum
inal Appeal—an institution which has been
giving the utmost satisfaction in
tive England for the past three years—has
been applauded, in an academic sc
fashion, by the legal members of half a dozen
“Liberal” Ministries; but not one of them
dared to give it a chance. The need fo
radical change in the Industrial C°u t »
long been apparent. Judge Heydon Pjophe
sied last year that the Wages Boaid system
must break down. “Liberalism had no sug-
gestion to offer. This paper remarked at
the time: —
What would probably meet the case V T lld ™fnr tn
institution of one or more tribunals with the power to
group together co-related industries, and conside
them in the mass. Thus it would be feasible for one
Court to handle all the industries connected—say
with the business of coal-mining, just as, under t
old Act, one Court successfully handled all the in-
dustries connected with silver-mining.
This is the course which the Government
proposes to take. As Holman put it:
Instead of a vast number of Wages Boards we
shall arrange allied trades in groups, and appoint
smaller Courts or Boards for each.
It will be bad news for the army of briefless
barristers who, under the Wade system,
drew fat fees as chairmen of the countless
Wages Boards that came into being; but it
will be better and cheaper for the State.
These are some of the reforms that the
N.S.W. Labor party pledges itself to get
busy with at once. The only criticism that
can be aimed at them is that they may not
be accomplished. And if they emanated
from a “Liberal” Government, that plaint
would carry a deal of weight. But Labor pos-
sesses the splendid advantage of haying no
Vested Interest howling protests in its ear ;
and, on past form, it should do the things it
has been commissioned by the country to
do, and do them soon.
Why Britain Consists Largely of Poorhouses
The big-syndicate papers printed the other
day this cable :
The British Board of Trade has issued a report
on wages, rents, and prices in the United States.
The statements show that an American wonanan,
although employed for shorter hours, earns two-and-
a-quarter times as much as a workman in England.
On the other hand, rent is twofold and food one-
third higher. But the cost of living altogether is
as 152 to 100.
Having printed tlie report, the Argus and
the Herald and the Telegraph and the rest
of them conspired to conveniently forget all
about it. A thousand times they and their
friend Obesity had told the working man
that he was no better off for his high wages,
because all the extra money he earned went
in paying extra for the old bread, sugar and
sauce. In particular, he was assured that
Protection was a silly thing; his adviser al-
most wept over the yarn of his own inven-
tion that the protected German workman
has dead horse for dinner, and black bread
for supper. As for the American workman,
everybody knew that the poor, harassed,
over-wrought wretch had to plug away a ter-
rific ' number of hours in order to earn
enough to pay the equally terrific prices
that ruled in America. Now, on top of all
these beautiful stories—all tending to show
how foolish a man is to ask for more wages,
and how a “rise” in pay really, amounts to
a punishment, so that a sufficient number
of “rises” would mean starvation —-comes this
depressing cable. The protected American
workman, it says, works shorter hours than
his British brother, and yet earns £225 where
the Freetrade Briton earns only £100;
nevertheless, the American’s living costs him
only £152, where it costs the British Free-
trader £IOO. So where the British Free-
trade workman can simply make both ends
meet, the protected American can, with
shorter hours, live as well, and put by £/3.
This isn’t the screech of some Yankee immi-
gration agent, either; it is the report of the
British Board of Trade—the sort of docu-
ment on which British publicists are ex-
pected to base their conclusions. No won-
der, then, the Argus, and the Sydney Tele-
graph,' and the rest of the Freetrade and
low-wage papers decided to simply ignore
the document. But that won’t, suppress it,
any more than the profound disrespect of a
caterpillar will, suppress Kosciusko.
Bush Nursing.
Lady Dudley’s bush-nursing scheme has
made a new start; so it is worth casting the
eye of criticism afresh on a proposal which
was worthy and reasonable enough in itself,
and which would have been in full flower
long since, had our ancient nobility done halt
the financial things that were expected. At
Beech Forest, as elsewhere, the nurse will
attend to residents, and generally help the
local doctors. The point which needs to be
emphasised at an early stage —since one State
Government is subsidising the scheme, and
others are likely to follow suit —is that,
though the nurse’s presence may be cheering
enough to those whom she encounters, from
a State point of view she represents much
wasted energy. There is a need in Australia
for a bush-nursing scheme. In fact, the
various States would save much money and
many lives if they supported a number of
bush nurses without attempting the hopeless
job of cadging their salaries and board-money
from the well-to-do. But it needs to be run
on business as apart from sentimental lines.
The bush person who (1) dies, or (2) un-
necessarily clogs the already congested
country hospitals, or (3) does both, for lack
of a bush-nursing system, is not the bush re-
sident so much as the swaggie, the fencer, the
boundary-rider, the share farmer, the rabbit-
trapper, and the rest of the great • army of
“campers.” When the “camper” gets ill he
either attends to himself or else visits the
nearest public hospital. The doctor in charge
is immediately in a quandary. He knows that
if he treats the man or prescribes for lnm
and sends him away, the patient will soon
be pottering round a muddy or dusty
camp, cooking, boiling billies, and other-
wise doing all that is necessary to
make his ill probably worse. So m
even trivial cases the country doctor orders
the afflicted into the hospital. Thus the pub-
lic is put to a vast amount of unnecessary
expense (Oogtilan estimated that each hos-
pital bed in N.S.W. cost £37 10s. per an-
num) ; and, worst of all, the hospitals are
crowded to the exclusion of really serious
cases. An intelligently organised bush nuis_
ing scheme would go a long way towards re
medying this. In those centres wheie be mi
gratory population tends to congregat, _
Bathurst, Dubbo and Armidale /N-fe.W.) ,
in Echuca, Nhill and Mildura (Vic.) ac
commodation houses of the iuexr y
'the N.S.VV. Government has built at xarran
gobilly Caves and other tourist resorts, might
be run up. They should be supplied with
simple remedies, and be under the control of
qualified nurses Taking as o w cost
LAN’s asylum figures for N- s - w ->
per bed per annum would be a shade under
f°l The difference between that and the
cost of a hospital bed would represent a sav-
ing to the community of £ls _los. And the
overloading, which is a chronic troub
country hospitals, would be mitigated 1
weren’t ended. A bush-nursing scheme, framed
Jo cope with this trouble, would not only
save the country a lot of money and benefit,
the person who has either no home at all o
only a makeshift one, but would incidentally
be a blessing to every bush resident who is a
potential hospital inmate—-which is to say,
to about 60 per cent, of the inhabitants of
Australia.
A Disease Which is Shot on Sight; and
Another Which is Asked m to Have a Drink.
Last week the Mooltan arrived in Westralia
with small-pox on board. The moment the
disease was diagnosed the solitary sullerer
and his cabin companion were isolated. Un
reaching Adelaide the steamer, its cargo and
its 266 passengers were quarantined. ±he
S. Australian passengers were ordered to
Torrens Island, and there vaccinated and
disinfected. At Melbourne the rest of the
passengers —barring live ‘conscientious o -
lectors” —were vaccinated and disinfected.
The cargo was fumigated. The doctor who
did the vaccinating, the nurses who helped
him, the constables who were employed to
ward visitors oil’ the ship were all torced into
quarantine. A quantity of coal had been
ordered at the Semaphore. I he lumpers,
though offered special pay, declined to work
beneath the yellow fiag. Only those passen-
gers who proved that they had not associated
with the original cause of the trouble, and
that they had been successfully pre-vaccin-
ated, were allowed to leave the ship. Even
then they were put under surveillance, and
were ordered to appear fpr medical inspec-
tion at stated intervals. A Mooltan pas-
senger who had been duly vaccinated and
disinfected arrived in Sydney ahead ot ttie
ship. The local Health authorities were
panic-stricken. Dr. Ashburton Tiiomp-
son “knew no peace of mind until this man
had been found and quarantined.” I he daily
papers wrote leading articles, pointing out
heatedly that ‘‘the incident indicated a very
serious defect in Commonwealth Quarantine
administration.” In fact, small-pox was not
given a sporting chance. It was, metaphori-
cally speaking, poisoned, and shot sl tt in S’
and then dvuamited. Set aboard the Mool-
tan, in addition to the small-pox patient,
were certainly half a dozen, perhaps a dozen,
possibly twice that number, who were suf-
fering from a dreadful disease to which it
was no one’s duty to attend. This disease,
according to Cogiilan, is the direct cause of
death in one out of every 330 females, and
one out of every 229 males in Australia to-
day. Locomotor ataxia, general paralysis
of the insane, brain-softening, practically all
nervous troubles, and countless ghastly eye,
throat and skin afflictions arise from it.
It is as loathsome as small-pox, and is liable
to be a thousand times more disfiguring
later on. Whereas small-pox runs its course
and is done with, this complaint stays with
its victim to the grave. It is so communi-
cable that any infant may catch it from any
nurse; any bather from any towel; any
drinker from any cup or glass; any man or
woman from any friend _ or relation. A
drug exists which, even if it fails to cure
individual sufferers (and there is good reason
to think it does cure them), certainly pre-
vents the creation, by infection, ot fresh
ones. And not a single Government in the
Commonwealth is moving in the matter! A
disease which has been the bane of the civil-
ised world for four centuries, and whose
grim evidences are everywhere 10-day, is
blandly treated as though it did not exist.
An imbecile British convention forbids the
mention of this complaint on the stage, in
society, or ip the columns of sheets which
circulate in the home. Politicians dread to
legislate against it, or .even to speak ot it;
and the papers suppress 5 their remarks when
they do speak. Last month the London
Family Doctor said with justifiable pride:
One after the other diseases are disappearing.
Small-pox, typhus, hospital gangrene, diphtheria,
malaria, Malta. fever, consumption, are all going.
All “germ” diseases are going. Length ot lile is
extending. As many people died at 40 in the reign
of Queen Elizabeth as now live to 70.
But there is no diminution of syphilitic
disease. The doctors, the politicians, and
the Press fully appreciate its ravages, but
they lack the nerve to bring the light ot pub-
iic knowledge and opinion to bear on it; and
disease, like other evil things, flourishes in
darkness. It is a depressing reflection on
our intelligence as a people that if success-
ful campaign is ever to be instituted against
this Horror, the Horror will certainly have
to be called housemaid’s knee, or some such
innocuous-sounding name, before the Genteel
will take any useful part in scotching it

The Lash.
“The Ancient,” on noticing that in Wes-
tralia the other day, under a newly-enacted
law, a man convicted of a sexual offence
was ordered a flogging, in addition to life
imprisonment: .
Flogging is a class punishment. The lash is for
the man in workman’s garb, and for him alone—
who ever saw a silk hat and a broadcloth coat
doffed at the triangles ? ‘ Society simply couldn t
flog a “gentleman,” no matter what ne had done;
if "it did it would raise livid weals on the soul of
his silver-haired old mother, anil make raspberry-
marks on the immortal parts of his sainted sisters.
The hitherto-ruling classes have, however, little
compunction in vicariously flogging Mrs. Smith, the
vulgar washerwoman, through the back of her
blackguard son. ,’. , ,
And not onlv is the lash a class torture, but
there is a greater disproportion between its relative
effects on' different individuals than obtains in any
other form of legal punishment. (Here let it be
noted that the flogger is set by law to avenge crimes
of passion as well as of cold, calculating brutality.)
The late N.S.W. Chiei Justice Dakley, after order-
ing a man to be- imprisoned and flogged, relented
on hearing an appeal by the prisoner’s counsel, and
consented to remit the flogging. When the man
came up to me a few days after his trial,’ said
the Chief Justice to a legal friend, “I quite failed
to recognise him —he seemed to have shrunk to half
his size through fear.” After which Justice Darley
flogged no more. That illustrates one type of flogee.
He whose lashings were remitted by Chief Justice
Darley was similar in temperament to a man, a
recalcitrant prisoner, whom 1 long ago saw flogged
at Pentridge Stockade. He was brought out look-
ing like a mad ghost; at the first lash he stiffened
himself up and howled; at the second he emitted a
wild shriek and strained at his bonus-; later on, he
gave the awful sort of groan that you hear from the
injured horse which lies dving in agony beside the
steeplechase fence. Then his head fell to one side
and he became limp and silent, whereupon the acting
gaol-surgeon, a young friend of mine who has since
climbed high, stopped the orgy of insensate savagery
which insulted his instincts as an educated medical
man and a civilised human being. The other type
of flogee whom I met was a man of 36 years, one
j| — J who had been awarded the awful penalty of
50 lashes. The flogger was a sturdy ex-convict,
with whom R had quarrelled in gaol.- R——,
an ex-soldier, a splendidly set-up man of sft. 10m.,
and perhaps 12st., on being triced up to the triangles,
glared at the flogger and quietly remarked that he
was “a mean hound” to take such a billet, but
that, after all, it was all that could be expected of
“such a sneak.” The third or fourth lash brought
blood- —the, flogger was evidently taking revenge—
but, incredible as it may seem, R never winced
throughout, and, toward the end, when his back
looked like a section of fresh sea-mullet, he turned
round and contemptuously smiled at his tormentor.
“Forty-seven, forty-eight,” said the adamantine pri-
soner, counting the strokes aloud, “lay it on, you
miserable son of a ! Forty-nine! Now put it
all in, you crawler! Fifty! Domino!” On being
unstrapped he calmly wrapped round his body a
towel which was tendered him, and coolly remarked, as
he walked away with a warder, that he was a man
anyhow, and not “a thing” like the fellow who had
done him a mean trick in gaol, and then gloried in
trying to take it out of his hide because lie' had
pounded his head before the governor. Those two
cases illustrate how tremendously disproportionate
nominally the same lash-punishment may be in two
different cases. , , ~
The Australian Supreme Court Judge who would
revel in ma.n-fl3.ying is almost extinct, and the Execu-
tive which would back him up—thanks to Labor
in politics—has become rarer still. It is true that
the minor Bench of one province still endures the
presence of a Judge who some years ago dared to
publicly read before a great religious assembly a
panegyric on torture, and of another (also noted
as a religionist) who used to ladle out lashings
wholesale until he became utterly disgruntled by
Executive interference; but the pair now only figure ,
as causticised warts on our legal system. All the
iame, the power should be taken away, even it there
are only two who exercise it. Some States have
indeed existed for quite a long while without the
old lag-time fetich, the Lash, and the others will be
pushed’ into line when the class nature of the punish-
ment is fully understood.

The Tragedy of Being Honest.
After allowing a decent interval to elapse
Bkucl Smith wrote to the press concerning
the launching of the Warrego. He mention-
ed his unwillingness to “perform the func-
tions of a wet blanket,” but was forced to
own that the proceedings were “distinctly
insular, parochial, and on the whole un-
worthy. . . . whilst the ludicrous attempt
to drag in a sort of apostrophe to civil free-
dom was a hollow mockery to a glorious
sentiment, and a transparent insult to the
memories of that audience. And he yet
again registered Ins unshakeable belief i
Dreadnausea, and announced that he cou
"go further, and produce correspondence
from Lord Tweedmoutii, confirmed by
later correspondence from Sir Harry Haw-
son,” to prove that hired defence was the
only worthy naval policy for a self-respect-
ing Commonwealth. This dogged adherence
to a cause which is utterly lost is character-
istic of Bruce Smith, and provides a useful
lesson or Horrible Example for aspiring
voung “Liberals.” The member for I arkes
is probably the only real Anti-Socialist in
the so-called Anti-Socialist party. He would
hand the railways over tal.riva.te Enter
prise to-morrow if he .could,; and do away
with the State tramway system, even though
it meant reverting to the old arrangement
whereby a private omnibus Co. charged Is. a
head to carry people from King-street (Syd-
ney) to Darling Point. He stul regaids
Early Closing and Pure Food legislation ,as
interferences with the law of supply and de-
mand and assaults upon the lmerty of ■ the
subiect He is a Freetrader —net one of the
bogus Cooic-Wilks-Fuller brand, but the
real article. If he were given the chance
he would afflict the Commonwealth with
complete Freetrade to-morrow, and be found
subsequently arguing among the rums that
it was not the policy, but the dreadful wea-
ther, or the prevalence of goats la Turkestan,
or something, which had done the damage.
He would swamp Australia with cheap nig-
gers from India, S’Africa, and Ceylon, and
would denounce those Australians who de-
clined to treat their fellow subjects of color
as brothers and sisters. In fact, Bruce
Smith is a genuine Anti-Socialist, a genuine
Freetrader, and a genuine Imperialist.
Dozens of his colleagues in both federal
Houses claim all these titles, but they a^ e
frauds. They do not dare, theft; days, to
deposit so much as one oratorical blossom on
the grave of Freetrade. They are more So-
cialistic where it is a question of seed wheat,
show subsidies, or anything else which
touches their own or their supporters inter-
ests, than the “Socialists” themselves; and
they would not have a fragment of Austra-
lia’s existing Socialism wiped out tor worlds.
Their love for the Empire’s nigger is a purely
academic passion. They would rather sleep
in the yard than share a room with him, and
they would rather see their fernum offspring
dead than married to him. And what is
Bruce Smith’s reward for being the only
consistent member of his party ? This the
party has enthusiastically agreed to make
him its Ishmael ! Here is a man of ad-
mitted probity, a King’s Counsel, the holder
of the safest seat, in Australia, and an ex-
perienced Parliamentary hand. iet tor
vears past he has not been oifered office,
and it is unlikely that he ever will be of-
fered it. If the narty organisation could
have ousted him from Parliament at the
time of the Financial Agreement Referen-
dum, it would have been delighted to do so.
Ignoramuses of the Joe Cook brand are
placed in authority over him, and mediocri-
ties like Quick are preferred to him. He
is looked on as “impossible”—because he
holds the views about defence, fiseplism, so-
cial legislation.' etc., which “Liberalism is
trying to forget it held the other day. The
moral which the case of B. Smith provides
for the ambitious “Liberal” is that it is ad-
visable for the ambitious Liberal to be
brisk in “reneging” on hi% convictions the
moment the party bosses give the office.
There is nothing which more exasperates
“Liberalism” than a “Liberal” oolitician who
is the same sort of “Liberal’ on Tuesday
that he was the previous Saturday.
The Sydney Evening Bloodstain last week
pictured N.S.W. as a stupid but respectable
old charwoman in the Federal pawnshop,
with a wooden railway locomotive under her
arm. Attorney-General Hughes was smirk-
ing behind the counter and looking hungrily
at the engine, while “Friend” Wade was
hanging round the door like an unemployed
hero-actor, and darkly advising the addled
old ladv to look at the pictures of the ruined
Post Office and Defence Department on the
wall before she pawned her mechanical toy.
The cartoon represented the case for State
Frights in all its rolled-gold glory. But, on
the next page, the bald-headed commercial
page, which doesn’t care a turnip about cur-
rent politics, after mentioning tersely that
weevil had made its appearance in the un- ;
sheltered wheat stacked down by Southern
Riverina railway stations, and that some. of
the grain would be unfit for local milling
or shipping oversea, went on to say:—
Some of the N.S.W. shippers and millers have been
taught a severe lesson this summer and autumn in
regard to buying wheat in the country districts. It
is probable "that next season they will only buy
wheat for delivery on trucks (country railway
stations), in order to throw the responsibility of the
stacking, and of finding trucks, on to the growers.
Hitherto it has been customary for the responsibility
of the wheat-growers to cease as soon as the approved
wheat sold is delivered at country railway stations,
and thp purchaser has had to bear the loss of subse-
quent damage to wheat (in the bags) through lack
of sheds, tarpaulins, or other shelter, when rain falls.
Such damage has this season been very heavy.
It is a pathetic instance of bow hard it is
to make the writers who deal in facts and
prices agree with the artists who deal in fic-
tion and pictures.

“Aural” : I want to throw a clod at the
Defence Department. Barmedman (N.S.W.)
has formed a rifle club of 40 or more active
members. The first letter to the Depart-
ment was written two and a-half years ago.
and the Department took 12 months to send
an unsatisfactory reply. The determined
little township pegged away, however, and
iu another 12 months got the funds to build
a range. Another few months of masterly
delay, and then the Department sent 2S
rifles for 40 of Australia’s most enthusiastic-
defenders. Twenty of these weapons are
single-shot crossbreds —something between a
Gatling gun and a torpedo tube. More-
over, 100 rounds per annum constitutes each
member’s full allotment of ammunition.
Fortunately, these fellows say : “Well, even
if they think it’s enough we know it isn t,
so they buy another 1000 or so with the egg-
money. And they do more than that. Some
of the members are young farmers who come
as much as 15 miles to the range. Others,
who live nearer, leave immediately after their
shoot, and take charge of the working teams
to allow some other chaps the chance of an
hour’s practice. It’s men of this kind who
are cold-shouldered by the Department. An-
other thing, though this has nothing to do
with the Defence people. These farmers
defence clubs often practise on Sundays
where the Wowser voice is not loud in the
land ; it is often the young farmer’s only heme
of becoming an effective defender of his
country. But at Coolamon recently some of
them were fined by a misguided magistrate
for “shooting for sport',” as he called it.
Evidently this lawgiver never heard of the
little brown man, or the treaty that is to
run out in 1916.
Professor Anderson Stuart is an illus-
tration of how a man may receive the high-
est education, and still not know where bad
whisky may be bought. Speaking at the
National No-License Conference, held at
Sydney last week, he said:—
Very often people put down their misfortunes to
bad whisky. They could take his word, as one who
had been President of the Board of Health, that it
was all fudge. Seldom, or very rarely, was any
deleterious adulteration found in whisky. It was
adulterated with water, and it would be better, it
more water were put in it.
It isn’t so long ago that Ridley’s Wine and
Spirit Trade Circular (London) raised,
an outcry against the Commonwealth Spirits
Act, which requires, before a Government
certificate of merit is issued, that whisky
must have been distilled wholly from barley-
malt by a pot still, or similar process, at a
strength not exceeding 85 over proof; must
have been matured while subject to the con-
trol of the Customs by storage in wood tor a
period of not less than two years ; and must,
have been certified by an officer to. be pure
malt whisky. Practically ; none ot the un*
ported whiskies carried a Government guar-
antee, and reliable evidence as to their
quality or age is not obtainable. Ridley s
advanced the argument that the Comuion-
wealth certificate would be fraudulently used
by “unscrupulous traders”; and the. fact
that such traders exist is backed by the re-
cent statement of a “trade” paper in Mel-
bourne, that “thousands of e-allons ot so-
ealled whiskv are shipped to Australia every
year —beverages that have not been within
hundreds of miles of Scotland or Ireland,
and have neither age, quality nor any other
good point to recommend them. It Pro-
fessor Anderson Stuart ever gets stung by
some of the cheap, raw spirit that passes for
whisky at race meetings, sports grounds, and
numerous taverns in and out of Sydney, he
will know what he was mistaken for when he
reads the sign over the door, “Refreshments
for man and beast!”
The Australian Stinking Fish party,
fortunately, doesn’t get all its own way-
abroad : the good works which the mud
slingers vilify are regarded on their merits
occasionally. Thus, an overwhelming ma
jority of the United States’. Immigration
Commission, which has been sitting for foui
years, and has lately presented its report,
enthusiastically supports some such system
as the Australian “literacy test. At one
blow,” commented London Times, this
would exclude about a third of the people
from Southern and Eastern Europe, who at
present land in the U.S.A. That, how
ever, is done deliberately, the idea being to
limit immigration to those most likely (1) to
prove useful to America, (2) to help and
not hinder the economic development ot the
country, (3) to become good citizens and
(4) not to lower the standard of living
among the wage earners. The undesirable
alien receives special from the
Commission. - It proposes that Chinese im-
migration shall remain forbidden: that the
law as to Japanese immigration shall stay
as it is, provided existing regulations work
satisfactorily 7: and that an understanding be
arrived at with Britain to keep out Hindoos.

Stinking Fish triumphantly pointed as proof

A stop should be put to the common and
dangerous practice of employing voting forms
to advertise party aims and ideas. Through-
out the last weeks of the Referenda cam-
paign electors have been confronted at
every turn with staring posters, made up
alter the manner of voting slips, headed
F t mJ ote ’” with a heay y cross opposite
uf- , newspapers have persistently
published the forms with the same manipula-
tion. This is not argument; it is simply a
flagrant misuse of the voting forms, intended
to deceive the elector under a cunning pre-
tence of enlightening him. The “Yes” party
was drawn to adopt the same device in seif
defence, but the practice should be declared
corrupt and illegal, no matter who has
hitherto been guilty of it.
Another of Victoria’s Wages Boards has
bumped against a dead end. The Carters
and Drivers’ Board arrived at a determina-
tion which, besides covering journeymen’s
wages and. hours, dealt with apprentices and
other details. The_ Chief Inspector declared
these latter regulations ultra vire&s and de-
sired the Board to obliterate them. The
Board refused ; whereupon the Chief Secre-
tary communed with the Crown Solicitor,
who also said the Board was exceeding its
powers. On this advice the Government
sent the memo, back to the Board once more.
But that body stood firm, and remarked that
it was regulating the conditions of the in-
dustry, and it wasn’t going to budge. And
as there is no appeal to the Full Court, and
as Victoria hasn’t even got an Arbitration
Court to settle the dispute, the whole busi-
ness is hung un indefinitely.
“Pat O’Maori” : An Aboliar who has strayed
into the wilderness of one of the weeklies
supplies further data to assist my investiga-
tions regarding the habits of snakes and
loans. Meeting a dangerous reptile on an
open plain, this Aboliar threw a boot at it,
and the snake, seeing no other cover,
promptly crawled into the No. 12, and filled
it with serpentine laughter. He then threw
his other boot, and succeeded in dislodging
the mirthful enemy. ■ The story ends there;
and I feel somehow that it is not convincing.
A really humorous snake would have crawled
into the second boot, and so doubled the
effective horsepower of the joke. Curiously
enough lou Ward acts exactly as the
Aboliar in question did. His notion is that
by throwing a second boot of Maoriland
credit he can dislodge the snake that has
taken possession of the first; but, as every
person who has sampled Cohen’s liquor is
aware, that snaae will call up his whole
family to assist him, and occupy just as
many boots as may happen to be thrown.
If the boot-throwing policy continues,
louseph Ward will soon be dancing round
in his stocking soles, wishing that he had
as many extremities as a centipede, or that
he could call his Dreadnought from China
to open fire on the unsportsmanlike reptiles
who have commandeered the national elastic-
sides.
A case heard in last week’s District Court
clamored for a Greater Sydney. A bridge was
built many years ago between two of the 40-odd
pocket-handkerchief boroughs of the metropolis.
It began to crumble through age, and, while the
two boroughs wrangled, its senile decay accumu-
lated. Finally, one of the councils decided to
condemn the bridge, pull it down, and replace it
by a foot-bridge. This reform was duly carried
out; but the penny-wise council built the new
bridge with the timber out of the old one. Recently
a little toddler of about three years pulled part of
the concern over, and a post fell on him ana broke
his leg. Hence Law, £3O damages and Costs.

“Our Miss Gibbs’’ shows no disposition to
throw up her place. She even went through
the Easter holidays without finding out that
her mother had been taken seriously ill, and
that filial love demanded an instant depart-
ure to the bedside. The old address, the
Sydney Royal, still finds the constant maid.
*• * * *
There is nothing to be said about “Jack
and the Beanstalk,” at her Majesty’s, Syd-
ney, save that the former is well, and the
latter still growing, while the multitude is
crowding to watch the proceedings with un-
abated enthusiasm.
** * *
If the first act were as good as the other
three, the Sydney jury would by this time
have returned with a verdict that “The Wo-
man in the Case” deserves at least an honor-
able mention if not a blue ribbon. True,
there is the very doubtful bad woman to be
considered, and she doesn’t intrude in the
first act. Clyde Fitch evidently had trouble
with that lady. He had to invent one who
would captivate three men of different types.
Not one of the three was a high type ; yet a
later_ inspection deepens The Bulletin’s
first impression that the woman in the case,
as she is played at the Criterion, would
not snare more than two, and probably only
one, out of the three, inferior types as they
are.
** * *
At the Adelphi (Sydney) “The Bad Girl
of the Family” continues her career of bad-
ness amidst the loud applause and manifest
appreciation of considerable numbers of
people. A philosopher once said, “He who
grieves at sin is a saint,” and if that is
correct, saints must have unanimously black-
balled the Adelphi. Anyhow, there is no
grief on the premises.

Hughie, the amateur crook (Leslie Holland),
