At the Tivoli, R. A. Roberts, who is his
own co., and draws all the salaries, is just
dow seven people in the piratical sketch,
‘Cruel Coppinger.” It deals with the days
when the pirate-smuggler wore a red or
cocked hat instead of a bell-topper, and took
risks in a draughty cave on tbe sea-front, in-
stead of sitting in an electrically-heated
plate-glass office, and sebding underpaid
clerks out to take them at the Custom House.
After inspecting Coppinger, one yearns to
transform the frock-coated brigands and their
bundles of invoices into picturesque outlaws
with belts stuffed with pistols. Peggy Pryde,
Conjurer Leipzig, Tom Dawson and a couple
of new and smart girl acrobats complete the
first line of the entertainment.
** * *
At Ascot (Sydney) Racecourse, last
Saturday afternoon, Hammond, on his Bris-
tol bi-plane, twice climbed about 100 feet
into thin air, and flew round the track and
over the heads of the cheering crowd who
had paid to see the performance. If he had
been a less polite aviator, he might have
taken up a crate of inferior eggs on his
second flight, and dropped them on the
people who stood on the seats of their motors
and looked over the fence like poverty-
stricken milk-ohs. The strong-looking Bris-
tol sped evenly through the air and dipped
gracefully back to earth, the wheels taking
the ground without any perceptible jolt.
During the first flight, a couple of wander-
ing sea-birds from Botany Bay, close by,
winged after it as if they recognised a
friend. Demonstrations are being given
every calm afternoon this week.
* » * se
Slapoffski and the Sydney Amateur Or-
chestral Society had the first concert of
their season, and, simultaneously, their
first success, one evening of last week. And
Alfred Hill shared in the triumph; for he
took Slap.’s place on the stand to lead the
Orchestra through his own bewitching
“Maori Lament” —a weirdly picturesque
performance, which did not suffer at all by
comparison with Vincent d’lndy’s “La Foret
Enchant6e,” a comparatively new symphonic
legend, heard for the first time in England
at the end of 1909. There are, neverthe-
less, some very delightful passages for the
harps on this imported number. Dvorak’s
“New World” symphony was, as usual, too
heavy for the consumption of even the mod-
erately musical. The wonderful overture
to “Die Meistersinger” was a more welcome
contribution—though Slapoffski’s stick had
a devil in it sometimes that looked as
though it wanted to bite the heels of some
sluggards.
** * *
“The Fatal Wedding,” which Spencer
einematographed recently, and is now show-
ing at Sydney ’ iyceum, lends itself to pic-
ture reproduction better than it does to act-
ing. The thing is spectacular, and some
of the situations have thrills in them, while
the drivel of the “book” is unheard. The
result is that the picture-goer gets his
money’s worth, while the old theatre-goer
got too much. The play was staged and
filmed at Bondi, Sydney, and the operator
was not the Higgins, but younger brother
Arthur. Cinematography seems to run in
that family, and the choice of light does
the stripling credit.
** * *
The Circus Editor of The Bulletin has
come to the conclusion, after a long thought
with a dash of lemonade in it, that it is
time Wirtlis’ big show in Prince Alfred
Park, Sydney, moved on. During the past
few weeks the public has got the habit of
going round after tea every night to watch
the performing horses and other wild ani-
mals, instead of stopping home to read the
leaders in the morning papers, or attending
the meetings of disinterested and heroic
statesmen like Gregory Wade. And this
is not as it should be. This paper has no
quarrel with Wirths’ Circus, or even with
Wirths’ whisky. Both are excellent, as cir-
cuses and whiskies go; but the trouble is
that too much of them is dangerous. For
instance, if the crowds that have been study-
ing Lizzie, the hippo., had instead studied
the urgent needs of their hobbled country,
would there ever have been any doubt of the
Referenda being carried? Therefore, this
journal calls upon the Wirths to furl the
menagerie and go. In any case, it is be-
coming more difficult to write something new
about the show; and if the blood-sweating
hippopotamus is there next week, it will have
to write its own notice.
** * *
“The Merry Widow” reintroduced its kiss
and cuddle at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) last Sat-
urday night. As of yore, Miss Young appears
in the part of the widow Sonia, with Reg
Roberts as de Jolidon,and Percy tucked nicely
into the character of Baron Popoff. Roberts’s
singing part is very heavy ; and a “throat”
hasn’t made it any easier for him. The
music, however, _ suits him admirably. As
Natalie, Miss Olive Godwin wears half Hat-
ton Garden on her fingers, and sings to the
utter gratification of the big audiences. Vic-
tor Prince is entombed in the part of Mar-
quis de Gascada; Arthur Lissant is General
Novikovich; and Charles Albert is a sober
and punctilious Mr. Nisch. As a nearly
lugubrious Prince Danilo, Frank Greene
gives a lengthy exposition of the mezzo sob.
On occasion his rendering seems a little tear-
ful, though in moments of passion he is
capable of strange, staccato thunder. Chorus
and scenery are as good as usual, and the
orchestra preserves its impeccable behavior.
* * * *-
Although far from being the best example
of its kind, “Paid in Full,” the American
domestic drama, which still runs at Mel-
bourne Princess’s, demonstrates that, given
pretty good material, six people can amuse
an audience as well as 60 or 600. The cast
of “Paid in Full” contains six names, yet
the performance is really a better one than
that nf the army employed in “Jack and the
Beanstalk.” This should be an influential
consideration in persuading managers to put
some dash into the good work of educating
the taste of the playgoer. The early advts.
mentioned that “Arms and the Man”—an-
other very small-cast play—would follow
Compiete Discharge of the Debt- but
"The Dawn of a To-morrow” is to appear
instead. The change happens on Saturday
evening.
** * *
Since its first performance at the Mel-
bourne Royal, “The Belle of Brittany” has
improved in a few particulars. The cook
is a brighter Stone than he was, though there
are times when he is still as matter-of-fact
as a granite pediment. Miss Lait has also
grown a trifle more charming, and Miss Gil-
liam is steadily reducing her attitudes to
the status of pretty mannerisms. On the
other hand, nobody will ever mistake Harold
Thorley for the weather; he is unchangeable.
As the romantic Raymond, in this play, he is
a living expostulation against the virtue of
energy.

At. the King’s they are still preaching the
doctrine that Right is Might, with a ver-
bosity suggesting that Right is Skite. These,
however, a.re the last nights of the cowboy
play, for it will be withdrawn early next
week, and probably be exported with the com-
pany to Maoriland. On Saturday, May 6,
Anderson s No. 1 Co. is expected to reappear
in a version, it is whispered, of “The Sor-
rows of Satan.” In the meantime the
Arion Society is preparing to nil in a few
evenings with an amateur revival of “La
Mascotte.”
** * *
. Miss Florence Cromer Challis, with the
nice, bare feet and the incidental songs, has
stolen into the bill at the Opera House, Mel-
bourne. For the rest the Perezoff's are still
coursing through their animated supper with
a careless disregard for the crockery. The
Zanfrellas continue their skilful turn ; Eunice
and Zena are filling the house with bugle
noises; but the vocally pleasing Columbia
Trio have passed on. These Columbia peo-
ple # are also humorists: but those who prefer
their comedy to their singing appear to this
commentator to be possessed of a sort of in-
tellectual squint.
“A Wreath of Orange Blossoms,” shown
at the Melbourne address of West last week,
concerns the usual snake which pushes into
a domestic Eden with heaps of cash and a
poisonous tongue. Soon he attracts the lovely
wife and leads her to the verge of an indis-
cretion. But suddenly, when he calls to
claim the lady, he finds her in the grip of a
Nonconformist conscience. The audience
cheers as she struggles to resist his diaboli-
cal fascination. And it roars with delight
when she declines to change from a husband’s
affinity to the spoilt annex of a Tertium
Quid.
#* # *
The Prahran Lyric (Vic.) has been screen-
ing “Tarquin the Proud,” a fragment of an-
cient Roman history. The film is a hand-
somely-tinted production, and the tale is
mimed by a Dago group accomplished in the
gestures of love and hate. Present programmes
at the Lyric, by the way, contain nothing so
popular as Red Indian dramas. These
things are crude in story and construction,
and always picture the deeds of braves look-
ing overclad in a girdle and a lick of paint.
Yet the picture-public loves these yarns with
a red-meat lust for the savage and primeval.
** * *
The whimsicalities of “Alice in Wonder-
land” will be related by Miss Maud Grantham
at the Melbourne Masonic to-morrow even-
ing. The clever lady, of whom this paper
has pleasant recollections, is elocuting in
aid of the new surgical ward of the Chil-
dren’s Hospital.
** * *
Adelaide certainly as the ideal city
for out-door summer shows. While other
Australian capitals have been getting floods,
Adelaide has not had since Christmas any-
thing more than a useful shower. “The
Dandies” have had a good time on the Exhi-
bition terrace, working gradually into pro-
nounced popularity under Dampier’s man-
agement. Miss Vere (Mrs. Dampier) has
been out of the bill latterly, with typhoid.
The farewell night was an occasion. Mean-
while the same Branscombe management is
running the very similar “Scarlet Trouba-
dours” at the new Kihg’s. The bright and
clever Williamson youngsters, at the Royal,
are presenting “Florodora,” and Henry
Bracy has been testing Adelaide voices, to
which—girls only—he gives a high charac-
ter. Unley,_ a'“city” suburban to Adelaide,
has started its annual competitions, which
claim to be already second only to those of
Ballarat. They began in a small local way,
but have since spread to open entries and
big prizes. Frank Harwood is at the Tivoli;
so are the clever juggling Kavanaghs, and
Cooper and Lait, who at any rate work
hard m their one-act plays. As for the vari-
ous picture-shows, they are still increasing
in number. The Empire gives “Hamlet,”
as played in London, and also a Babv
beauty-show. West’s give “Hamlet,” as
played on the spot in Denmark, and also the
Sydney surf-bathing girls. The Wonder-
graph crowd retorts with “The Merchant of
Venice.

Motor Street Cleaners.
The Renault Co. of Paris appear to he always
leading the world in automobile matters, and
their latest achievement is the supplying to the
city of Paris a number of motor-driven street
sweepers. The experiment is a distinct success,
and our city council would do well to enquire into
such an invention.*

SCHNAPPS.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Ola Jane Humphreys marries Ibrahim Hassan,
cousin of the Khedive. The Bulletin trusts she
will not spoil the Egyptian.
Walter Bentley (running a successful “college of
education and dramatic art” in Sydney nowadays)
clears up a few things: Is it my turn to speak yet?
If it is—referring, of course, to some recent enter-
taining Poverty Point pars—let me first plead guilty
to all the Brisbane enisodes related by “Condot”
(B. 20/4/’ll) save that I bought Saturday Night
as a going concern, and disposed of it in the same
way. The pigeon episode I conducted personally,
dispatching the birds from a steamer in the bay.
But the New York business is quite out of my line.
Who the Rev. Jack Costello may be I do not know;
but in all my experiences (and I admit they have
been varied) I never became a parson, and never
had any connection with a church in New York or
elsewhere as its rector. The nearest approach to
preaching was made by me on a voyage from Liver-
pool to New York, when I read the lessons and a
sermon, there not being a parson on board—for a
wonder. I do not know what might happen to me
yet; but I think the parson habit is one 1 won’t be
able to acquire.
Adelaide has had one of those comic theatrical
lawsuits that are more common in London than in
Australia. One of the “Gay Gordons” ladies sued
Fred Winn, the manager, for 12 weeks’ salary at £4
a week, he having, she said, dismissed her because she
declined to dress in the same room with another
“Gay Gordon” lady, whose language was Really Too
Much. Winn’s defence was that he had dismissed
nobody, but he certainly had said she could not have
a room to herself. Anderson, the new K.C., simply
revelled in plaintiff’s cross-examination. She pro-
tested that she herself did not swear, but might
say “Damn!” occasionally. Other questions took
this, form: “Didn’t you refer to Miss Y.’s father’s
low-down pub in Birmingham?” “Didn’t you say
she couldn’t have got on with So-and-So (big London
manager) unless she was familiar with him?”
“Didn’t she then say that, as you tried to get on
with him and failed, he must be particular whom
he is familiar with ?” The answer was always “No” ;
but, as a counsel hardly dare suggest remarks like
that unless he proposes to try to prove them later,
Miss Y.’s evidence was awaited with much interest.
And then the unfeeling Bench stopped the case, with
: a verdict for Winn, just as Miss Y. had entered the
box.
THE ARTIST McCUBBTNS , SEN. AND
JUN , OF MELBOURNE.
The latest to drop into the picture business are
Roland Staveley and Sidney Stirling’, erst mummers-
in-ordinary to J. C. Williamson. The pair have
engaged a slice of the Melbourne Exhibition, which
they will open on May 2 as the People’s Permanent
Picture Palace, at Painfully Popular Prices.
Harry Musgrove, .junr.. pocket edition of the
Family, was married at Melbourne this week. Mus-
grove is the man in a perpetual hurry. Only a few
ears ago, as a very young muzzy, hie was learning
he value Of advertising as a member of the Sydney
Daily Telegraph commercial staff; then Edwin
Geach took him over to help run the Wests’ busi-
ness; before Geach kgew what had happened, Mus-
grove bad appointed himself secretary;. and when
old-man West looked in lately, about the. first thing
he had to do was to sign Musgrove: on as a partner.
And he did it with a good grace.
“L.H.P.”: I am afraid “The Flowers of the
Forest” (1849) and “The Green Bushes” (1845), so
rapturously praised, by .“Earthworm” (B. 30/3/’ll),
are now back numbers. The last time I saw “The
Green Bushes” was in 1886, with the late Grattan
Riggs as Wild Murtagh, supported by Bob Inman as
Kennedy, and Emma Bronton in Celeste’s great part,
Miami, the huntress of the Mississippi. But l«ggs
found “The Irish Detective ” and Boucicault paid
better, and rarely put on Buekstone’s play for more
than a few nights. Neither did the Gattis find
“The Green Bushes” a money-maker in their native
home, the London Adelphi, where in the early 80 s
they elaborately revived it, with Terriss, Kate
Rorke, John L. Shine, Clara Jecks, etc., in the cast.
The fact is, both pieces, like all Buckstone’s melo-
dramas, are out of date. In the ’4o’s, picturesque
gipsies, impossible robbers, daring smugglers, dash-
ing brigands were popular at the Adelphi. Buck-
stone was also lucky in his two low comedians
Edward Wright and Paul Bedford. They, like other
authors of the period, wrote only skeleton parts for
these two inveterate “gaggers,” who, after a while,
usually abandoned the text altogether and trusted to
their own resources. An attempt was made some
years ago to revive “The Flowers of the Forest”;
but the London audience only laughed at what they
called the old-fashioned twaddle. And as it was
then interpreted, they were not far wrong. Yet
modern melodrama, with all its startling effects,
has given the Anglo-Australian stage nothing more
impressive than the last scene of this play.
“Exile” (from Fogtown) : For the benefit of
Coronators, let me mention that the Empire and the
Alhambra are still the most entrancing places in this
hamlet. For one thing they provide plenty of room
to walk about, plenty of room to sit down and the
most comfortable seats in Christendom. At each
man’s elbow is a stand for his whisky and soda and
his cigar. And when the show palls there is the
promenade. Here the hedonist and the voluptuary
can see the most extraordinary market in the world
carried on decorously and acording to law. Dozens
of beautiful women, richly and fashionably,. dressed,
ambulate around silently and gracefully. The
visitor is never accosted. That is against the rules.
But he may accost ; and he is never rebuffed. The
bars are gorgeous and highly expensive, and if you
ask for a cup of tea, two doctors attached to the
theatre immediately “certify,” and you are removed
from kith and kin. The show at each place covers
the usual music-hall agenda, excepting that the Em-
pire and Alhambra ballets are unequalled anywhere.
“Pea Nut”: The development of the old, original
“Press representative,” or puff paragraphist, into a
skilled purveyor of facts and fiction, is a very not-
able feature of theatrical enterprise these times.
No management can cut much ice without the ser-
vices of a gossip-monger, of some pretensions as a
journalist, who issues to the Press in type-written
form a ceaseless flow of paragraphic matter concern-
ing the show and all people and things connected
therewith. Spme of the feeblest shows give oppor-
tunity for the' most impressive newsmongering; some
of the most ordinary mummers are good subjects for
extraordinary anecdotes, and some of the smallest
truths can be magnified into an appearance of im-
portance. Then, made up in a packet, more or
less skilfully mixed, items are blown like seeds into
town and country newspaper offices, to fall on stony
or fruitful ground as the case may be., We talk of
mummers as always posing in the limelight off the
stage, as though they and the newspapers, and the
women and boys who are stage-struck, were jointly
responsible for theatrical tattle. As a matter of
fact, the managers now supply most of the limelight
off the stage as well as on it. The up-to-date Press
representative is the limelight man.
“An Australian ‘At Home’ ” —who doesn’t feel it
—-sends a vehement disparagement of Maud Allan,
the Cockney dancer whose public gyrations have
made her fortune in London. She is described as
“a weird creature, ungainly, spider-limbed, and
sicklied o’er with the pale cast of pastry. I don’t
think she would draw twopence in Australia, where
theatregoers have no pathological interest,” con-
tinued the traducer. “I don’t think she would draw
much anywhere out of London. She is like some-
thing of Aubrey Beardsley’s that has come to life,
and a class of rich Cockneys watch with consumptive
curiosity to see what she will do next.”
“Melb”: The Tait presentation of “Called Back”
on the biograph is very creditable. The cast is not
pretentious, but. does fairly. The ideal in Australia
would be Beatrice Holloway as Pauline, Rignoid or
Mervale as Macari, Athol wood as Ceneri, and Roberts
as Vaughan. How well “Called Back” opens—better
than any other work of fiction, I think. You are en-
thralled on the first page. On the other hand, how
slow Dickens, Scott, Thackeray and Balzac are in
getting off. Both “Pickwick” and “Vanity Fair”
were, in fact, in danger of stoppage when they first
appeared as serials.
“Hamer”: After all, the picture-show, instead of
proving a curse, has turned out to be a blessing in
disguise to the travelling mummer.. The innumerable
shows have absorbed a large number of heavy-voiced
actors to interpret the puzzle-pictures to the aud-
iences ; whilst other mummers, who made a pre-
carious living in travelling engagements, having
raked together enough money for a cinematograph
outfit, have blossomed out as prosperous showmen.
The touring dramatic show is, therefore, not as
numerous as it used to be, and after a surfeit of
pictures” the country towns rush the comparative
rarity when it appears. In these happy circumstances
the actor’s screw has been substantially increased, and
in some well authenticated cases he has received the
whole of it.
“Billy’s Nephew”: Re “Propstick’s” estimate of
“Billy” Hoskins (B. 30/3/’ll). “Propstick” evi-
dently knows hardly anything of the man he be-
littles. My uncle was playing leads to Macready at
Sadler’s Wells in the early ’so’s. He was the ori-
ginal Cardinal Richelieu, and played that part
during 1841-2 for 101 nights. In London he was
recognised as a sterling actor; was his own lessee,
and played alternate nights with G. V. Brooke. His
subsequent career in Australia stamped him as one
of the finest Shakspearian students and a comedian
second to none, as witness his Doctor Pangloss and
Sir Peter Teazle. On September 25, 1869, he produced
in Sydney “The Tempest,” and it had a great run,
a critic of the day saying that seldom in anv first-
class theatre, even in the Old World, had a play of
Shakspeare’s been represented with greater ability
or more signal success. I have before me my uncle’s
speech—-a marvel of diction. His intellect was
great to the last, and when an old man, almost 70,
he still held the public’s regard for his impersona-
tion of old man Jaikes, which is still remembered.
Among all old actors he was generously recognised
as a fine, all-round actor; yet “Propstick” says he
had no variety! Among present-day actors, like
mv friend Harry Diver, his is still a name to con-
jure by, and he is remembered by the proud title —
“Father of the Australian stage.” “Propstick” asks
what became of his little son by his third wife. It
shows clearly that he does not know very much
about the family. And neither is it any concern
of his, otherwise I would tell him.
There is only one continuous picture-show in
Maoriland, and to the McMahons are the profits.
The Me. brothers, who were pioneers of the picture-
graph business in Australia, have fitted out a home
in Wellington (M.L.) for the newest venture, and
have named it The New Theatre.
“Briner”: The character of Captain Williams, in
the drama of “Paid in Full,” now running in Mel-
bourne, is suggested by Steinberger, an American,
who flourished in the latter part of Grant’s Presi-
dency, and had a yacht named the Peerless, for
hanky-panky in southern seas. America, England
and Germany ell coveted Samoa. Steinberger had a
grand plan to seize it for America, as Jameson planned
to seize the Transvaal. He wanted Grant to give
him authority, and Grant appears to have at least
told him that he might do what he liked without
being afraid of America treating him as a pirate.
So Steinberger went and collared the Samoan capital
and hoisted the American flag. Germany, however,
howled, and put England on the qui vive, whereupon
Captain Stevens, in H.M.S. Barracoota, a wooden
paddle-tub, well known in Sydney, went to Samoa
and toppled the intruder over. America was nettled ;
and the English Admiralty brought Stevens before
a Court Martial, and, to save appearances, repri-
manded him. ne" was like Rooke, who took Gib-
raltar. England reprimanded him—-but stuck to the
Rock.

play in Australia, was the original Trilbv.
“Lud”: The cast of Bulwer’s “Money,” to be
acted at Drury Lane on May 17, before the German
Emperor and King George, includes: Clara Douglas,
Irene Vanbrugh; Georgina, Miss Carlisle; Lady
Franklin, Winifred Emery; Evelyn, G. Alexander;
Vessey, Hare; Graves, Beerbohm-Tree; Blount, Cyril
Maude; Stout, Bourchier; Smooth, Charles Wynd-
ham; Glossmore, F. Terry; Sharp, Laurence Irving.
Subordinate parts are taken by such as Weedon Gros-

tine, Loraine, Lyn Harding, Esmond, Fernandez and
Boucicault. Bancroft is stage-manager. No less
than four theatrical Knights are involved, and no
Julius. Vet I will wager the performance will be
inferior; such galaxies always are. The illustrious
actors don’t know their unwonted parts.
The bosses of the theatrical profesh. in London are
doing their best to boost the cause of National De-
fence. Prior to the recent annual meeting of the
Actors’ Association at Her Majesty’s Theatre, there
was a meeting of the National Service League,
whereat Lord Roberts, the Dean of Norwich, cricketer
C. B. Fry and others joined with Beerbohm Tree,
Courtneidge and Oscar Asche in re-asserting the fact
that Britons are living in a fools’ paradise, where an
Englishman’s Home will prove' to be a poor castle if
the invader comes along. The text of the N.S.L. is
“compulsory drill,” and the theatrical managers have
undertaken to commend it to the notice of the gal-
lant armies who rally round the Flag in spectacular
drama and comic opera. Also, it is understood that
the orchestras will miss no opportunity to play “Rule
Britannia!”
Marie Corelli has written a theatrical sketch to
satirise the suffragettes. The trifle, entitled “The
Sedan Chair,” is to happen at the London Hippo-
drome. The scene is laid in a country cottage, and
there are five characters whose chief object in life
appears to be to fire epigrams at one another. Marie
expects some of them to do terrible damage to
the woman’s movement.
“Haresfoot”: When a new play comes out, take
up an old one. The effective drama, “Right is
Might,” at Melbourne King’s, is built on Robert-
son’s comedy “Progress.” A New York syndicate
wants to hook together two railroad lines in Texas,
but cannot get across Beatrice Day’s ranch. She
won’t Sell the right at any price. In “Progress”
the ranch is the English park of the Vavasours. This
earlier play was founded on the real case of the
Duke of Buckingham and Aylesbury Vale, where the
ducks come from. The Duke, like other landholders,
would not sell at all. Millions would not compen-
sate for the murder of his ancestral estate. How-
ever, when the thing had been done by main force,
he turned round, and was as pleased as the Dudleys
over their iron and coal properties. The Duke’s son,
Chandos, became, ill 1 fact, a railway magnate.
“U.S.” : I lately introduced, Poverty Point to
Dorothy Nicholson, the star of the American Bio-
graph Co. Her more powerful rival is Florence
Turner, of the Vitagraph Co. No other actress in
the world appears before so many millions. An in-
terviewer says there was only one question he dared
not ask Florence. It was whether she had descended
from the Lord’s Ancient People. He is himself con-
vinced that only one race could produce such genius.
Compare her with those French and Italian biograph
actresses, their conventional emotion, their heavings
and gaspings. But you can’t. As for other great
biograph performers, Foolshead is Signor Deed, of
Turin; Tontolini is a Roman imitator. Miss Turner
impresses upon the interviewer that the astonish-
ingly spontaneous appearance of the biograph is the
fruit of painstaking, assiduous, and minute rehearsal
of every scene. Behind the operator there will be a
ring of critics, with the inexorable stage manager
saying, “No, no, that won’t do.” “Oh,” says she,
“those pictures cause me a good deal of thought, I
assure you. Have I not laid awake thinking, think-
ing? We have a story drafted, with careful dia-
logue. The early pictures were so absurd because
the characters did not talk. See how you talk
when any crisis occurs in real life. What rapid in-
terchanges of conversation ! You must talk in front
of the camera exactly the same as if the public
would hear your voice.”
J. C. Williamson relates how, when he first pro-
duced Gilbert and Sullivan’s plays in Melbourne, the
old Irish cabman who used to drive him home was
under the impression that G. and S., whose names
he had seen up in big letters, were actors appearing
at the theatre. “Mr. Williamson,” he said one
night, “you do be havin’ two great new min at
the theaytre—-Gilbert and Sullivan. Yis, they’re
grate min. Some like Gilbert, but I like Sullivan
meself. Yis, he’s the best man, be a long way.”
Contracts have just been signed by the -‘Bohemian
Twins,” Rosa and Josefa Blazek, for a tour of Aus-
tralia and Maoriland, commencing next Christmas.
Rosa and Josefa are joined together in such a man-
ner that they manage with three instead of four
hips. Rosa is married, and has an offspring, whose
coming was awaited with tremendous interest by
the medical profession, and whose arrival was de-
scribed at length by that delicate organ, the British
Medical Journal. The twins present about the
only good argument on record in favor of polygamy.
“S.S.”: Australian Socialists have all
taken Nietzsche to their bosoms, and quo£e the
inspired German exuberantly ; but they do not*
quote this from “A Glance at the State”': “To
demand equality of rights, as do the Socialists, is
by no means the outcome of justice, but of
covetousness. If you expose bloody pieces of
flesh to a beast, and withdraw them again, until
it finally begins to roar, do you think that roaring
implies justice ?” Nietzsche was, indeed, a greater
Anti-Sosh than George Reid, and I wonder-at
the Socialists’ delight in him. Of course, the
weakness of the quoted analogy, is on the surface.
To make the simile real, you must admit that the
beast hunted and killed the meat; and then the
roaring does imply justice, as when the producer
says to the monopolist: “ That’s my meat! ”
A building with a past will shortly be
demolished in Flinders-lane, Melbourne. During
the first roars of the ’so’s it was tenanted by De
Gruchy and Leigh, lithographers, who used to
publish views of infant Melbourne. In 1856, and
for a few years afterwards, it was occupied by a
compositor named Ward, who afterwards opened
as a printer in Little Collins-street, and founded
the Australian Journal, the present mild pro-
perty of the Massina people.
Thus H. S. A. Smail, a Sydney man who
has climbed high in the Civil Serviceof the Feder-
ated Malay States :
We get our labor from Sumatra and Java, and some-
times from Madras. But it is not cheap, by any means.
1 once had ideas about black labor for some kinds of work
in Australia—away at the North, you know. But after
my last few years’ experience I am for White Australia all
the time.
Which only goes to show that .T. Caesar was right
when he said to the humiliated Caractacus:
“Carac, old chafer, an ounce of experience is
worth a ton of theory.”
Conservatism lost another joint in its tail
last Sunday at Sydney, when a “district” of the
Australian Journalists’ Association was formed at a
representative meeting of daily and weekly paper
Sressmen. Victoria, Queensland, South Aus. and
’. S. Wales are now hauling on the same line,
and the first fish they expect to land is the Regis-
trar’s decision that the A.J.A. can enter into
business relations with the Federal Arbitration
Act. The old hedgehog ideas are so rapidly being
burnt out these times that any day now may
spring from their ashes the Industrial Union of
Reformed Parsons.
The Westralian Labor party has marked
two or three of its members down for an overhaul-
ing. One or two are to have hints thrown at
them that the party can’t afford to carry passen-
gers, and that only a working crew is wanted ;
while Horan, who advised all hands to say “ No.
No” in the Referenda is to be confronted with
that and other political offences, and probabiy
invited to take his proper place outside. The
’Groper Labor camp is ruled from the goldfields ;
and the mining-field Laborite hasn’t, as a rule,
any time for the man who isn’t a whole-hogger.
The German steamer Coblenz berthed on
the western side of Sydney’s Circular Quay on
Saturday, and the British steamer Mataram
berthed on the eastern side. The Coblenz had on
board 103 Chinese coolies bound for Tahiti to
work on the plantations; the Mataram brought
50 Javanese coolies en route for the nickel mines
in New Caledonia. The South Pacific is getting
nicely populated. Both China and Java are suf-
fering from plague in its worst form. Some of
these days Sydney, as the assignation house for
these relays of cheap labor, will be called upon to
pay heavily for the honor.
Staniforth Smith was reminded of his old
Kalgoorlie days almost as soon as he had got back
from the Papuan expedition which so nearly
turned itself into a funeral for him and his whole
party. One of the first wires he received was
“My God, I am proud of you,” sent by an old
Kalgoorlie chum who had been on the spot with
Smith when Bigjohn Forrest faced the “infuri-
ated ” diggers and was accidentally poked in the
midribs with an umbrella. That brolly-poke was
magnified into a t’othersider’s murderous attack
on the ’Groper leader, whereupon that other old
’Groper, Maitland Brown, R.M., broke out by
wire into the historic hysterics.
A swift and certain movement has started
to honor one of the big little men of Sydney,
Prof. M. W. MacCallum, who this year completes
his quarter-century in the Challis chair of Modern
Literature at the University. MacCallum, who
owns the front name of Mungo (St. Mungo is the
patron saint o’Glasgie), provides an inspiriting
example of hard work leading to its own recog-
nition. A brilliant graduation at Glasgow started
him on the path. The post he left to come
to Sydney in 1886 was that of Professor of English
at Aberystwith College (University of Wales);
and even that far back he had made a reputation
by his literary articles, and had won the friend-
ship of Leslie Stephen and other eminents. In
England he was really a mediseval scholar ; in Aus-
tralia he has built up a vigorous modern school of
literary studies—English, French and German.
Twenty years ago English was a neglected sub-
ject, quite subordinated to classics ; but all that
has been changed. In between classes he has
made time to produce books on “ Tennyson and the
Arthurian Legend” and “Shakspeare’s Roman
Plays”; the latter, published last year, won
flattering criticisms. His own Drivate good
works, in the way of assisting students and so
forth, are manifold—which has gone far to make
him one of the most popular professors in Aus-
tralia. The proposal is to commission Longstaff
to paint a portrait to be hung at the University ;
any surplus is to go towards a scholarship or
something of the sort to promote the study of
modern literature.
“Pick”: In what unheard-of part of
Australia does “Crass” (B. 30/3/T1) reside
where, among an adult male population of 30,000,
none can he safely backed to hit a town hall with
a rifle at 200 yards ? In the larger cities there are
those probably who, from force of circumstances,
cannot shoot, though every city has its rifle clubs;
but the fact that in N. S. Wales alone over
27,000 men have been enrolled as voluntary rifle-
men reservists, who annually are called upon to
qualify by a course of musketry, should make it
appear even to a decayed-fish party like “Crass ”
that something is being done ; while in the coun-
try I emphatically deny that “ Crass” or anyone
else can find among every 100 phvsically-
capable men five who cannot shoot, and
shoot-well. In ray district every male over
the ag§ of 10, and many of the other sex
as "well, know all that it is to be learned
about a ; and putting on a “ possible,” or, with
a repeater, howling over a rabbit running at full
speed, is a common feat, the like of which Mere-
dith probabiy had in mind when he remarked that
“ Australians can shoot.” I have been a member
of a Smith Coast rifle club for 10 years, but have
not yet met the restrictive and exclusive condi-
tions that “Cris Crass” complains of. Our
annual subscription is a modest “ half-caser,” and
ammunition is nearly always free. Certainly rifles
are not lavishly supplied, and what are doled out
are of an antiquated pattern ; but they shoot well,
all the same, if kept in order, and any enthusiast
may purchase from the authorities a gun of his
own on the very easiest terms.
HAMLET IN HEAVEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was an actor good and grand
Who won his way to Paradise;
Upon a cloud he took a stand,
And gazed about the holy band,
The angels pinioned and precise.
Came one with measured steps and slow,
With high and melancholy mein,
And pale young face, and shirt cut low.
“Here’s one at least that I should know.
Who’s this, so tragic and so lean?”
“That’s Hamlet, sir,” a saint replied;
“The Prince of Denmark, sir, you see!”
“That Hamlet? Bah!” the actor cried.
Stirred was his fine professional pride—
“ You angels wait till you’ve seen me!”
victoria.
SILAS SNELL.
THREE WORKINGMEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas in “the gods’ ” Olympus packed,
Amongst the humble blokes,
I watched the funny man who cracked
The comedy’s best jokes;
He crossed his eyes—he slid and fell
With force upon the floor,
And was rewarded with a yell
Or some lone fool’s guffaw. ,
Two brawny toilers, half-asleep,
Stared glumly at the show;
Upon his knees they watched him creep,
The man who “made it go.”
They had large corns upon their hands,
And brick-dust seemed to lurk
About their nodding heads. Their arms
Were fit for any work.
Like sympathetic judges they
Looked down upon the place
Whereon the jester of the play
Dealt roughly with his face;
They listened to his frightened shouts
And watched his “comic” rage,
As all the chorus dealt him clouts
And kicked him round the stage.
Three acts of this the pair sat out,
And then the rag came down
Upon the stage folk ranged about
The breathless, bowing clown.
One toiler muttered, with a sigh,
“I pity that Jim.”
“Same ’ere” ; groaned James, “I’m glad that I
Ain’t got t’ toil like ’im!”

SOCIETY
Auckland (M.L.) lias just been badly
seared by the discovery of three cases of bubonic
plague, one of which was swiftly fatal. The fit of
cleanliness and rat-suppression induced by the
plague scare of a few years back soon passed, and
left the local authorities to their normal slumber.
Now that the disease has become endemic, even
the Tory Herald cries aloud for the State health
officers to be given control of the cleaning-up,
instead of the job being left to the “dirty coun-
cils.” Some idea of the condition of the slummiest
parts of the city can be got from the evidence at
the inquest on an old man who died in a stable
loose-box about a stone’s throw from the main
street,; This shelter, which housed two old men,
was so filthy that the police had to drench it with
disinfectants before they would venture in ; and,
the man’s clothes had to be removed with tongs,
and his body covered with quicklime in the coffin.
Yet only a little while back the City Council
reduced its staff of sanitary inspectors in the
glorious cause of economy and the reduction of
taxes.

The usual herd of marine mules sailed into
the line of tire on blaster Monday, when the garri-
son artillery was trying to snatch a little practice
from the Port Jackson forts. The men behind
the guns waited and swore at the white sails
hovering about the dancing targets ; but at last,
seeing that it was useless, the officers shut down
the shooting programme for the day. The holiday
yachtsman has got into the habit of cheerfully
ignoring the formal published warning that shot
practice is to be carried out, and that he will
greatly oblige by not risking his life in the
vicinity ; and it really seems that one or two of
him will have to be killed for the discouragement
of the others. The Shocking Example was just
missed a while back at the South Channel forts
in Port Phillip, on a morning which had been
publicly dedicated to practice. A breeze that
had been blowing dropped suddenly, and a boat,
which had been trying to run past, was left help-
less on the face of the glassy sea. One of the
crew, who wasn’t the sole support of his widow
mother, but wanted to live all the same, asked if
there was any danger; but the skipper lit his
pipe, and like a true Liberal answered “No,
No ! ” Being pressed further, he said irritably
that he reckoned the boat wasn’t very far inside
the danger zone. Two minutes later there was
the hollow slam of a big gun from shoreward, and,
with a long crescendo scream, a devil plunged
from the sky into the sea just under the boat’s
nose, and burst with a dull boom. A white
column of foam leaped up like a tall tpmbstone
and collapsed—-partly on the boat. However, the
next shot was well away, and the succeeding
shells so remote that a less shaken band of
sailors might have enjoyed them. To this day
none of them are sure whether a homicidal bom-
bardier was at work, or whether it was plain
rotten markmanship.
* * * *
A touching case of the thrift which the
Right Thinking Person generally believes should
be rewarded with an extra vote has come under
medical notice in Victoria. A Brighton gardener
took his load of turk’a caps, etc., to market, then
loaded up with manure, and on the way bought a
German sausage, which he bestowed in the tool-
box under the waggon. Arrived home he forgot
all about the mystery, but suddenly called it to
mind a week or so later. Being a worthy man,
he handed the goods to the wife and desired her
to cut away the overripe, parts and put the rest
on the day’s menu. The local doctor succeeded
in saving the wife and the youngsters, but it isn’t
certain at time of writing whether the gardener
will be an angel or an M.L.C.

“Berkeley”: He was a model young
man, but, as usual, a woman caused his downfall.
When she threw him over, he rushed into my
room, his usually sleek hair disordered, his eyes
rolling in frenzy. “ Look at that !” he groaned,
throwing her letter at me. “Look at it! By
Heaven, it’s drink I must have now.” He was so
enthusiastic that I hadn’t the heart to contradict
him. “Come —come! It’s to the Devil now!”
he shouted. Joyously I accompanied him. He
flung his coin recklessly on to the bar. “Beer,”
he demanded. “Beer!” And then, as an after-
thought : “And put some lemonade in mine.”
What a lot a girl will endure if she be-
lieves that slur is a Venus or an Ivy Schilling !
Open sea-bathing in Melbourne was only legalised
at the tail-end of the season, and the water was
already cold before most people could get their
clothes ready. But the fairies with limbs to be
displayed are not to be deterred by a mere trifle
like cold sea. Along the foreshore the shapely
maiden in costume that fits her like a mould con-
tinues to display her beauties in spite of wind
and icebergs. A crowd of male things gazing in
the most abstracted way out to sea is a sure indi-
cation that some bashful darling who would scorn
to show her shoulder in the public street is about
to take the waters. Many of the girls seem to
fully realise that it is a shame to wet the pretty
things they wear, and put off the awful moment
as long as possible. It is a terrible thing to have
a good figure in cold weather.
* * * *
So far as Victoria is concerned the death
knell of that venerable and insanitary institution,
the municipal tip, was sounded at a recent Board
of Health meeting, when it was resolved that the
abominations should be abolished, and that the
metropolitan municipalities and the cities of Bal-
larat, Bendigo and Geelong be given two years
notice to become decent. Which means the
adoption of the up-to-date “ destructor ” system
of disposal, whereby the smellful garbage, with
its dead cat and defunct dog, may be quickly and
effectively disposed of by fire. The destructor not
only justifies its title, but there is a valuable
municipal asset in its residue—clinker, solder from
tins. etc. In fact, it has been proved that the
superannuated jam pot and the rejected kerosene
tin can be converted into paving blocks, or goats’
feed or something, while generating valuable
motive power, light, etc., in the process.

An American writer, after touring Aus-
tralia, sets out to prove in the Mm Pacific maga-
zine (Honolulu) that the Scawtch pereon who
described the Commonwealth as “ the saddest
place on earth” had porridge in his eyesight.
ISome clips
I love Sydney next to Pari®, and more than I do New
York. Sydney is more home-like than Paris, and almost
as gay at times. ... Melbourne is Chicago all over again,
except that Melbourne is Australia one day in the year—
when the Melbourne Cup is raced f0r.... I did Tasmania,
and loathed to leave that wonderful island. ... Sydney
carries more of a metropolitan air than any American
city other than New York. .. . I went to Australia ex-
pecting to be disappointed, vet every day there were new
impressions, and every day I postponed the date of my
departure ; for Australia has as much, if not more, to
teach the American than has any Old World country.
The American writer gets off the rails in places,
nevertheless. For instance, referring to Sydney,
he says : “It is the rule that any woman found
on the streets after dark on the night before New
Year’s Day may be kissed.” No account of how
it feels to be in an Australian gaol is given, so
apparently the Yankee man did not try to put
the alleged rule into practice.
* # * »
On the American continent much fuss is
made over flags. In a U.S. college every student
has “Old Glory ” nailed up in his room, and any-
one who enters is supposed to salute it. The
other day, according to a letter written by an
Australian bov to his father, a Sydney student
who had the flag of the Southern Cross displayed
in his diggings at an Ontario college, pointedly
drew the attention of some visitors to the fact
that that was the ensign of his country. In-
stantly they saluted it. Australia as an entity is
getting along.
THE EMANCIPATION OF WOMAN.
“ Hurrah! Out of hobbles into trousers.
“Klinker,” from an engine-room :
Marine disasters are usually associated with a break-
down of the machinery. Hence I’m not so surprised
that now and then a ship conies to g rief on the Australian
coast, as I am that the disasters are so few ; for once Aus-
tralian coastal vessels start they are kept going' without
any proper overhaul of machinery until the engines can-
not run any longer or something is absolutely smashed.
Even then the repairs are rushed so that the ship shall
not lose the usual run. Passenger boats go sometimes
for 12 months without a stop, and are then rushed into
dock for five working days where they should have as
in my weeks. Theycome into port looking spick and span
outside ; but if you went below, you would sometimes
find the engine-rooms and stokeholes perfect wrecks,
dangerous alike to crew and passengers.

Insolent hound ! Once on the ground
He won’t be able to “ sliick.”

Then, like u in in, as soon as yon can,

“ Adelaide ” : Somebody suggests (B.
13/4/’ll), apropos the revived effort to put down
spitting on the footpaths in Brisbane, that Svdney
and Melbourne could make big revenues by fining
their culprits £2 apiece. Well, let them try, and
they will be pleasantly disappointed. A mere
handful of fines, and the knowledge that there
were more in the bag, would stamp out the un-
pleasant practice, which arose only from laziness
in the first place. It has been thus stamped out
in Adelaide. So far as the principal streets are
concerned, it simply does, not exist. And there
was hardly any’ trouble about-it.

“ Gwy ” : Cables mention that it is likely
’Ome Universities will confer honorary degrees 011
some of the Coronating politicians from Oversea.
If Fisher and co. hav.e any sense of the fitness of
things, they will shovv it by declining 1 the mstinc-
tion, such as it is. Speaking as a graduate who
had to graft fbr his degrees, I should like to
observe that if there is any English-conferred
“honor” more foolish than the rest, not even
excepting the cheap little C.M.G., it is the
honorary degree. It really signifies nothing.
The degree usually selected for this discreditable
purpose is the LL.D. or D.C.L. Now, the degree
of Doctor of Laws, won by exafnination, is one of
the most difficult to obtain, and one of tlje
soundest evidences that its possessor is some
pumpkins in ' the way of erudition at j
least. In N. S. Wales, for example, though '
many desire it, few have the courage to
sit for it, and; the Doctors of Law may
almost be counted on the fingers. But
universities, which are usually so punctilious in
their demands that they won’t encourage (say) a
promising science student unless he has
“ crammed ” some dead languages which he will
proceed to forget as speedily as possible, have
fallen into the bad habit of flinging this hon.
LL.D. at any visiting personage., Thus Sydney
University went through the solemn foolishness of
making the York dukeling an LL.D; when he was
here 10 years ago, though no one pretends that
he has ever absorbed enough law to give him a mild
headache. Similarly, when Editor Ward, of
Sydney D.T., returned from a recent jaunt to
England, it was with the same tag, and his sense
of hbinor isn’t sufficiently developed to keep him
frori£ calling himself “ Dr.” The honorary degree,
of Whatever kind, is bogus—a cheap irpita,tidn of
the real article, and its conferring is an injustice
to those, who have won it in the genuine way. If
the Universities really thirst to honor distin-
guished visitors, let them institute some special
degree—another brand, so to speak. The foolish-
ness of the thing might then become apparent.

The harem skirt has already killed a man
-at Bendigo (Vic.). He wasn’t wearing the gar-
ment himself, but he got in the way of a curious
Easter holiday crowd which was following an
excited female who was wearing it; and, being
old and not nimble, he fell and was trodden; to
death Lovely woman always makes the danger-
ous mistake of trying her dress experiments m
crowded cities and streets, instead of making
tests in lonesome places like Yassberra or
“Liberal” meetings, and gradually working in
towards civilisation.
A Maorilfinder has discovered a new way
of clearing his character. Finding that a story
had broken loose in the town that lie .was detected
in his store “on a certain Sunday night with a
certain young lady,” he appointed a commission
of two parsons, three solicitors and one Civil ser-
vant to try him- Evidence tor the prosecution
was called through the advertising columns of the
local paper, and a date and place fixed fot the
hearing but the prosecution was unanimous in
i its absent a fact which may have-been an elo-
quent tribute to the storekeeper’s virtueor merely
the result of a wholesome and intelligent fear of
having to subsequently face an action for slander.
Anyhow, after waiting 20 minutes, the defence
was entered upon, four witnesses were examined
on oath, and the -judgment of the commission
published in the provincial newspapers next day
as an advertisement, thus :
Wei being a committee set up for the purpose of in-
quiring into the alleged misbehavior of Mr.” *
certain young lady, and, having thoroughly investigated
the said allegations, do unanimously and unhesitatingly ,
declare, on positive proof, that such charges have not (l-e
slightest foundation in fact.
It is an original idea, and opens the way to some
curious ; possiV>ilities. Any day, now, an advertise-
ment of the following nature may be expected :
Public Notice.
In the matter of a suspicion that has been circulated in
police circles that I- broke and entered the premises of
John Smith and. Co., merchants, and stole certain moneys,
I Burglar Bill, Caused to he set up a committee.™ in-
vestigation, consisting of six reputable.gents. No evioenee
for the prosecution being forthcoming 1 , William Sikes,
Esq Joseph Cionk, Esq., and Jemmy Chubb, Esq., gave
evidence on oath for the defence. The committee then
unanimously declared that the rumor had ho foundation
in fact. God Save the King. Given under my seal and
• finger-print th.fs day.—B. Bill.

* * # *
‘ ‘ Rifleman ” writes -
The recurrent growl about the insufficiency of targets
for practice vitalises the question as to the efficacy of the
fixed target as a war preparation. This fact is unques-
tionable, anyhow—the nations which should know most
about the business are scrapping the fixed object, with
its known ranges, except as a preliminary training for
recruits, and seeking to perfect the moving apparatus.
Captain Brand, an Australian officer attached to tne
Indian army, lately sent along to the Common-
wealth authorities an interesting report of a “ fire
problem ” meeting held at Umballa. No bull's-
eye targets were used, but) in each match the com-
petitors, who were divided into, teams of varying
strengths, were set a problem to he worked out by. the
individuals competing or the officers commanding the
teams. In one match two men formed a team. The men
were told that a scouting party of three was advancing at
a range of anything up to 1500yds. The “ enemy ” was
represented by three targets working across country from
one cover to another, at intervals of about two yards. If
the teams fired ap one target before the others appeared
it vanished, and nbne of the targets materialised there-
after; but if fire were withheld until the three were
visible they raced across to the nearest cover after the
fashion of the “ running-man" targets. It looks a good
test of a soldier’s fi. ness for his job. If he fired too soon
he got no further chance of hits ; if he delayed too long
the “ enemy ” got to cover and he got no hits at all. And
as he had to judge the distance as well as fire at the right
time, he had to know his business to win through.
Another match was open to teams comprising
either' a double company or a squadron —l2O
men with a commander. The commanders were
told that the. “enemy” was using a certain
locality as headquarters. The alleged force, represented
bv targets, appeared suddenly at an (unknown range
sav, 1500yds. If fire was not opened promptly it ducked,
and re-appeared at, say, 1100yds. ; and if it wasn’t then
shot at it again went under and bobbed up at, sav,
700yds., and so on. Here, again, coolness, rapidity and
accuracy of distance-judging were the things that stuck
to the rifleman. Of two things Brand’s Indian experi-
ence has convinced him— (a) Judging distances must
take a foremost place in the training of the Common-
wealth’s citizen S''ldlers ; and (b) Australian officers
must get all the practice they can in fire discipline and
control. 1

THE SORROWFUL STAGER.
[For The Bulletin.]
The grand old stager bowed his head above his
dinner-beer, . . .
A thousand headache noises drummed into his aged
And Trom the food ten thousand notes of fly-enjoy-
ment rose,
A loud and vicious hum. The old man, worried
with his woes,
Groaned in his beard. His soup was but a reservoir
for flies.
And, as he groaned, he gazed upon his beer with
plaintive eyes.
The summer day was blazing hot, and very warm
and wiiu
The table-helper was, his table-manners far from
mild;
And, as he whirled hot grub before each hungry
toiler there, , • .
The thumping of his feet threw clouds of dust into
the air;
And all the time he bravely held a horde of flies at
bay, . „ . .
Nor failed to curse the heat and dust in a refreshing
way.
And yet, for all the blazing heat, the cloud and
swarming flies,
The coarse corn-beef-’n’-cabbage smells, the table-
helper’s cries
for plates of rude, assorted food, the crowd that
sat at meat,
Contentedly continued to resist the flies, and eat.
The grand old stager, he alone, of that perspiring
bunch,
Sat groaning on his chair, and failed his plate of
food to munch.
A thousand gums around the pub sighed faintly in
the heat,
The table-helper sighed as well, and paused upon
his beat ,
Seeing the old man’s shoulders heave, he said, The
flies are bad!
This heat is Hell! But try an’ eat.” The stager,
wholly sad, ... T . .
Looked up, and moaned, “Th heat is bad. But,
son, it isn’t that.
“Look ’ere!”, (He caught his, breath.) “Just look!
It’s flat. Th’ beer is flat!”

ANNOYED WITH NATURE'S PLAN.
ALMOST INSANE DAIRY EMPLOYEE (to milking herd lowing at the gate of cowyard)
“ Hell! Are all yous back again l Why th ’ blazes can't yer LAY IT same as fowl s!”
“A.L.”: Old timers on the cattle runs
about the head waters of the Upper Murray,
the Mitta Mitta, the Snowy and the Tambo
Rivers, would be greatly amused at recent
exhibition attempts in Sydney to throw and
tie a steer. Years ago, when wild cattle
were numerous, bullocks ten and twelve hun-
dred pounds in weight were thrown, tied and held
fast till the herd of tailers were brought to them.
Even now stockmen in those parts think nothing
of running down, throwing, ear-marking and bang-
ing the tail of a two-year-old cleanskin, and they
do it on their own, too. A stockhorse on one of
those runs, with hardly a touch on the reins from
his rider, will follow the beast you are after, wheel
with it, range up alongside when the timber is not
too thick, pull up dead in two strides as the animal
i 3 thrown, and never think of moving away when
you leave him.
“Eddvson”: This happened within a few
miles of the spot where the two seeond-ha# nnmng
trucks, hauled by a donkey “portable, winch the
Vic Government has the audacity to call a mou -
tain railway, butts into a gum-stump at Gembrook,
and decides to go no further. Twain was stand-
ing at his kitchen door, enjoying the evening
glow as the declining sun slid over Slippery
Hill when the sore-looking stranger rolled his

“Condamine”: Did I hear someone say
that a horse never gets bushed? Let hin J who sa[s
so attend. I was a good way out in a belt of scrub
the other day, when, owing to the sky becoming-
cloudy, I was at a loss to find my way back again,
so I decided to trust to my charger’s homing n-
stinct, and gave him his head. He set off tieely,
and I am sure that he considered he was mak-
ing for home. In about a quarter of an hour 1
noticed that we were back to where I first re-
linquished the helm. I guided him for a while in a
different direction, and let him go again.. Same re-
sult. For curiosity I gave him another trial, and
once more he looped-the-loop. Just here I decided
that this particular prad’s bump bf locality was
about the size of a hen’s ear, and, selecting a break
in the clouds for a guide, I took a firm hold of ins
countenance and eventually conducted him into the
open. -

“Yarramalong” : Greenhide and No. 8 fenc-
ing-wire are not the mainstay of the coastal cocky,
whatever they may be of the Outbacker. Corn cobs
are: without them the coastal cocky’s life would be
a blank. They are, to start with, used as fuel,
although, as heat producers, they are dismal failures,
for, when first lighted, they flare up. like a capi-
talist when high wages are mentioned, and in a few
minutes are a smoking, smellful mass like a 1< rce-
trade argument. But —they cost nothing. A real
cocky never uses a curry-comb and brush to give
his horses their annual grooming. Not he! lie
simply takes a piece of chaff-bag in one hand, a
worn‘corn-cob in the other, and hard words in his
mouth. Also, he uses cobs for emergency reaping-
hook handles, file handles, lighting his pipe (saves
matches), as corks for his “kag” (keg), water-bag,
and yeast bottles. My own opinion is that cocky
reckons he could open the door of Sheol with a
corn-cob and escape.
“Walkabout”: The real ’Groper isn’t
dead; on the contrary, in some quarters his dislike
for the t’othersider is as strong now as it was years
ago. When Bill arrived at W'hangamup, he said he
was a native, and on the strength of it got a job
with an old ’Groper. For 14 years Bill was looked
upon as one of the best, and finally decided to get
married. The annual grass-fed race-meeting came
round, and as beer was cheap and plentiful, Bill
got a bit over the odds, and confided in the girl s
brother that he “was no blanky ’Groper, but a
Sydneyite.” That was the end of Bill’s dream; in
fact, half the population of the district was after
his scalp,'for his cheek in trying to marry one of
“our gals.” Bill was terribly sorry when he got
better —the “gal” had property —but it was no go.
He was a t’othersider, even after 14 years, and that
was the end of it. He’s a “Combo” in th<* Nor’-
W'est now.
an opportune change.

“Gwy”: “Wonga” (B. G/4/’ll) remarks
that my idea of making occupiers destroy noxious
weeds is nothing new, and points out that,-, in N. S.
Wales, that power already lies with the shire coun-
cils. Right. But as soon as any proposal is made
to turn the power on, “friends” spring from all
quarters to the pest’s assistance. Even if it be
next to impossible to eradicate the hyacinth, as
many believe, a few hours’ work occasionally will
keep it in check, so that the river steamer can get
close enough in to take Cocky’s cream cans aboard;
and if the shire councils are too apathetic to do a
fair thing, or insist on occupiers doing it, the central
Government ought to clamber over the general feel-
ing of letting things slide, and hit the. Local Gov-
ernment Act with a suitable amendment.
“B. R. Chewer”: “Karee” (B. 16/3/’ll)
says he knew only one binghi to use a hanky. \Y ell,
I knew another, which makes two. My pfeller
turned up at Ravgnswood (Q.), as a witness for the
prosecution in a horse-stealing case, and nearly
drove the Court to fine itself for language by his
manipulation of an heirloom in the shape of what
had once been a white handkerchief. A specimen
of the proceedings:—
Bench: “What is your name?”
Witness (carefully using the heirloom): Robert
Bob.” , , ,
Bench (irritably): “Do you go to church? •
Witness (repeating previous performance) : Oil
er—yes. Hebben.”
Bench (exploding) : “For goodness sake, man, put
that confounded handkerchief out of sight.”
Witness (with a longer clean-up than before) :
“Oh, all right, Boss.”
In between wipes Robert Bob admitted that the
arresting constable had taught him his evidence.
Being thereupon told to stand down, he sat in front
of the magistrate, shining up his face-bulb till
the exasperated Bench threatened to give him “the
rising” for contempt of court.
* * * *
“Palumpas”: Re bush-hospitality, the
N.S.W. Cow-Couhtry is the coldest-hearted I ever
sampled. I doubt if even “Jimmie Pannikin,” who
is here somewhere on the Mighty Muddy Manning,
could induce the average butter-baroh of the North
Coast to emulate the spacious gracious hospitality
that is alleged to be a distinguishing characteristic of
the Outbackers. I recently went two miles out of my
boggy, slushy way to deliver an important telegram
to a Paspalum Prince, and thoughtfully struck the
comfortable homestead just as the family had started
dinner. Father came out, took the telegram, read it,
yarned to me for about 20 minutes; then-a son took
his place and talked cows and corn and pigs for
about six minutes of an inhospitable hour. I decided
to see the cow-country comedy through. Another son
relieved son No. 1, and discussed separators and milk-
testers for fully .78 of an hour. Then mother took
her turn in keeping me at bay till father and the
boys came out. “You’ll have to excuse us now,”
said father ; “we have work to do down in the corn.”
Said I: “Permit me to thank you for the pleasure of
your intellectual and interesting conversation, and to
say that I don’t want to be asked to dinner.” “Oh,
don’t mention it!”, said father. “Dinner’s over. So
long.”
“Dingo” : Had just absorbed the effusion of
“Bingo” (B. 9/3/’ll) re the absence of hydrophobia
ill England and France, when I discarded the red
rag and turned my attention to the Mourning
Herald of 22/3/’ll. There the following thrust itself
upon me:—
Paris, March 21. —Twelve persons have been bitten
by rabid dogs in Ushant. They have been sent to
the Pasteur Institute for treatment for hydrophobia.
So my vote goes every time to absolutely support
the Commonwealth Government in excluding mongrels
from overseas, whether it be France or Timbuctoo. I
vote to exclude the British mongrel, too, for that
matter.
* * * *
“Blue Bottle’s” new friend :
A few days ago I was walking bare-legged through
some tea-tree near Manly (N.S.W.), when I received
a shock similar to that caused by contact with the
famous “blue-bottle,” which at times infests the
ocean beaches, and whose greeting resembles that
of a much-magnified stinging nettle. A careful
search discovered my assailant, who measured luilj
half an inch over-all, and clung close to the leaves
ofthe tea-tree with which his color-scheme har-
monised. He was nicely painted in shades of green,
yellow, and brown; but the most remarkable thing
about him was that at each end of him there were
four low turrets, each mounting, 12 or 15 disappear-
ing guns, in the form of' minute spines. When
cleared for action he elevated these after the manner
of the clusters of thorns upon the fretful prickly-
pear, and it was with them that he dealt out
“stoush” to all intruders coming within range. I
have since had two or three encounters : with his
kind; and ‘as I have never, before this season, met
the invader, 1 anxiously await his identification.

“The Pariah”: In a Bulletin a while
back “T.J.M.” admired the cunning of Brer Fox in
selecting his residence in the neighborhood of the
jay, in order to profit by the warnings of that
watch-dog of the bush. Well, every man as he
finds it, and this is how I have lately found it.
With two others have been after “Skins” —rabbit,
hare, fox. Whenever we heard Sister Jay holding
an open-air meeting, „ we made for the noise, and it
was an even chance that we got a fox. My conclu-
sion, therefore, is that the fox derives at least as
, much harm as benefit from the jay, because the bird
makes more fuss over onp fox than over 20 men.
When Uncle Remus made the fox the goat among
animals, he knew a bit more than “T.J.M.”

“Oxo”: Whilst doing the rounds on tin'
selection, the other day, I noticed a small object-
dangling from the top barbed wire of a fence, and
paused to investigate. It was a bird —a white-
breasted chat—swinging in the breeze with the aid
of a length of horsehair twisted round its neck.
Dunno whether the mite had become, entangled in a
section ,of„ its building, establishment,, or had been
disappointed in love, and had committed suicide.
“ J.R.M.”: I am satisfied that “Agricola”
was right when he credited the magpie with surgical
skill in bandaging broken bones. Fired at a black
satin bird one day, and, as it turned,out, broke his
leg. Had another shot at him a few days later,
and bagged him. Found his broken limb ban
daged with black feathers. He was the only black
bird in a flock of 30.

Agram”: “Walt Iv. Harris” (B. 9/3/’ll),
who contends that the trees in Gippsland (Vic.)
are the tallest in Australia, says that the other day
wnen he was in Nana Glen (N.S.W.) he failed to
ind the champions which I had nominated. If he
had gone a mile and a half from the Glen P. 0., and
steered due east, he would have struck a tree on
Bucca Creek measuring exactly 96ft. in circum-
ference. It is an ironwood, and I’ll back it against
anything “W.K.11.” has up his sleeve. Photographs
have been taken of the vegetable, with two or three
families standing between the hips, and yet leaving
room for another family of 17. Hundreds of piles
some of them measuring 86ft., are being cut in the
district for the Queensland Government, and you
can calculate from that what sort of seedling we
rear.

“Yalgun”: Seen the tinerang yet? I have
—among the remnant of a Clarence River (N.S.W.)
tribe on the Orara. Billy cadges the raw material,
which consists of a strip of tin plate from the local
canning works; and, having twisted-the goods into
the required shape, he does the same old tricks with
it as he does with its wooden brother, the boomer-
ang.
* * * *
“Tiaki”: In my own experience of the
Bay of Islands (M.L.), in the old whaling days, I
met many instances of the Maoris capturing men
who were “wanted” by the police, and delivering
them, trussed like poultry, as lately described by
“Puketapu” (B. 16/3/’ll). Poor devils who de-
serted their ships, . looking for freedom and escape
from untold hardships, were placarded in Russell,
and a reward of £5 or £lO offered for their re-
turn, the rewards being afterwards deducted from
the escapees’ oil “shares.” In most cases the
runaways made inland, and were collared by the
Maoris, trussed up, swung on a manuka pole, and
delivered at the lock-up or ship’s side. It is only
just to say, however, that there were some real
“white” Maoris in the district, one Mange Hare, par-
ticularly, being a noted provider of sanctuary for
hunted sailors. On the other hand, there were
some dirty white men, who induced sailors to ab-
scond, and then betrayed them for a few pounds.
As for those runaways who got clear, what must
their sufferings have been! I came across a tree
on one lonely hill-side track, upon which was carved:
Henry Atkinson, Alabama, 1870. God have mercy
on me!” I reckon he never saw Alabama again.
* * * *
“Thoughtful Theo”: When I was travel-
ling per mail coach from Port Linepln to Fowler’s
Bay (S.A.) some years ago, we drew up alongside a
cocoa-box on a corner post in the scrub. The driver
inserted therein two letters and some Bulletins. He
then placed a stone on the box to prevent it blowing
away. Sometimes it did blow away, I learned, and
the settlers put in a joyous week tracking their, mail
to the edges of the horizon. Once a sundowner stole
this post-office (which nobody thought of nailing
down) for a tucker-box, but the neighborhood rose
enmasse and pursued him 25 miles, and brought the
box back. It never struck them to get a new post-
office. Further on we collided with a gunny-bag
swinging from a tree, and in this the mail-driver
dumped some more Bulletins and letters. I heard
later that a farmer collected a gohanna therefrom,
which had battened on the mail; so the district
clubbed together and bought a kerosene-tin, which
answered better. As we drove along, a man emerged
from the mallee and asked us to take a letter on to
near Glen Boree. He told the driver to just drop it
on the road—somebody’d find it and give it to the
addressee. At another place, where a few crows
lived hardily, the post-office was an ancient piece of
china with the handle off, and a board laid across it.
The thing that broke me right up, however, was a
plank which reclined against the remains of a wall.
On the plank was written in chalk this notice:—
Leev ther male under the boord on ther side of
ther wall where ther wind ain’t blowin’.
You might not believe it, but all this was before
the Labor Government took office!

Hank Morgan” : In regard to the slieep-
shearing championship, I contend that a match for
one hour or two hours may be a test of speed and
style, but is no test of the more important stamina
and endurance. Why not get a match going for a
solid week? One-third of the gate-money to be in-
vested in whisky, to be divided among all the other
shearers present. Such a carnival would help to
make History.

“The Jester”: I propose to give judgment
in the case of Carpet Snake v. General Public. 1
knew a scrub-cutter who was bitten by Carpet, and
suffered no ill-effects; also I saw a cow similarly
assaulted, and she died in an hour. The judgment
of the Court is that Carpet is sometimes harmless
and sometimes deadly. The only way to find out is
to let him bite you. If you die, it is almost certain
from the foregoing either that he was an offensive
beast or that you were a cow. Costs on the higher
scale.
* * * *
“Eryander”: The Westralian land-crab
(“Vigilans,” (B. 30/3/T1) seems to be identical
with the queer West Indian creature that lives in
large communities, often as far as four miles from
the sea. This long journey these enterprising
“crusties” make once a year in order to spawn in
the sea-sand. They never travel by daylight; but,
by the aid of what has been called the “magnetic
instinct,” they make a perfect “bee-line” through
the darkness. They live in holes, and come out to
forage only at night. If one manages to get a grip
—and it is a grip—bn your hand, he promptly de-
taches his pinching-implement at the shoulder, so
to speak; and, while the orphaned member hangs
mechanically on, giving you pepper enough to oc-
cupy all your attention, the owner skedaddles to
safety.
“Palum-Pas”: A recent par. referred to
the fearful fact that, in the Mighty and Magnificent
Manning district (that’s what the local yokels call
it), the Cow is Allah. Smart agents, who want to
sell separators, etc., to the .Cow Kings and the
Cream Countesses, make a point of wearing a spray
of Paspslum Dilatatum grass as a outtonhole bou-
quet, and of whistling “The Cows are in the Corn.”
It is not kind to sneer at prominent bank managers
who proudly lead prize Cows round the show-ring.
Such an honor is conferred only upon the Best Citi-
zens. No member of Tins Bulletin staff could ever
hope to gain such spendid social prestige. The most
successful agent used to wear two miniature Cow-
bells on his watch-chain; his scarf-pin was a small
model bail and milking-stool; lie invariably lifted
his hat to the championess Cows, though he gave the
Cow-man’s missus but a curt nod. When a Manning
River Butter-Baron wishes to flatter one of his
horses, he calls it a Cow. This term of endear-
ment has quite a different meaning in parts where
the Cow is not Allah. A local parson said recently
to the pupils of the Poddy Creek provisional school —
‘‘And now how can you repay your, parents for all
their love and tenderness?” A girl of eight replied
—“Please, sir, by milking as many Cows as we can,
an’ by feeding the poddies, an’ gittin’ up early! ”
Poor little devils 1 They rise summer and winter be-
fore 5 a.m.

“Walkabout” on Westralian agriculture :
A mixed (sheep and wheat) farm in W.A. must
consist of 1000 acres or more if the cocky is to do
well. Pick up a local paper any day, and you’ll
find it full of farms to let or sell, and one would
think the cockies were leaving the country in a
body. But they are not. The land fever is as bad
as ever; but most of them find their old holdings
are too small, and so they wan* to pass them on.
In some instances,
certainly, men go out
a long way to take up
more than they can
manage. They get the
limit from the Agri-
cultural Bank, and
want more; but as
the bank places restric-
tions on borrowings,
the usual practice is
then to go to a local
bank, get a bit extra,
pay off the Agricul-
tural Bank and go on
again. If the man has
tne luck to strike a
couple or so of good
seasons in succession,
he is in on his feet.
If he hasn’t that luck,
he is usually out on
his ear.
The best land in
the State is along the
Midlands Co.’s line.
At Three Springs last
season 46 bushels of
wheat and 48 of oats
were obtained to the
acre—not the same
acre.
In some of the
southern portions of
the State the small
cocky has a rough
time. It is either tod
much or too little
rain. The supply
wants regulating.
Machinery agentg
are as thick as bees,
and unless a cocky
knows his way about
he will find himself
loaded up with ma-
chinery before h e
knows where he is. I
know one man who
this season harvested
.56 acres. The mac-
hinery he used cost
hi m £275 in
I O U’s.
Some of the immi-
... , , grants who landed
with a bit of money are doing well; but, generally
speaking, they are poor farmers, and are slow in
adapting themselves to circumstances.
Agricultural laborers get from 30s. to £2 and
keep, and are scarce at that, as, if they can make a
start at all, they prefer to have a go at the land
on their own. The best of the land is, however, held
by the old hands, whose farms run to as much as
20,000 acres of freehold, some of it on river front-
ages. To get any kind of decent land now one must
go back 30 miles from the railway. Agricultural
lines are spreading everywhere; but some of the
settlers can never hope to get their crop to market
without crossing 20 miles of sand plain. Generally
speaking 1 , if it was not for the return from the
sheep, many of the small men could not live.
In the southern part of the State there is good
potato land, but it costs £2O an acre to clear it.

Trade Mark Case.
A warning.
It having come under our notice that various
substitutes are sometimes sold and passed off
as 0.T., notice is hereby given that this practice
is a breach of the Trade Marks Act, and con-
sequently illegal, as O.T. is a Registered Trade
Name, and applies only to the beverage made by
this Company.
After this date all persons who so continue to
pass off other drinks as O.T. will render them-
selves liable to proceedings under the “Common-
wealth Trades Marks Act.”
O.T. LIMITED,
F. Malvin Quinn,
March 1, 1911. Acting Managing Director.

with him. But John firmly avers that he will
not return from England with anything in the

HIS STAR TURN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In urging the rejection of the referendum proposals
Mr. Deakin said :“ We have a {treat Australia. Do not
let us leave it peopled by little Australians. "—Beautiful
wriggle reported recently.
List to the great contortionist,
And heed what he affirms
Who stands upon his head to twist
The truth about like worms:
“ Since two and two make five (or more),
Since snow is black as coal,
And since the part (as Euclid swore)
Is greater than the whole—
“ It follows, as the horse the cart,
Or head below the heels,
The finish ere the race’s start,
Or hunger after meals,
“As clear as mud, as short as long,
As sure as black is white—
That right-way-up is always wrong,
And wrong-way-up is right ;
“ Hence, as the single brick (you twig?)
Is larger than the wall.
Each tiny State is huge and big,
And big Australia, small! ”
His stupent audience cheer him high,
This dazzling star, forsooth,
Who cannot tell a straight-out lie
And scarce a straight-out truth :
The weirdest, wildest human freak
That ever did a twist!
" Three cheers for boneless Alf! ” they shriek,
“ The Great Contortionist! ”
VS.W.

Ihe following simple prescription, written by a

practice as a specialist in-all forms of rheumatism,
lumbago, sciatica and kidney and bladder troubles,
will be found to afford immediate relief, and, if
persisted in for a short time, will completely cure
tiiese troublesome ailments.
The Prescription.
Tincture Gentian Compound, one ounce; Syrup
Rhubarb Aromatic, one ounce ; Liquid Barkola
Compound, one ounce; Syrup of Ginger, five
ounces.
The dose for adults is one teaspoonful taken
after meals, and one at bedtime. Children should
be given one-fourth to one-half teaspoonful, after
meals only.
We are glad to publish this prescription, as we
know it will help our afflicted readers who give it
a fair trial. If taken continuously for a few weeks
it will be found to be an almost absolutely certain
cure for rheumatism, kidney and bladder trouble,
backache and all allied complaints.*

John Hancock, the early Victorian Labor
member, once jokingly said that he had been
offered a title, but had declined. “For myself/’
he said, “I do not mind. But, if I died, Mrs,
Hancock could not possibly take in washing as
Lady Hancock.” '

two well-known fathers had a nonsensical argu-

and called upon “J.m” Anderson, K.C., to
