Died the other day, in Melbourne, William
Meudell, a former general-manager of the Bank
of Victoria, and father of various good Australians
—including George, now making his name as a
writer on the London financial press. Meudell,
sen., was a goldfields banker in the early days,
and then and in all his subsequent life trained
himself to keep his head cool; so, in the light of
subsequent events, it wasn’t atall surprising when
in 1891 he quitted Victorian banking, and would
have no more to do with it. Two years later came
the smash.
The finest collection of Nelson relics in
Australia is owned by Mrs. Wright, widow of Dr.
Horatio G. A. Wright, an old-fashioned physician
who practised for many years in Wynyard Square,
Sydney, and was a nephew of the famous admiral.
Dr. Wright’s practice was chiefly obstetric, and
for many years of his life he had so much to do
that he had little time to spend in bed, and got
most of his sleep in his fincient brougham as lie
went round to see people into this world.
Mrs. C. E. Williams, who died at Ballarat
a few days ago, lived for many years on Aus-
tralia’s only battlefield. In the ’so’s she and her
husband were camped in a tent at Eureka. For
some time previous to the actual hostilities Petbr
Lalor’s men did a good deal of pistol practice and
cleaning; wherefore, as a precaution, Mr. Wil-
liams built round his tent an embankment of
flour-bags filled with earth. Rummaging one
day, he lifted down a bag from the side of the
bed and found two bullets embedded in it—which
seemed to suggest that the precaution was neces-
sary. Mr. Williams was the first schoolmaster at
Eureka. Colonel Williams, Ballarat’s city clerk,
and formerly editor of the local Courier, is one
of his children.
S. Australian sheep-barbers have an idea
that, on records, Ewan Cameron is the nearest
approach to a shearing champion Australia has
had. Cameron (“ The Long Fellow ”), who came
from Myponga, got among the “ guns ” by
defeating Mclnnes at Bucldand Park in the latter
: 70’s, and thenceforward never held second blade
in any shed until he retired in 1905. His best
eight-hour tally was 285 fair sheep. Cameron was
pressed to be the Labor candidate for King
O’Malley’s old seat when the Sovereign left the
State House; but he stood out. He even stood
out after he had been gazetted a J.P., and wrote
in to the Government to say that he reckoned
there was enough of the breed already.
Maoriland has decided that it will be able
to worry through its geological survey work with-
out the aid of an imported director in succession
to Dr. Bell, who resigned recently ; and Percy G.
Morgan, formerly director of the School of Minesj
Waihi, and latterly in the Geological Survey
Department, has been placed in charge. Being
an M. A., a memberof the Australasian Institute of
Mining Engineers, a member of the Australasian
Association for the Advancement of Science, a
certificated land and mining surveyor, a battery
superintendent, and a certificated assayer of gold,
he seems to have some qualifications for the job.
Thomas Britt, who in 12 years in the ’so’s
and ’6o’s won nearly £lOO,OOO worth of gold from
his claim on the Huntly lead, Bendigo, died a few
days ago. He came to Victoria as engineer for
the Melbourne Argus, but after being there a
year the diggings called him, and he struck it
rich. He possessed some interesting inky-way
souvenirs. One of them was a copy of the Mel-
bourne Advertiser for January 3, 1838, pub-
lished in MS. by John Pascoe Fawkner. He had
also a copy of the Argus printed in gold on satin
—one of six issued when Sir Charles Hotham
arrived in 1854 to take over the Governorship.
Another curio was a copy of an address of wel-
come to Hotham. It was printed on a lorry while
the welcoming procession marched through Mel-
bourne and was distributed broadcast.

Melbourne. Bunny has become moye consciously
French than any other Australian painter. He
has been a constant exhibitor in the Paris Salon
since 1886, and one of his canvases, “Aprds Le
Bain,” was bought by the French Government.for
the Luxembourg.

The South Au.s. Government, which is
considering the question of appointing an extra
Judge, announces that, if an appointment is
made, one of the terms shall be that, Before he
draws his monthly salary, the new man shall
certify that no matter in his Court has been hung
up for 'decision for more than 90 days. The curi-
ous condition is the result of the Chief Justice’s
vast capacity for brooding over his judgments.
Btill, this can be said for them when he does
deliver them—the Oldest Inhabitant can’t re-
member when one was upset on appeal.
Australia lost a good Protectionist and
Molong (N.S.W.) a good citizen, the other day,
by the death of John Black, the miller. Black,
who was a native of Ayrshire; (Scotland), claimed
to be a'descendant of Roderick Dhu. He came
into Australia in the golden ’s9’s, and after a
brief spell at milling, went mining on the Turon,
but made more money out of a battery. With
this capital he bought a flour mill at O’Connell
Plains, near Bathurst, and in 1877 founded another
at Molong, where he settled. When he died the
town went to the funeral as unanimously as
though it had lost a father.
Richard Rendle Holter, of Williamstown
(Vic.), an old seafarer, who sailed into port a few
days ago, was a survivor of one of the earliest and
most sensational wrecks on the Queensland coast.
The ship was the .Jenny Lind ; the year, 1850 ;
and the cause, Kent’s Reef. He and others lived
precariously on a rock for six weeks, during
which time they built a boat, and, after many
perils, reached the mainland. For the last 49
years he had held a Victorian Government billet
in connection (vith the lightering of explosives at
the Outer Anchorage arid Altona Bay.
The proposal of Victoria’s Minister for
Agriculture Graham that, instead of Federal
Ministers forwarding a stray “ fiver ” to help the
harvester trade strikers, they should give a
month s salary each—which would amount to
close on £l4oo—recalls the action of Chief Justice
Higinbotham during the maritime strike of 1891.
Higinbotham stpod almost alone amongst men
holding big public positions in justifying the
strikers, and made no bones about showing it in
the letter which has since, become historical :
The Chief Justice presents his compliments to the pre-
sident of the Trades Hall Council, and requests that he
will be so good as to place the amount of the enclosed
cheque of £5O to the credit of the strike flind. While the
united trades are awaiting compliance with their reason-
able request for a conference with the employers, the
Chief Justice will continue for the present to forward a
weekly contribution of £lO to the same object.
And he did, for 15 weeks.

The departure of Australasian Premiers to
the Coronation junket recalls an untold story of
Richard Seddon. Soon after he returned to
M.L. from high falutin’ at Queen Vic.’s diamond
Jubilee he paid a visit to Palmerston North,
where he was the guest of a local cattle magnate.
At the homestead he was invited by his host to a
glass of Dry Monopole, but expressed a preference
for a whisky and soda. “Do you always stick to
whisky and soda?” inquired the cow king.
“Well,” replied Seddon, “it’s this way. I pro-
posed returning to Maoriland by way of the Suez
Canal, and consulted the commodore doctor of
the line I was to travel by as to the dangers of
excessive heat on that route, I asked the doctor
whether it would be safe to drihk anything.
‘ What do you generally take?’ he ihtjuired ; and
I answered; ‘Whisky and soda.’ ‘All I can
say, then,’ quoth the doctor, ‘is that on the last
voyage from Australia we had five deaths from
excessive heat, and four of them were lemon-
squashers.’ That has been a solemn warning to
me ever since.”

“ De Yid ” : The Bulletin Christianised
Ikey Jonas into “I. Jones” the other day
(30/3/H). Sim Cohen and Ike Jonas, the great
Ballarat restaurant and hotel rivals in the boom
times, were brothers in-law, and the business feud
spread into their private lives. Cohen, who had
made his money cheap jacking up country, started
business in the dilapidated hole known as the
“ Rats’ Castle.” He made a big fortune, but lost
most of it m speculation. As well known as
Cohep in those days was his henchman, Ike
Michaels. I met Michaels the other week, and
thought of ringing for an ambulance when he
mentioned, as frequenters of the Grand, “’Lijah
the Prophet.” “The Angel of Death,” and
“ Churchy-Half-a-Finger.” Likewise Bill Bailey
—Bill being a well-known mining man some 30
years back. The others were convivial spirits of
the same generation. They don’t seem to invent
nicknames like Churchy Half-a-Pinger ” now-
adays !

This infant among Australian banks —it was born
in 1888, and there has been no increase in the
family since—is putting on flesh very fast. Its ad-
vances have increased in the past 12 months by no
less than £437,834, which means that they arc
now more than one-third greater than they were a
year ago. Under ordinary circumstances this very
rapid growth would create some suspicion. Of
course most people try to convince themselves that
these prosperous times are going to last. In fact,
there are plenty who say that these times are not
really prosperous compared with those we are going
to have. This paper, however, declines to take the
view that the country won’t sulfer from any more
set-backs. There is going to be a pretty general
drought some day, and it won’t be any the less a
■drought if there are twice as many farmers as there
were 20 years previously. Also, the time will in-
evitably come when the States will have to either
leave off borrowing altogether or borrow most spar-
ingly ; and that will mean the stoppage of the vast
construction works which are now absorbing tons
of thousands of men. The value put upon land is to
a great extent governed by the return which it is
yielding. It won’t always yield the return it has
done during the past few years of fat harvests and
fat prices. It is as certain, then, as anything can
be, that the pendulum will swing back, though this
paper isn’t prepared to say how far it will swing.
For that reason, when a bank has made the bulk of
its advances during the last year or two, one must
look upon it as having probably made those advances
on a basis of inflated values. In this case of the
Royal, however, the position isn’t quite as suspicious
as it would be ordinarily, because this bank lus
lately opened in Sydney, where no doubt it had
friends ready to transfer their business from other
banks.
The large increase of advances already noted has
put no strain upon the bank. This shows the prin-
cipal figures:—
March, March,
1910. 1911.
Notes in circulation .. £15,877 £18,917 Inc. £3,040
Bills in circulation, etc. 96,333 157,417 Inc. 61,084
Deposits at interest ..1,007,571 1,223,299 Inc. 215,728
Free deposits .. .. 536.320 711,291 Inc. 174,971.
I.iquidassets .. .. 8<>4.721i 841.998 Inc. 37,269
Advances .. .. 1,232,904 1,670,738 Inc. 437,834
Premises, etc. ... .. 64,088 63,545 Dec. 543
Money has thus come in faster than it has gone out,
so that the liquid assets represent just upon 40 p.c.
of the total liabilities —a most substantial propor-
tion. Not only is this an effective buffer against
any ordinary shock, but it puts the bank in a good
position to take new business. Further, notwith-
standing that the deposits have come in so well, the
bank is still by no means overloaded with public
money in comparison with its own funds. For one
thing, it has kept its dividends modest; and has laid
by large reserves ; for another, it lately increased its
capital and asked for very fair premiums on the
shares. Thus, between one thing and another, the
liabilities of all kinds total only £4 13s. for every
£ 1 of shareholders’ funds—a proportion which is
believed to be very well on the safe side.
Comparing them with capital and reserves com-
bined, the profits are very good indeed: —
Bept., 1907.. £lO 10 10 p.c. Sept., 1909.. £8 11 4 p.c.
Mar., 1908.. 9 9 9 „ Mar., 1910.. 813 9 „
Sept., 1908,. 10 1 8 ~ Sept., 1910.. 813 3 ~
Mar., 1909.. 9 311 ~ Mar., 1911.. 9 5 2 „
A good profit return of this kind doesn’t necessarily
mean sound business. Just before the smash of the
Melbourne boom, , banks were showing 12 p.c. on
their funds; it simply meant that, the securities
being doubtful, or the borrowers being reckless or
unduly optimistic, a very high rate of interest was
charged. Unfortunately in such cases the usual plan
is to add the interest on to the principal; and when
there was a slump of the asset which was supposed to
guarantee the principal, the interest vanished. So
the profit was not really earned, and the profit-earn-
ings were bogus. Profit earned is not, therefore, an
infallible test. On the other hand, under normal
conditions, a high rate of earning 3, calculated on
capital and reserves combined, ought to mean that
the capital and reserves are real —possibly even that
the assets are worth more than book-value, the sur-
plus constituting an undisclosed reserve. All that
can be said is that, on the figures, this bank’s posi-
tion and prospects are exceedingly good.

This society is just about out of the old wood;
whether it is getting into a new one time only will
tell. But, unless there is shocking misgovernment,
the new forest —if there is a new one—-won’t be as
thickly populated with devils as the old one. The
only thing that can do much damage to a decentiy-
managed building society is a sudden slump in pros-
perity and values. Now, even a bad season won’t
produce a sudden slump. But a sudden stoppage of
public and private borrowing, coupled with the
wholesale withdrawal of private deposits, will do it.
When several banks are called upon to repay large
sums quickly, and when their own tills are not
strong enough to meet the strain, the only thing
they can do is to realise quickly; and that
means a smash of values. Then the building
society suffers. The stoppage of public and private
borrowing I:brows thousands out of work ; their dis-
missal affects others; borrowers can’t keep up their
payments; and when the assets are thrown on the
society s hands, the dread of more forced sales only
demoralises the market worse than ever. The Bul-
letin doesn’t say, and never has said, that nobody
can make money by borrowing; on the contrary, it
has said that sometimes it is a case of Borrow or
Burst. But the borrowing habit is like the drink
habit- it grows, and the worse it grows the more
disastrous are likely to be the results of a sudden
stoppage. Yet in nine cases out of 10 there has to
be a sudden stoppage, for the simple reason that no-
body will lend.
During this society’s last year £42,605 was ad-
vanced, as against £17,363 in 1906. “On the other
hand, owing to the increased prosperity of the
people, the repayments have been correspondingly
larger, consequently the amount due by borrowers
has only increased from £137,949 in 1910 to
£139,395 in 1911.” Properties in possession,
though they are lumped with “Borrowers” in the
accounts, are said by the directors to have been re-
duced to £2957. No doubt this was the weak spot
in the co.’s affairs; and the directors have made a
splendid clearance. But that means that property
values have risen to something like their old level :
ana, as new loans have no doubt been made on this
basis, there is plainly some risk of a new crop of
losses, through another slump. Still, on present
appearances, there won’t be a sudden slump. Gov-
ernments certainly are borrowing extravagantly; but
the banks, even if they have been taking money,
have been keeping very large sums in hand; and so,
unless something quite unexpected happens, there
won’t be any violent rush to realise properties. Be-
sides, there has been no pouring of foreign deposit
money into Australia—and it was that foreign
money which caused the trouble in the ’9o’s. There
may, then, be a set-back in values, but it is not
likely that there will be a sudden and severe one;
consequently such a carefully-managed society as
this should come to no harm. As for the depositors,
they are most abundantly protected. Total liabili-
ties—mainly deposits—are £74,686, and the paid
capital is £72,857 ; so, with reserves, the share-
holders have more in the business than the deposi-
tors, etc., have; which means that the depositors
and other creditors would be safe even if some ap-
palling disaster knocked off 50 p.c. of the book-
value of the assets.

ihis is a remarkable jump. But, as has been often
observed here, there have been various reasons why
Australian brewing cos. should put a little by.
Right months ago, for instance, it wasn’t at all cer-
tain how the Prohibition vote would go in N. S.
M ales. The anti-beer party might have won; and
if it had, bang* would have gone considerably mere
than sixpence of this co.’s assets, because the assets
consist largely of hotel properties, for the goodwill
of which, no doubt, as much has been paid in many
cases as for the premises. That is to say, the
brewery co. has no doubt often paid as much for the
right to work the beer-pump as for the visible pro-
perty; and the right to work the pump depends en-
tirely upon an uncertain vote of the people. More-
over, it looked as though the inclination of the
people was to vote the beer-pump down. The anti-
beer party, however, failed badly ; hence the manage-
ment of this co. doubtless feels justified now in put-
ting by less to provide for contingencies' than it had
been doing previously. All the' same, though the
last test was all against the no-beer party, the
brewery and pub investor who thinks that that settles
matter is, liable to be badly surprised presently.
Ihe Judkins party collapsed so badly, simply because
it campaigned so badly. If it had been satisfied to
go for reduction, it might have won. It may have
sense enough to do that some other time. If it does,
and wins, a few hundred more pubs may be wiped
out ; and with them will go half or two-thirds of
’heir value to the brewery co. or other landlord.
?or the pub that will go first is the pub that has
feast value except as a shed to shelter, the beer-pump,
and the tinted Venus who works it,

The directors make no mention of a dividend for
the second half of the past year; but in the fore-
going it has been assumed that the usual will be
paid. In the days when Peter Perkins was Queens-
land’s Jonah, this co. did a big business, and had
its hand on many a beer-pump and the premises
around it. When the boom blew up, the co. was
knocked into the soup, and it looked at one time as
though it was hardly worth trying to get it out.
After an attempt to square things by writing down
the capital from £162,500 to £87,500, the at-
tempt at salvage was, in fact, abandoned, and a new
co. was formed with a capital of £58,333. Then
the tide turned. Queensland had never lost its
mouth, though for a time it hadn’t the means to
often wet it; but soon the wetting began again in
earnest. The co.’s position improved so fast that
£41,667 of reserves were transferred to capital;
and on the total capital of £lOO,OOO thus built up,
the recent dividend is, of course, at the rate of 20
p.c. Even on the old capital of £162,500 it would
be equal to over 12 p.c.; so the recovery can be put
down as very thorough indeed. Nevertheless, it will
be noticed that, though Queensland has made much
progress during the past six years, the profits are
not as good now as they were even in 1903, which
was a droughty year. Wages and the cost of the
things whereof beer is made have moved up ; but a
part of the shrinkage in profit is due to the fact
that rival breweries got new supplies of brains and
energy, and have in some directions been pinching
Perkins’s hard. “The net profit, you will see,” says
the directors’ report, “is somewhat less than last
year, but that is due to our being forced to reduce
the price of beer from November 31 last on account
of the unbusincss-like tactics of one of the other
breweries.” It tastes like trouble.
Still there is a big field, and a dry one ; and the
co. is in a strong position. This shows the move-
ment in some important respects in 10 years:—

These accounts gibber at you like a skeleton. The
Profit and Loss statement consists praeticallv of two
items —plain, bald, “Receipts” on one side, and
plain, bald “Expenses, including- depreciatioh,” on
the other. There isn’t a hint as to what the de-
preciation amounts to. Too much may be written
off, or too little. If too much, then the net profit
is of course under-stated. This ■ paper knows no
reason, however, why the profit should be under-
stated. Yet, if it is not under-stated, the 5 p.c.
dividend for the half-year should most certainly not
have been paid. As a matter of fact it absorbs not
only all the half-year’s profit, and the profit for the
previous half-year, but all the surplus profit: for the
previous 18 months, and the premiums received on
shares as well. The policy fills this critic with
distrust. The co.’s liabilities, mostlv on overdraft
and mortgage, amount to £16,906; ‘but the credi-
tors don’t worry—they are no doubt well protected,
if not by the ferrv property ( £46,288), then by the
freeholds, etc. (£34,684), which the co. has ac-
quired about the harbor, mainly for picnic purposes.
Apparently they are a mighty poor investment, so
far, anyhow, as a speedy return is concerned.

‘Subject to note hereunder.
The trouble, or, rather, one of the troubles of this
co-operative co. was that the co-operators wouldn’t
co-operate. Consequently, after much discussion, a
new rule was passed, and came into force in August,
1909, providing for a penalty of £1 on the share-
holder who failed to spend £l2 per annum in pur-
chases with the co. In the first 12 months tnese
fines, or forfeitures of capital, amounted to £4062,
or about l-15th of the total ; and this amount has
been written off to cover depreciation. For the past
six months it is calculated that the total forfeitures
will be and this amount will also be written
off in the same way. The trading profit for the half-
year goes into the same sink. Tnese heavy writings-
off now are the result of the failure to provide for de-
preciation when the clouds hung very heavy over the
co. Not that they have lifted altogether. Thus —
“The Board feels that the position of the stores,
distant as they are from the retail trade centre, is a
serious impediment”; and it is thought that things
might be better “if the business were removed to
premises occupying a more central position.” So
the co. is to shift, and try to let or sell the Flinders-
street premises, which it should never have acquired
- —at any rate, when it did. The co. paid £10,750
for jthe land and £17,622 for the building, besides
£1765 for premises in the Lane, and this at a time
when the whole capital could well have been em-
ployed in the business. The liability with which the
co. loaded itself scared many shareholders; for the
premises, were far beyond the requirements of the co.
ilk the co. can get out of the place now, without
more than a nominal loss—and in view of the appre-
ciatton of land values it may be able to get out at
no loss at all—the co. should sell, rid itself as far
as possible of debt, and make a fresh start on the
more Vmodest scale which befits its infancy. The
present debts amount to £46,917, incurred for this
Flinders-street property, for the bakery (£22,812),
and.- on account of sundry trading purchases. The
bakery, which met with so many unexpected diffi-
culties that it also appears to have been an ill-
considered outbreak, is now doing better. A working
profit of £605 was shown for the half-year; but for
some reason or other interest is charged against this
•department, and only the balance is transferred to
• Profit arid. Loss. This,, apparently, means one of two
things: either that the total amount payable by the
co. for interest is not disclosed by the Profit anti
Loss account, or interest is dqbited to the bakery
account and credited on the other hand to the other
part of the business. 1 It is not a satisfactory way of
doing things. The net result seems to be to put' the
general stores business in too favorable a light.
In a note to the tahle printed on top of these re-
marks, the profit, etc.) for the past' few .half-years
is stated with a reservation. In the Appropriation
account, which you will find at tne back of the
balance-sheet, there is this astonishing item —“Error
ill sundry creditors, July. 24, 1909, and January 22,
1910, £848.” There is not a word abo’ut this in
the directors’ report; on the contrary, \it says—
“ The net profit is £935, and has been applied to
depreciation.” As a matter of fact, it now appeals
thit, instead of the loss for the half-year ending
July,, 1909, being £3712) it was £B4B more than
that; and the error has had to be made good out of
the past; year’s profits; so that instead of £1291
being' written off for depreciation, only £443 has
actually been available. Thu Bulletin hopes that
the mqddling to which the co.’s business has been
subjected, is now at an end. Co-operation can’t
stand: having heaped on its chest, many such monu-
ments of ineptitude as the institution has supplied.
*. * * *
The N.S.W. Labor Government is going
I? introduce various financial, reforms. It. is going
to establish a sinking fund to extinguish the public
debt in 60 years or so ; it is trying to amalgamate
the two savings banks; it proposes to follow S. Aus.
and Victoria, and make State Bank advances to
people''Who want to secure homes of their own ; and
it thinks that when public works: show a profit, that
profit should go in reduction of the . debt owing on
them. These are all very excellent proposals and
principles. But while the Government is relieving
the load of debt-at the tap, it is increasing it at
a tremendous rate at the bung. Acting-Premier
Holman says he doesn’t believe in the no-borrowing
fad.” Probably no man in Australia is, however,
less qualified to express an opinion about finance
than he is. It is a subject which, he hasn’t the
temperament to master. Nevertheless, Holman ought
to be the most strenuous anti-borrower. He is a
Socialist; and Socialism means the ownership of
the instruments of production and distribution. Of
course Holman may say that he owns a coat if he
has merely ' borrowed it, or the, money to buy it;
but when the lender calls for the coat, Holman
may find reason to change his opinion. Also, he
may find himself dreadfully cold.
Some of the tens of thousands who pay
to go to the Sydney Easter Show wohder, no doubt,
what becomes of all the money. The Agricultural
Society of N.S.W. has just issued its accounts for
1910, and the document tells them. The revenue
for the year, was. £23,965, of which : £18,052 came
from Show receipts (gates, booths, bars, entry fees,
etc.). The rest was from members’ subscriptions,
Government subsidy (£1000), and ground rents,
etc. Prizes,ran to £4640 (cash, £4538 ; ribbons,
£59) medals, £43) ; exhibition exes, (gate-men,
bands, luncheons for officials, etc.), £4961. Then
there are the permanent staff (£2974), rates and
taxes, maintenance, insurance, etc.) £9182 was
spent on improvements; and there still remained
£1507 to put in the stocking—-from which it will
no doubt be taken to pay for still more permanent
improvements.

THE SQUOZEN RACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Victorian Treasurer Watt complains that Aus-
tralian State Governments are viciously “squeezed”
every time they go to England for a loan, or with a
conversion scheme. He wants the Premiers at the
Coronation to put their heads together to devise
some means of dodging the “squeeze.”
What time we venture over seas.
Ma country greets us with a squeeze.
The,chest she hugs us to is cold
And hard—in fact, a chest of gold.
So we are taught our proper place.
The Chosen? Nay, the Squozen race!
VICTORIA.
S.S.

Kyloe (N.S.W.) doesn’t talk too cheer-
fully about the flotation process, which some people
have been relying upon to get the co. out of its
troubles arising from long road carriage and poor
fuel supplies. “On account of the small percentage
of mineral contained in this ore, as compared with
that previously treated by the flotation process,
several modifications in the machine have been found
necessary.” It sounds as though the ore is too poor
to pay unless the plant can be induced to digest
more of it. #
C S A., near Cobar, has been run very
quietly for some time past. It was thought once
that Great Cobar would purchase the show because
of the fairly large blocks of basic ore developed;
but nothing came of the talk. Just at present tne
south-east crosscut from No. 3 level is facing very
solid basic ore. Prospects also show improvement
in the south drive from No. 5 crosscut, the face
showing 8 p.c. ore. A winze is down 26ft. on this
ore body, and the ground is becoming softer and
more favorable for a make of ore on the footwall.
So far nothing very big has been found, but there
is still a sporting chance of a valuable discovery.

Doolette’s refusal to acquire the optioned
Corinthian property (W.A.) is a clear indication
that London isn’t having any more Yilgarn side-shows
until it sees how Bullfinch shapes. The sale price
was fixed at £25,000 cash; and as Doolette is part-
owner, and has already spent £lOOO of his own
money in getting the property ready for the market,
its purchase and subsequent flotation, either singly
or in conjunction with the Oroya Exploration Co.’s
lease, was considered a certainty.
“Oof Bird” (W.A.) : There is a big fly
somewhere in Bullfinch’s /ointment. My reasons for
asserting this are—(l) Failure of co. to sink on the
rich ore below 100 ft., after the London Financial
Times's official announcement that orders had been
“sent put” to do this work ; (2) tne uncontradicted
statement that in stoping above 100 ft. the ore tapers
away, and that this is the real reason for the discon-
tinuance of ore treatment at Kalgoorlie; (3) the
fact that, in spite of a big depreciation in market
valuation, no independent mining engineer has been
called in to give . shareholders his view of things.
Only the other dav Pierre Ledo- x went below on be-
half of a few privileged London, Paris and Brussels
speculators, but before being allowed to do so he
had to give his word that he would not pass on his
impressions to the papers. In other words, Ledoux
and his friends were permitted to have a look in to
the detriment of the general body of shareholders.
Another thing which suggests that there is more be-
neath than appears on the surface is the report that
certain London operators have sold shares freely in
Adelaide, and at , the same time have cancelled the
numbers of the sold share certificates on the London
register, and cabled those figures to ■ enable prompt
delivery of new scrip to be made by the Adelaide
office. What foundation there is for. this report re-
mains to be seen; but one thing is certain -tens
of thousands of shares have lately come out irom
London, and in a greased-ligbtning sort of way, too.
So much of this stock was bought at between 555.
and 655. that it. is no wonder a howl of dissatis-
faction has been raised in the West over D. L. Doo-
lette’s naive statement that the “market value of the
property is still in the mine.” It is poor satisfaction
to the reckless gamblers who got in at the top of
the market at the time Doolette and his friends were
puffing the mine and alleging that there was half a
million sterling or more visible to the naked eye.
Randolph Bedford: Bulletin (G/4/’ll)
quotes Gerard Williams, the Times person who
showed his knowledge of Westralian mining by
talking of jewellers’ shops in buck quartz, like the
Wealth of Nations and Londonderry, in the same
breath as Bullfinch, and who cud his best to turn
down Elliotts at 30s. The Bullfinch did not show
more than lOdwt. samples at surface, except im-
mediately west of No. 1. and there the iron con-
cealed the gold in the dish and made the ounces
look like dwts. No. 4 shaft was from poverty at
the surface to Bdwt. at 52ft., when the 6oz. ore came
in. Williams is one of those wise young persons
whose wisdpm is after the event. He considers there
should be no delay in sinking existing shafts. A
main shaft is going down as quickly as it can be
sunk —after a delay for winding gear —and is down
120 ft. If Williams’s idea of mining is to keep on
sinking in hard lode instead of in soft country, this
only proves that Williams is Williams. When did
these indiscriminate negatives of professional pessi-
mism make a field? But for the optimist most Aus-
tralian mining would be down and out to-day. Simi-
larly Williams, who took most of his tale from one
of the defendants in the Chaffinch case, says of the
Chaffinch a month or more after the mullocky leader
had cut out, “Nor, indeed, did I see anything which
had been exposed that appeared even likely to carry
gold.” Quartz never docs, does it? “I have ex-
amined specimens which came from the trench, and
the gold is very similar to the gold in similar horn-
blendic schists in the N.E. Coolgardie goldfield.
Where did Williams see specimens similar to C.ia.-
finch in situ? His knowledge of N.E. Coolgardie is
so recent that his experience of the oxidised ores ot
Kalgoorlie must surely have been gained in a museum.
“If any other such pocket be found in the same
rock, I shall begin to believe that the age of miracles
is not yet dead.” This prattler’s veiled adherence to
the charge of salting has been answered by expert
evidence that found hornblendic schist on the Oha -
finch after the prosecution had been taken, and
that identified that schist with the rich speci-
mens from the trench, and by the statement ot
Magistrate Roe, that no man could hear the evidence
and not be sure that the gold was present by any but
natural means. # *
Vivien (W.A.) lias given it up. Ore re-
serves petered out in December last, and as the co,
was not game enough to spend any more money, the
mine was shut down. Even tributors could not be
expected to work the show, so shareholders met in
London and decided to wind up. It is expected that
after the plant and other mine assets have been rea-
lised, and the proceeds lumped with the small amount
of cash in hand, the liquidator will be able to declare
a baby dividend.
Boulder No. 1 Co. (W.A.), through its
London secretary, denies that it is in need of further
working capital, and reckons that all the needful
funds can be got out of crushing?. Since the dis-
claimer was made, assays have dwindled somewhat;
and as there was barely enough cash in hand to make
things meet at the beginning of the year, the co. may
have to eat its words before Jong.
London, Australian and General Explora-
tion, Ltd., announces that the options on the co.’s
shares, expiring on June 30 next, have been extended
for another year, upon the condition that if at any
time after the first-named date the make-up price be
not less than 7s. 6d. for two consecutive accounts
on the London Stock Exchange, the options then
outstanding must be exercised on the terms stated
in the option Certificates within seven days of the
second settling day, or such options will be con-
sidered abandoned and at an end.

Cathcart, Ararat (Vic.), chirrups a lialf-
yearlv roundelay in good tone, and George Bryant
isn’t' afraid to say that, despite the last attack of
market indigestion, the patient has more than a
dead man’s chance. The mine hasn’t back-slid any.
When 3300 fathoms pan out 64730 z., the average re-
calls the best days of alluvial mining, and fills the
soul with hope as to what will happen when the Cen-
tral and the New Langi Logan get a hustle on. Away
down the lead the gutter is 200 ft. wide, instead of
the 120 ft. that it was expected to be. Off No. 4
north branch, where poor wash was said to be show-
ing in each face, good dirt has been opened on the
high ground. Its good quality is shown by 26
fathoms yielding 73%0z. Wonderful are the ins-
and-outs of deep-lead mining! Off the mam level,
too, things are looking better ; but here the trouble
ahead is the way the gutter is dipping towards the
level, bringing nearer than ever the necessity for
a No. 2 shaft. Up the lead the Heather Bell tribu-
tary seems to have shifted into the right horizon,
and a run of wash 100 ft. wide is proved. It is
patchv, but believed to be payable. The current
half-vear is being begun with 2177 fathoms opened
up, but at least half of that is green. _ As 3300
fathoms were treated last half-year, the mind is four
months ahead of the panelling line. This is a narrow
margin, but with George Bryant in command, to
weather storms, shareholders will sleep soundly.
The mine, so far, has eaten up £15,000 of contri-
buted capital, produced £116,953 of gold, and paid
£33,000 in divs.
Another Victorian deep lead show is out
with figures. This is the Duke and Main Leads,
Maryborough, which, since it started divs., hasn’t
had a break; £38,750 has gone to shareholders on
a paid-up capital of £01,458. Its gold output has
been £200,000. The big difference between it and
the Cathcart is that the Duke handles a big main
gutter at a deep level, while the Cathcart is min-
ing a tributary in comparatively shallow ground.
The first-named, too, has few slugs; the Cathcart has
many. The two claims can be contrasted: —
Costs per
Fathoms. Gold. Divs. Fathom.
Duke and M.L. .. 10,678 £24,878 £7,500 34/4
Cathcart.. .. 3,300 25,744 13,188 48/6
The average of the gold per fathom in the Mary-
borough mine was £2 6s. 4jd. ; costs were 345. 4d.,
and it was able to pay six divs. of 6d. each during
the half-year. Unfortunately the grade of its wash
has dropped to llidwt. To make profits on that
value means good mining; and it isn’t dead-easy,
because from the No. 9 rise to the No. 12 rise the
good wash is on the high ground, with the result
that it is lower in grade and narrower. In the
north end, from 900 ft. to 1000 ft. of work down the
lead has proved cementy and patchy wash. As the
mine is a big one, and has done so well, it is no
time for the Stinking Fish party to cry Out. _ More-
over, the leaders of the Board are not stock .Jobbers,
so with a good miner in charge the Duke still stands
as a Triton among the alluvial minnows of the State.
Mount Morgan (Q.) is gradually getting
its feed-floors at the. copper smelters into order. To
keep a grip on the fumes from the Many Peaks flux,
the feed-apertures have been closed in by pyramid-
shaped covers, 20ft. by 10ft. by 10ft. high. This
arrangement carries the sulphurous draught in to the
big flue before going up the stack. The trucks for
the automatic feeding scheme are now to hand; it
is expected that by this change the number of men
on the feed-floor will be reduced from 10 to two per
furnace. The other eight won’t complain much about
retiring from that portion of “the copper,” as it is
locally called. The stack at the Mundic works is
also to be overhauled, and improved by a scheme to
catch the flue dust. Until the installation is com-
pleted, the Mundic is not the purest breathing-space
in the country-side. A part of interest outside the
main works, on the slope of the rich hill, is the pre-
cipitation works or vats. In the old days, after the
chlorine had done its work on the roasted ore at
the Mundic works, the residue liquid went to the
river. Some few hands down the river made a good
living catching the copper by precipitation
on scrap-iron, as the copper trickled down the Dee in
solution. The co. presently built its own vats, and
made the money which the “catchers” used to make.
The precipitated copper is collected in brick-form,
about 70 p.c. pure, and, having been dried, is thrown
into the converters with the matte. In the March re-
turns, of the 494 tons copper, 68 tons were the re-
turns from these vats. Morganaires are thus enriched
by £lOO odd per day.
Many Teaks sent G 485 tons of basic flux
to Mt. Morgan during March, as against 3940 tons
during flooded February. The quarter's total was
16,578 tons treated for 1550 z. gold and 501 tons
copper. Value, £20,050 = a trifle over 245. a ton.
The resurrected Mount Leyshon, outside
of Charters Towers (Q.), has 20 stampers and some
eight evanide vats to catch the gold from the low-
o-rade stuff (The co. has no use for anything to
treat high-grade stuff.) The hill is quarried by
open cuts on the summit; and shoots have been
fixed down the side of the mountain to the crushers,
to which the ore gravitates. Though most work is
done in open cuts, two tunnels—one 200 ft. and the
other 60ft. —have also been put in. It is intended
to go into the hill on a level with the treatment
plant, and from this work up, as is done at Many
Peaks When the mine is in full swing 1000 tons
a week will be treated. In connection with the sur-
face plant, the old charcoal kilns have been dis-
carded, and the burning is done in tanks, which thus
supply clean fuel for the 120-h.p. suction gas-engine.
Leases totalling 169 acres have been taken
up in the Gladstone (Q.) district, in the names of
three boss employees of Mount Morgan. r b p se
areas include two large ironstone outcrops, which
are spoken of in mysterious whispers. A shall is
being sunk; but a diamond drill is to be used to
rush prospecting, and go deeply into the matter.
Meanwhile, those . outside . know nothing, and talk a
good deal. *
Geologist Dunstan (Q.) was called in to
diagnose the Mt. Shamrock, the shareholders agreeing
to abide bv his report as to whether it was worth
while going on. Back in 1889 3700 tons of stone
yielded £7 3s. a ton, and once, in 1896, a parcel
yielded £lB a ton. In 1900 the value got down to
j 2s. Bd. per ton, but the average was about £2 a
ton between 1902 and 1907. In 1907, 3580 tons
returned 325. Mining, milling, cyaniding and other
expenses ran from £1 10s. to £1 15s. a ton; but
Dunstan reckons that £1 ss. should easily covei
everything, if the mine is worked on an adequate
scale. The ore reserves are hopefully reported on,
so the co., in all probability, will take off its coat
again. # #
For a start the Ilampden-Cloncurry
(Q.) smelting works wall have a capacity of 120
tons per day; but if the first smelting campaign
shapes well, and the copper market holds together,
the plant will be duplicated. In view of this a
good deal of prospecting is going on near Hampden,
for it is understood that the co. will cater for a
ci stom trade. The Trikelana, a recent discovery,
will probably be taken over under option. The
Hampden made a hit by the purchase of the Duchess,
and the management, therefore, keeps its eyes open.
Mount Cuthbert, 70-odd miles from Clon-
eurry (Q.), has had a development at the 350 ft.
level. At the end of the south drive, at 273 ft„ the
face shows 7ft. of 12 p.c. ore, with more standing
on both sides. Overhead, at the 250 ft. level, there
was only 9in. of 8 p.c. ore. A development at
depth is just what was wanted to put heart into the
shareholders, w'ho have to face expensive development
work.
East Mexican, Charters Towers, is push-
ing on with development, but at the moment results
are not bright. The Mexican has always had a
hankering to prospect for another reef on the toot-
wan side of its main level, so a crosscut was run in
from No. 5 level, and at 43ft. this has cut 3m. of
stone in diorite, with a granite wall. No doubt
more prospecting will be done. For the first tew
feet No. 4 shows only lightly mineralised stone, but
is expected to improve. The crosscut at the 760 ft.
level is in hard diorite. Mexican’s chances, beyond
the next few months, are still very much of a
gamble. hast month’s return was 757 tons for
£4957, which has been followed by 411 tons for
£2o92—that’s for a fortnight. This will hardly
run to a regular sixpence per month. Still a No. 2
has just arrived, and others will certainly drop in.
“A call of Is. was made in December,
and it was confidently expected that this would be
the last money required from the shareholders. Stop-
pages from the causes referred to [floods, machinery
breakages, etc.] have resulted not only in a in-
duction of the output, but also increased expenses;
and it may be necessary to make another call of Is.
per share at an early date.” Thus the directors of
Stannary Hills (Q.). The property is a regular
Old Man of the Sea. About 27 times success has
just been within lassoing distance; and then it has
scampered off. Manager Stockdale, who came from
Broken Hill Junction, says: “We have not been
able to crush one-half the hard ore that has been
available, on account of its extreme hardness. The
ore seems to get harder as it goes down.” Still,
though that means more expense —“I am confident
the scheme will work successfully.” *
Queensland Exploration Co. is unhappy.
It thinks it had better die, but so far there seems
to be a difficulty about getting enough shareholders
together to authorise the suicide. The responsi-
bility will probably be thrown on to the London
directors. # # #
Briseis (Tas.) did not do badly during
1910. With the New Brothers Home No. 1 tribute
sluiced out of the race, and with only the tail-end of
Langford’s original Briseis float to be dealt with,
profits of £41,000 were netted. The way things
have gone is shown by the smaller yield. That for
1910 from the Briseis and No. 1 mines was 684
tons tin, against 1102 tons for 1910. Fortunately
tin stands at a high figure. Now the Briseis is
punching out the Krushka flat, whence prospects are
sufficiently good to swell the chest of the general
manager. #

From George Blakemore, formerly general
manager of the Great Cobar, now spending some time
rCalifornia: “If the Roma (Q.) gas bore can get
the gas again, there is hope for it as a gasoline
producer. It has been discovered by an experi-
menter working for the lloncduUi Consolidated 01
Co., whose wells are in the Buena Vista Hills, Cali-
fornia, that by compressing natural gas to 4001 b.
per square inch, each 1000 cubic feet of gas, at
atmospheric pressure, will yield two gallons (Ameri-
can) of gasoline of 0.800 gravity. Some natural
gases yield more than this, whilst others are not
profitable to treat. Ordinarily, 20 cubic feet of gas
f s equal to lib. of crude oil for heating power.
Gas is sold for sd. to 17d. per 1000 cubic feet in
different parts of America, according to quantity
consumed. Through California, practically no use
is made of the vast quantities of pouring out
of the thousands of wells in action. In the Buena
Hills district a well produces from 12 000,000 to
50 000,000 cubic feet of gas daily, and there are
many wells, so that the wasted fuel and horse-power
runs into figures that would make Niagara Falls
look puny as a power-producer alongside. And this is
the gas waste of only a small section of the oil dis-
tricts of California. The oil produce of California
for 1908 was over 1,500,000,000 gallons (English),
and for 1911 it will be over 2,000,000,000 gallons.
The air smells of oil. Its use on hundreds of miles
of roads produces a dustless track with a glistening
rubber-like surface. But the construction of the
road in the first place has to be done m the right
wav; it is no use pouring oil on to the macada-
mised roads of Sydney and expecting results. These
California roads which are dressed with oil abso-
lutely beat anything in the way of a road there is
in the whole of Australia, for such traffic as motor-
cars and light vehicles. For the parks ami drives
about a city they are incomparable. Constructed
with good rock foundations they would be suitable
for heavy traffic. The ‘crude’ sells at 40 cents per
35 English gallons on the oil fields.”

Word comes from Jewburg that no altera-
tion is to be made in the existing practice of
Sunday ore treatment on the Rand. On the point of
taxing profits, however, the Union Parliament has
decreed that the sum expended on excess develop-
ment, which has hitherto been added to current
working costs before calculating taxable profits, must
not be so included in the future.

should give close attention to the power wasted in
transmission of power. The Hyatt Flexible Roller
Bearings save 20 per cent, power and 75 per cent,
of oil, so one of the first duties is naturally for
engineers to direct their special attention to these
bearings and to car boxes for trucks. Hyatts are
in continuous use in Cement Works, Ore Dressing
Mills, Factories, etc., in Australia. Bushings of

Professor Richards, in the revised edition of his
celebrated edition of “Ore Dressing,” 1909, devotes
14 pages to the Wilfley concentrating table, and,
among other things remarks (p. 1465) : “The first
table sold was installed in the Puzzle Mill, at Breck-
enridge, Colorado. Since then over 12,000 have been
sold. This machine has revolutionised ore-dressing,
and as such is a phenomenal success. The original
form, and forerunner of all modern, shaking tables
with longitudinal riffles., has been greatly improv-
ed.” And further on (p. 1478) remarks: “One feature
of the Wilfley table to which especial attention should
be called is its ability to separate sands from slimes
while making the ordinary concentration. This sep-
aration is more complete and thorough than can be
made by any classifier, and should be taken advant-
age of in designing mills.” In the extensive addi-
tions just made to the Golden Horseshoe Mine (W.A.)
a large number of Wilfley Concentrators have been
installed. The Austral Otis Engineering Company,
Limited, South Melbourne, have the sole manufac-
turing rights.* H

Extract from the Saturday Review, Jan. 7, 1911.
—“lnsurance: A Colonial Mutual Departure Policies,
combining life or endowment assurance with othei
benefits, °are gradually coming into favor. Then-
popularity seems to be justified. Why should one
be compelled to take out two or more policies when
several risks can just as easily be covered under a
single contract? It was a consideration of this kind
that some short time ago impelled the Board of The
Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society to introduce
in Australasia a combined Life, Accident and Sick-
ness policy, and as the experiment was attended by
a most welcome increase in New Business, it was
recently decided to extend the issue to the United
Kingdom. There were evidently sound reasons for
the departure. Neither life nor endowment assur-
ance policies afford quite the full protection needed
by most persons, because no provision is made for
accidents or sickness that do not end fatally. . . .
Regarded as a whole, this new policy possesses many
attractions. It is decidedly cheap, and will appeal to
those who canot afford to disburse large sums yearly
in the form of insurance premiums.”*

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, April 24, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —
Governor Chelmsford’s evening party at
State Government House was, in its own
way, the notable innovation of race week.
It broke away from the modern dance, and
avoided the besetting sins of some former
Keepers of vice-regal ceremonies. Sydney
Society will suffer much; but it fiercely re-
sents, behind its yawns, the type of Govern-
ment-house diversion which sits it in a chair
and fills it up with a two-hour course of dull
music. The brighter entertainment of last
week gave every excuse to young perambu-
lating parties of two to wander out on lan-
tern-lit lawns.
The State Cabinet Minister stood out like
a lighthouse in a frothy atmosphere of chif-
fons. Mr. Carmichael and Mr. Holman towered
conspicuously above some of the under-sized
youths of the gilded-butterfly species who
were present at the Governor’s soirde. Mrs.
Holman and Mrs. Carmichael are also above
the average height. Mrs. Trefld, another
fine figure of a woman, more than passing
tall, has the audacity to clothe herself ii
good taste. The Melbourne Age will have
to talk severely to Mrs. Trefie, who forgot
the color of her politics, and omitted to go t •
Government House in a sugar bag, with
bomb-shell trimmings.
The new wing of Rose Bay's vice-regal
halls includes the long, brilliantly-lit room
m which the great foregathering took place.
It s a handsome enough place, with white
or very light-colored walls. No one, so far
as I know, has. ever given a party inside the
plaster of Paris scrolls of a wedding cake,
otherwise this annex might be the circum-
stance. The white blaze of incandescent
light on the white-face make-up, which is
this season’s craze, was coldly cruel. Hard
lines round selfish, worldly mouths, and the
ghastly effect of complexions buried in a
deadly-looking paste, showed up in sharp re-
lief.
With the Burdekin group at the evening
party was the handsome young woman who
left Sydney as Miss Irene Marks, and some
time afterwards honeymooned in foreign
parts as Mrs. George Vivers. She is a
good deal thinner, and wore one of the ap-
parently simple affairs —a yellowish-colored
drape—which the inexperienced male expects
his wife to double (at the little shop round
the corner) for a pound or two. Women
know better. Doucet, Paquin or such, gen-
erally sign their names on tags inside the
waist lines of these cunningly-designed de-
ceptions.
Governor Chelmsford had Lady Dudley,
the Duchess of Bedford, and Lady Beatrice
Kerr-Clark as blue-ribbon Beerage entries at
his evening party. •To work this “Who’s
Who” problem out to a correct answer, his
lux. must have made three descents on the
supper table. He Took Lady Beatrice, a
very pretty Irish girl, on his arm, during
one of these raids on the more serious re-
freshments reserved for superior persons.
When our old acquaintance, Robertson K.
Clark, was looking for a wife, he made a
peaceful invasion into the family circle of
the Earl of Drogheda, Co. Kildare. Their
personal name is Moore, and Henry Charles,
the present owner of the perquisites, is ac-
cused of being the tenth coronet. If he
wishes to worship in Cold Land and Chinese
fashion at the tombs of his ancestors, he has
only to motor from London to Kent. Soon
after Norman William dragged Matilda along
the ground by the hair of her head, as a
mark of his personal esteem, the Moore
family left France for England. Benenden,
in Kent, was their first choice in British
acreage. Sir Edward Moore, a later-day
member of the Benenden branch, had the
temerity, in Elizabeth’s time, to mix himself
up in the Irish question. He snared the
Drogheda. title for doing some meddesome
nut-cracking in the subsequent wars. Hasn’t
Lady Beatrice got a long tail—quite the
longest present at the Easter dress parade!
But, besides the tail, she has' an Irish man-
ner made of the softest fur.
Lady Dudley’s ropes of pearls, and the dia-
mond display of the Elect shouted Money.
Mrs. T. H. Kelly glittered all over with
shivery-looking giit scales, that covered an
understudy in russet brown. Miss Rose
Scott seized the opportunity and the
man of mark, and incited a Cabinet
Minister to dodge her belligerent questions as
to the . blessed results of fighting machine
guns with Peace Society’s pamphlets.. Things
we talk about at entertainments of this kind
also included a discourse on the ethics of
hanging. Cohens, Isaacs, etc., were distinctly
present Mrs. (Justice) Cohen’s bi-plane black
silk bows, sticking fiercely out from her head,
were one of the harmless recreations of the
evening.
Wearers of short, tight frocks slowly
wormed their way across the saddling pad-
dock at Saturday’s last gasp of Easter rac-
ing. Society’s large flat feet were in a
considerable majority. The eelskin drape
that works its way up the shin bone in hur-
ried moments, accentuates the size of the
best shoes, and throws the large foot into
wicked prominence.
Male inhabitants of the grandstand’s
brass-bound pews that are dedicated by the
A J.C. to Governors, Admirals, and globe-
trotting coronets, deserted the stove-pipe hat
on Saturday for indeterminate millinery.
The tall Buchanan bride, sitting with Lady
Dudley, had her Seaforth Highlander in at-
tendance. Having pictured a Scottish war-
rior of this, traditional ferocity as a person
with gigantic shoulders and infuriated scar-
let whiskers, decorated with bagpipes, the
tame appearance of Captain Buchanan came
as a shock, and gave the lie direct to colored
illustrations in English papers. A mouse-
colored suit, a soft hat of uncertain pedi-
gree, and a facial ornament consisting of a
pale ale-colored moustache, with no spiked
ends, don’t belong to the showy style of mas-
culine beauty that captures the public imag-
ination.
Mrs. Buchanan’s bright blue and black
gown (chiefly blue) had a great bunch of
scarlet flowers in the waist-belt. A big
hat and a pair of dark black eyes that
looked through a wriggly-spidery, creepy
pattern of heavy black veiling, com-
pleted the picture. A nip in the air incited
the owners of high-priced pelts to give them
a lawn parade. Mrs. John Toohey, in black,
wore a handsome wide fur stole that aggra-
vated various persons to break the unwrit-
ten commandment, “Thou shalt not tear the
clothes off thy wealthy neighbor’s back.” A
slim young woman, in a speckled get-up,
witn tiny dots all over her dress, was
wrapped to the nose in huge stone-marten
furs, and looked as if she’d come down from
Kosciusko in a blizzard. Ninon and other
gossamer species of feminine fancy-apparel
continued to veil soft satin robes of divers
colors. Mrs. Burdekin’s gown, with a sus-
picion of some alluring shade in yellow,
showing under transparencies of raven black,
was one of the sartorial episodes of the meet-
ing. Dame Simpson’s hat, which was large
and black, was one of the new season at-
tempts to garnish the outside of the brim
with a .flat layer of white lace —reminiscent
of a child’s idea of trimming dolls’ hats.
The members’ stand, which is now cut
off from the lawn by a low picket fence, will
only keep the unsophisticated off the sacred
grass. When the keeper of the gate turns
his head —oh, well, you may just look over
at the pure merinos in the new yarding pen.
The Union Club Ball was successfully
soaked in champagne. An informative
friend tells me that the Town Hall basement,
after the event, looked as if it had been bom-
barded with corks.
The most unusual feature of the Union
Club ball consisted in the fact that the Town
Hall floor was capable of being danced on.
Ordinarily this floor is the grimiest and
stickiest in the metropolis. On Friday
night it gleamed like a newly-polished boot,
and no one’s skirts were blackened; it was
more or less slippery, and no one was un-
necessarily fatigued. But at the best it
is dreadful. Why Sydney’s two big clubs
don’t combine to : sink the profits of a
couple of. these functions in an adjust-
able . dancing. floor —an article with some
resilience in it, and which is not for ever
having concert-room chairs pushed over its
surface —is one of life’s little mysteries.
The Admirable King-Halls have a young
daughter of the teeny-flapper age who is
seriously addicted to music. She is already
continuing the classic pursuit of chromatic
chords and other musical gymnastics, under
a Sydney teacher.
Concerning the Admirable himself, a wail
of minor woe is reported from the junior
sprigs of the service. A usually-truthful
friend may have suddenly broken out into
the checkered career of a cheerful liar, but
anyhow this is his plain tale of a top-hat.
Says he: “The Admirable has jumped with
both feet on the sac suit and bowler hat for
race meetings. He insists on the dignity
of the Navy being presented to the Randwick
bookie in a frock coat, pepper and salt et-
and silk hat.” In spite of this
alleged ukase, I saw one or two naval men
last Saturday, who were garbed in well-worn
sac suits and straw boaters.
Lord Denman, whose most riotous pro-
ceedings in private life are said to be limited
to an unshaken affection for golf, billiards
ana hunting, brings in his train the Viscount
Bury, who married Sydney Myee, an Austra-
lian-born daughter of one-time Governor Car-
rington. The Carringtons and Rawsons
have had no rivals in their grip of the local
hand. Their dogged determination to lay
foundation-stones, open bazaars, and swal-
low interminable doses of illuminated wel-
comes was combined with a staying power
in shaking hands that has extinguished com-
petition.
Rachel .Toy, the Philip Mortons’ new baby,
escaped alive from a gorgeous christening
party. The Duchess of Bedford and other titles
shed the light of their countenances on the
small bundle of lace and muslin which has
created such a stir in Society during the past
few weeks. With Lady Dudley as a godmother,
Rachel Joy can cut her teeth on diamond
ornaments, or play up about her dietary, and
who shall dare to smack her? Mrs. Morton
made a triumphant progress through the re-
cent junketings, and is said to have alluded
to the baby considerably more than twice.
The ordinary man, who can’t be leg-roped to
a christening—unless he be parent or par-
son—is said to feel aghast at the list of
well-known men-about-town who meekly
swallowed tea in the vicinity of anything so
young and unexpected as Rachel Joy. “You
never know what they’re up to, when they’re
as small as that,” said one male ornament
who allowed himself to be captured for the
Morton Joyful party. “Personally I prefer
’em when they can answer back, and don’t
dribble on their chins.”
The Philip Mortons have decided to post-
pone their trip to England, though their
berths on the Mooltan and their London
rooms were already booked. It was feared
that the Red Sea, which is terrifically hot
at this time of the year, might prejudicially
affect the new baby.
As I write, a garden party is careering
round fancy cakes under the auspices of
Chief Justice and Dame Cullen. The
lawyer Cullens are quiet folks, who’d rather
exchange rose - cuttings than small-talk. The
garden of their Mosman home has been an
acute hobby. Unlike most amateurs in
flowers, weeds, etc., Knight Cullen is liable
to give away his best blooms. He’s also been
known to part, without any apparent pang,
with beloved cuttings of sacred scarlet ger-
aniums. A trait worth noting, for the gar-
dener is often the most selfish monopolist
who ever cornered the seeds of the soil.
When this reaches you. Lady Dudley will
have opened the Girls’ Realm Exhibition in
the Town Hall. If no complications prevail,
the Admirable King-Hall will also have sec-
onded, or otherwise officially ear-marked, a
serious resolution of thanksgiving to her
rather weary-looking Ex.
Dr. Julie Carlile Fox, of the Sydney
Medical Mission, has been impounded by
Miss Grace Burrows, the organising sec. of
the Exhibition, to go through a splint and
pill drill. Several scores of persons are
so eager to be half-soled and heeled that
they’re going to present their mild, non-
catching afflictions as subjects of Medical
Mission work. A squad of High School
girls has been trained to show how
simple a thing it is to put a patch on suf-
fering humanity. Mrs. Greig-Smith intends
to be officially responsible for a demonstra-
tion in Norland babies. This can be re-
commended to the curious as an exhilarating
bath-tub “tabloid.” Every aftei-noon, from
2.30 to 3 p.m., the Noi’land nurses will
show the untrained spectator how to wash
up a small non-fighting baby in a bath-tub
and recoi'd time.
Miss Edith Horrocks, a Sydney girl who
works at architectural and constructional
drawing for a local Building Society, is
going to “demonstrate” at the Girls’ Exhi-
bition. How to build a cottage that ivon't
crack its sides, and will have a pantry large
enough to accommodate more than the next-
door cat, ought to be woman’s part in the
modern building boom. Looking over some
plans the other day, I was confounded by
the masculine architect’s idea of a “comfort-
able” cottage. No bathroom, no pantry, no
shelves or cupboards, and a kitchen big
enough to hold a stove, a dresser, and an
undersized woman, if she stood still in one
place—this is the oi’dinary man’s notion of a
home of rest for weary workers.
From the menu of the N.S.W. Chamber of
Manufacturers’ luncheon last week, I pick,
with an inquiring pen, “Warrego Wobbler”
and “Waratah Charlotte Australian.” Vari-
ous Charlottes I have known. Werther’s
Charlotte went on cutting bread and butter.
Apple Charlotte is dressed up with bread-
crumb trimmings. But Waratah Charlotte
as best-girl on the menu card to “Wattle
Blossom Trifle,” remains one of the minor
mysteries of Australian Manufacturers’
, week.
Court etiquette has left to it some queer
fringes of primitive rag-and-bone ceremonies.
According to a Cold Land scribe, Lord Rich-
ard Nevill’s father, the venerable Marquis of
Abergavenny, is entitled to pick up and
pocket—or carry away in any sack or suit-
case he prefers—all eatables left on the
royal table at Coronation banquets. On
these festive occasions Lord Richard’s parent
has to support the title of Chief Officer of
the Lai’der.
According to the cables, Ola Humphreys
has been married to Prince Ibrahim Hassan,
a cousin of Egypt’s Khedive. She is
one of the two or three European women
who have entered the Khedivial circle with
a wedding ring. Some years ago an infuri-
ated opposition greeted the marriage of a
French banker’s daughter with another
cousin of the Khedive, who seems to have
escaped from the “Arabian Nights” with the
name Prince Ali Fazil. If Ola, with her
charm of manner, follows the diplomatic
footsteps of Princess Fazil, she’s likely to
conquer the early Egyptian prejudices of her
Moslem relatives-in-law. Khedive Abbas
Hilmi is reported to be a good-looking, civi-
lised specimen of his race, with a dimple in
his chin, and a taste for model farming and
railway building. He will, if nicely spoken
to, and frequently fed with the title “High-
ness,” discoui-se with foreign visitors in
Cairo, on onions, Jersey cows, and harem
skirts.
Dr. Coward, a gleeful member of the Shef-
field Choir, is a musical widower with three
good-looking daughters, who warble in the
chorus. Mr. Shaw, of this city, has applied
for the position of host to Cowai’d pere.
George Washington’s successor, who gave me
this news, has not added a postscript.
Whether the singing daughters are included
in the Shaw hospitality is therefore left
an open question.
Dr. Charles Harriss admits that, though
the Sheffield “sol fa” combination is not out
for loot, it has a human desire to add finan-
cial success to its meteoric career across the
globe. So far this desire has been gratified.
Writing from Ottawa to a friend in Sydney
he says: “The demand for seats has been
enoiunous here. In Toronto I was so busy
that I was seldom out of my office... We
are going to do Berlioz’s ‘Faust’ in Mel-
bourne... The Canadians are trying to kill
us with kindness.” Rose-tinted optimism,
with an aftermath of wet towels worn round
Sheffield Choir heads, apparently pursues the
songsters across Canada’s frost-bitten cities.
Mr. W. C. Somers is habitually agent in
Auckland (M.L.) for the P. and O. Co. dur-
ing those sombre hours in which Man offi-
cially glues his thoughts to a roll-top desk.
There appear to be some compensations!
Mr. Somers, who looked in at Randwick’s
festival, is a steward of the Auckland
Metrop. Racing Club in his more frivolous
moments. He took with him, by Morea to
Melbourne, excess luggage in the shape of a
charming wife.
Miss Molly Lloyd, one of last week’s
debutantes, is a daughter of the late and
handsome “Lance” Lloyd, whose genial man-
ners as official assignee were said to cast an
agreeable glamor over the chilly task of
hauling bankrupts over the glaciers. Molly’s
maternal grandfather, the late Solicitor-
General Williams, was once counted amongst
the rough-and-tough exponents of legal
learning in this city.
The father of Australian artist Arthur
Streeton was in Sydney recently. He is a
hale veteran of SO, who long ago retired from
the Victorian Civil Service on pension, and
recently returned from a visit to England.
He talks enthusiastically of his son’s suc-
cess, and of the talent and graces of his
wife, Nora Clench, the Irish violinist.
Dr. Alan Carroll, who died last week, did
splendid work in connection with the Child
Study Association. He was an old man
with a young imagination. His persistent
plea for the rational up-bringing of tender
youth, and his ardent belief that a sound
body contained a sound mind, made him an
enthusiast in the modern cult of helping the
child to develop the calves of its legs as well
as the twists of its grey matter.
The personally-conducted tour, as con-
nected with the overseas traveller who wants
to give his immediate attention to Australian
scenery, is comparatively new. Among the
pioneer stampedes of wealthy Americans,
stage-managed by their own agent, will be
the noble nine who arrive here on Friday.
Since December they’ve hunted the Maori-
land geyser in its pumice-stone lair, and
scratched the ice off Joseph Ward’s most
remunerative glaciers! They’ve taken their
time, and spent wallets of gold coin in the
pursuit of blue lakes and mud volcanoes. Now
they’re going to sample N.S.W. With the
party comes Captain Akin Higgins, as the
official representative of the TJ.S.A. tourist
agency of Itaymond and Whitcomb. He
“fixes” up his party with the skill of long
usage, and is going to loom large across the
snow-shod heights of Kosciusko, in conspir-
acy with Percy Hunter’s Intelligence De-
partment.
Mr. and Lady Beatrice Kerr Clark
left for Adelaide and the Coronation on
Sunday night. He talks of returning here
to settle down later on.
The Ernest Brookes —she is a daughter of
.Tames Burns, M.L.C., and he a well-known
Melbourne identity—are at Cromer flats.
Later Mr. Brookes will return to Melbourne
and business, and his wife will go to Gowan
Brae, the parental home near Parramatta.
The Jim Osbornes are also settled in
Cromer, after a strenuous spell of house
hunting. They have hired two flats, to ac-
commodate their various belongings and
guests.
Yours, with a broad nib.
VANDORIAN.

THE DASHWOODS.
By STEELE RUDD. With 15 full-page illustrations by CLAUDE MARQUET.
Price i/- Postage 3d.
When the Dashwoods visit the city the fun begins, for Old John and Young Peter seem
incapable of keeping out of mischief, and manage somehow to entangle the whole family in
their ludicrous escapades, until Mother and the girls begin to long for home. For pure merri-
ment and good humor the author has never written anything better than “The Dashwoods,”
which is sure to prove a favorite.
Billy Pagan, Mining Engineer.
By RANDOLPH BEDFORD. With four half-tone illustra-
tions by PERCY F. SPENCE. Price 1/- Postage 3d.
The mining expert who is sent out by the syndicate to see if
the mine under offer is “salted” or otherwise, lias often to pit
his wits against the low cunning of underground engineers, and
is not always successful.
Pie must be as observant as Sherlock Holmes, as wary as a
tiger and as brave as a lion, for, when baulked, the vendors are
apt to become dangerous.
Mr. Bedford has intimate knowledge of the things whereof
he writes and the power to depict situations and sketch character
in a most masterly manner.
The cases cited here are both thrilling and amusing.

EROS I
EROS WIN
“EROS! EROS WINS!”
A new Racing Novel by F. AGAR. Illustrated by PERCY
SPENCER. Paper Cover, 1/- Postage 3d.
“Eros,” be it known, was the Greelc god of love, and, as
love generally wins, it was thought a good name for a horse.
But even a good horse is not always allowed to win, and many
of the doubtful methods of preventing him are exposed in this
book. Both the upper and under sides of “The Turf” are
depicted here—the aristocratic, straight going owner, and the
low-down swindler who lives on the game ; but the little Greek
god has also an interesting innings, as there is a fascinating society
love-story running side by side with the racing yarn, and the
outcome of the plot and counter-plot is that again “ Eros” wins.
To those who don’t know much about racing, this book will
be an eye-opener; to those who do, it will be a treat.

The Charm of the Pianola Piano.
r I ''HE pleasure obtained from the STECK PIANOLA PIANO is twofold.
First, there is the enjoyment of the music itself—the great master-
pieces when you are in the mood for them, and the catchy “hits”
and comic-opera selections when you want something lively.
Second, there is the fascination of producing the music yourself. That
is something that you have to experience personally in order to appreciate.
Unite both forms of entertainment, and you have a combination that
is irresistible.
An Invitation to Call.
It is well worth a little of anyone’s time to find out exactly what the
STECK PIANOLA PIANO is, and how much it adds to the home life.
Do not let any preconceived ideas of the instrument debar you from in-
vestigating it. Call with a list of your favorite selections. Listen to them
critically, note the human-like effect, and then, if you will, play them for
yourself. See how sensitive the instrument is, how responsive to the
slightest turn of expression.
Vital Improvements.
OU cannot possibly judge the STECK PIANOLA PIANO unless you
have familiarised yourself with the latest developments. There is
1 the METROSTYLE, insuring an artistic interpretation—the
THEMODIST, bringing out the theme or melody—the Graduated Accom-
paniment, and other features wholly wanting in the many imitations. Re-
member that you are not investigating the PIANOLA PIANO when you
look at some of the so-called “ Player-Pianos ” that are prevented by patent
from utilising vital improvements.
The Steinway Pianola Piano.
A cordial invitation is extended to every owner of a Steinway Piano,
and to every admirer of this celebrated instrument, to inspect the latest
achievement, the Stein way with the PIANOLA built into it. The Pianola
Company control the sale of the Steinway PIANOLA Piano for the entire
world.

During the Easter Encampment our noble
defence forces fought, although they did not bleed,
for their country with immense fortitude and
enthusiasm. Especiall}' when inspired to their
most heroic deeds by D.O.L. Wisdom. The
soldierly qualities of the men shone resplendent
when led by officers mentally illuminated by that
arc lamp of wisdom, D.0.L., the Whisky of the
Wise.*
A MISTAKEN NOTION.
There is a prevailing; opinion that it is danger-
ous to stop diarrhoea, too suddenly. That tnay be
the ease when an astringent medicine is used, but
there is not the least danger of stopping it too
quickly when Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera and
