MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, April 24, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie,—
I am sure that Cupid has sold his bow and
bought a Maxim to shoot off his arrows with. In
Geelong, just recently, no less than 40 people
were tied in pairs, for better or much worse, in
one day. Fortunately the township that isn’t so
sleepy had sufficient churches to cope with the
rush of business. Nevertheless the city was dis-
organised for the day, and ran wild with white
satin bows, old slippers and a blizzard of confetti.
Melbourne received several of the honeymooners.
These happy days railway porters can’t wheel a
loaded truck five yards without endangering the
toes of a beaming pair or disturbing a chattering
group of pale blue or mauve-colored bridesmaids.
Top hats and frock coats are no longer unused
extravagances; they scarcely spend two days
together among tissue paper and moth halls.
Mabel Emmerton, lawyer Emmerton’s girl, said
“ I will ” in duet with tennis-champion Norman
Brookes in St. Paul’s Cathedral on Wednesday
afternoon. The chimes fought with the traffic
sounds for supremacy for half an hour before the
arrival of the bride, and resumed again for another
half an hour on her departure. Guests armed
with silver-and-white cards had to elbow their
way through a crowd which blocked the station
corner and surged round the railings and police-
men. Every Sassiety maiden in Melbourne
arrived in her smartest frock, and bridesmaids
from earlier weddings of the season found points
of vantage from which to observe the effect of
Mabel Emmerton’s group of six.
The bunch of bridesmaids was like a mammoth
bouquet of hydrangea. The pale satin of their
trailing gowns was hydrangea blue under transpa-
rent tunicsof pink. Nymph-like pink rose-wreaths
perched on their modern coiffures, and they
carried wired bouquets of the delicate-tinted
bloom. They did not match so well as regards
height. Big blonde Esther Fairbairn, of the
woolly family, towered over Dodo Staughton on
one side; and Vera Clarke, daughter of the
departed Janet, made' her look small on the
other. Nancy Grice, who is happier on her hunter
than afoot anywhere, was maid-in-chief. Vera
Madden, the drooping artist c daughter of the
Frank Maddens, and May Brookes, a sister of
bridegroom Norman, completed the sextette. Two
small children, dressed in cavalier style, gave a
Christmas-supplement effect as train-bearers. One
of them was the Pursey Chirnsides’ small son
Andrew, and the other Joan Richmond, the
Staughton granddaughter. Other helpers were
Archbishop Clarke, in scarlet array, and the Rev.
Evelyn Snodgrass, who defied the rules of arith-
rnetic and made two one. Flowers are not allowed
in the Cathedral as decorations ; but the altar was
heaped with white roses. The strip of scarlet
carpet leading right up to this dazzling whiteness
made a vivid theatrical contrast.
The reception flurry was at Raveloo, Toorak,
and thither the guests petrolled after the ceremony.
Governor Carmichael and his lady presented the
pair with a lovely blue enamelled jar, which had
centre place among a display calculated to make
any burglar’s mouth water. The bridegroom’s
gifts to the bridesmaids were gold chain bags
studded with pearls and turquoises. To his bride
he gave diamonds. The tennis racquet was
everywhere in the decorations. There were even
tennis racquets on the carriage. The Emrr.ertons
carefully brought a cake with them from Fog-
land, in case no Australian baker could be found
in time. It also was covered with the sporty
implement.
The elaborately married lady got into a blue-
velvet coat and skirt after the breakfast, and
travelled off with her husband to Mornington for
the honeymoon. They have taken the Reggie
Clarkes’ house in Queen’s-road, and will live
there on their return.
The guest list was the catalogue of the old
noblesse, and the dresses were like a chiffon store’s
autumn show. Mrs. Brookes carried purple
orchids and violets to lighten the sombre effect of
her black and crepe. Mrs. Emmerton wore dull
blue velvet, with a black velvet plumed hat. Mrs.
Deakin, stately and tall in a mole-colored gown,
was accompanied by her daughter, Mrs. Herbert
Brookes. The Staughtons, the Fairbairns and
the Chirnsides occupied the special front seats.
While the Brookes-Ernmerton wedding was
filling St. Paul’s on Wednesday, St. Pat’s, on the
summit of Eastern Hill, was having its own
marrying. Bess Noonan, a St. Kilda girl, and
Aubert Loughnan, of the Coonamble (N.S.W.)
Loughnans, were the contracting parties. The
bride, in the softness of a white Liberty gown,
wore some fine old lace that had done duty at
Mrs. Loughnan’s orange blossoming. The brides-
maids, all of them sisters of the bride or bride-
groom, were garbed in blue. After the ceremony
the wedding party went over the wax to the
Grand, where the big drawing-room was smothered
in chrysanthemums and autumn foliage. Break-
fast—that movable feast in wedding phraseology
—contented the hungry in the supper-room. The
bride then enveloped herself in mole-colored
wrappings, and the honeymooners made for Syd-
ney. The newly-weds will live at Castelwellan
station, near Tragowel, in this State.
Melbourne Town Hall is housing an address of
farewell to the Carmichael lady wherein Mrs.
Climber and Mrs. Clomb may record their names.
The weighty work will be bound in one immense
flitch, and presented to the popular Governoress
while the vessel by which she goes is still taking
cargo, so that the load of signatures may be
properly stowed under hatches. Some of the
farewell presents are, however, really sensible.
A rug from the Ballarat looms and a comfortable
deck hat from the local mills are gifts that will
make the ocean traveller remember the donors
with gratitude.
The Eight Hours procession trailed trium-
phant! Melbourne on Monday morning ;
but it was afternoon before an ordinary citizen
could shoulder a way through the holiday crowd
which had gathered to view the serpent. The
Tramway Co. announced in the morning papers
that the obsolete cable arks would only be motion-
less “for half or three-quarters of an hour.” But
they had to sit down on the highways for a con-
siderably longer period, and the cataract of thrip-
pences almost went bone-dry. Helpless and
furious gripmen rang ineffectual bells; for you
can’t shift a multitude by ringing. The marching
squads were conspicuously loyal. Before round-
ing the turn from Bourke-street into Spring-street,
each band halted before the House and rolled
forth “ Gorsave,” while the workers respectfully
• bared their heads. I presume that the demonstra-
tion was arranged to reassure Bruce Smith and
other prominent revolutionaries.
The dames of the Alfred Hospital bazaar
ruffled their feathers on Eight Hours Day. The
demonstration was so huge this year that the
Eight Hours people required the whole of the
Exhibition space for their gala, so the Druids and
the bazaar had to turn out. The stalls had to be
taken down late on Saturday night, after the 10
o’clock closing, and there was still some clearing-
up left for Sunday. Sassiety dames and assorted
mayoresses, with the sweetest charity maids of the
town, worked hard to get their Old English
street put away in due order ; for on Tuesday it
all had to be pieced together again. The break-
up was not an altogether unmixed evil, though—
it gave the stall-holders the chance for a series of
furious bargain sales.
The members’ list of the Quamby Club for
women has grown faster than a Williamson bean-
stalk, and the first crop it bears is an ambition to
be residential. So it has bespoken quarters in
the new arcade that is to cut through from Collins-
street to Flinders-street, and will have rooms
built to suit itself on a Collins-street corner.
Miss Onions, the secretary of that useful little
institution, the City Newsboys’ Society, went
away last week for a holiday. The hoys gave her
several hearty farewells, and presented her with a
fountain pen with the attached condition that she
was to use it to write them a joint letter every
week. The Carmichael lady, who makes a hobby
of the City Newsboys and is their president, helped
in the friendly farewelling. Mrs. Guy Sherwood,
another enthusiast in the cause, handed over a
comfy outfit for the deck ; the Herald people
came along with a dressing-case; and other pre-
sents were showered into the cabin of this hard-
working and much-liked lady.
Alfred Billson, Victoria’s Minister for Rail-
ways, went breakfastless on his way by train from
Sydney to Melbourne one day last week; and
thereupon hangs a change which other railway
travellers will welcome. At Wangaratta he ordered
a hamper from the refreshment rooms; but the
rooms told him that hampers had been forbidden
by the General Superintendent. At Benalla the
Minister hungrily ordered another hamper; but
the refreshment rooms again pleaded that ham-
pers were off. So the Minister did not fracture
his fast until nearly noon. On arrival at Mel-
bourne he first of all tore some food, and then
ordered hampers “on” again. The thus-
exploded rule was the outcome of a complaint by
a refreshment room that its trade was being
injured by another refreshment room putting
hampers on the train. It lasted only while the
general public was pinched ; when it pinched the
boss cocky of the railways it was a different
matter altogether.
Our own Bessie Major has made an 18-carat
name for herself in London. She played with the
Asches here, and actor-manager Oscar and his
missus took her away to London with them. By
all accounts, her Dame Quickly was a masterpiece,
and threatens to be the standard by which future
Dames will be judged. The Brough players have
scattered far and wide. Mrs. Brough is with an
Allan Hamilton co. in Perth. Emma Temple is
with “ The Whip” people, and writes cynically :
“You’ll be pleased to hear I am wearing beauti-
ful gowns. ” Titheradge is giving his nerves a rest
in his Sydney garden. Meanwhile the drama
goes—to the horse and the bedroom.
Commander Victor Gurner, R.N., who became
a post-captain in December last, is another Aus-
tralian who is climbing well. He entered Father
Bull’s Navy as a cadet in 1883. was gazetted
lieutenant in 1892, and commander in 1904.
Gurner, who now controls H.M.S. Philomel, of
the East Indian Squadron, is a member of a St.
Kilda clan which runs considerably to law.
Artist Ted Colles has confessed to matrimony,
aided and abetted by Florence, the stately and
eldest of the four Levenberg girls. St. Kilda
. tried to modify its somewhat rapid surprise at the
lady’s absence with a what-did-I-tell-you-all-along
sort of air; nevertheless it once again had to
admit that artistic-literary folk always were a
trifle sudden.
Reginald Roberts has been suffering from influ-
enza and a “ throat.” Last week the theatre did
not see him for two performances: and he wa3
fighting for some days before he retired coughing
to his room.
The Alliance Eran<jaise asks its friends to a
soiree on April 26, one hour after Australia has
flung its last emphatip “Yes —Yes” into the
ballot box.
Mrs. Gay and Mrs. Aarons, two members of
the Alliance Frangaise, have had “the purple
ribbon,” known as Les Palmes, conferred upon
them for their organising work by the French
Minister of Public Instruction. The energetic
pair have been on the committee of the Alliance
for years.
Manager Miller, of West’s, with M*rs. Miller on
one arm and daughter Eily on the other, departs
by the Morea this week for a round-the-world
jaunt. The Miller daughters are clever girls.
Phyllis was recently sent off for a long holiday
by West’s, for whom she had been doing valuable
work. She returned the other day after rambling
abroad for 18 months. Molly, another girl of the
Miller household, spent many months in Manila
looking after her father’s interests, which hap-
pened to be iron-pipes or something else poetic.
On the canvas wings of the post comes the news
that George Allan, of the Block musical firm, has
established his two sisters in Paris while he is off
to Germany. Later on he is to conduct them to
the Coronation.
Miss Sweatman joins the artists who are show-
ing their wares in this city. An exhibition of
portraits and Warrandyte sketches will be opened
on Tuesday, May 2, at Alexandra Chambers.
Dalgety’s, with Captain Douglas hosting, are
giving a marine At Home on board the new
Aberdeen liner Themistocles on Thursday.
Special trains to Port Melbourne for the guests
will be one of the big firm’s hospitable extrava-
gances.
All the world knows that the bride who respects
Tradition wears a blue garter for luck. “Some-
thing old and something new, something bor-
rowed and something blue ” ; so runs the old saw
—and it cuts a deal of wedding cake. At a re-
cent marrying, the bridegroom made a bid for his
share of the luck by borrowing the other garter
rom his bride and clumping it on one of his sup-
ports.
A proclamation lately went forth from Customs
Minister Tudor prohibiting the export of the
following birds: —
Emus, terns and gulls ; egrets, herons and bitterns ;
lorikeets, cockatoos, parrots, dollar or roller birds, king-
fishers, bee-eaters, cuckoos, lyre birds, pittas, robins,
ground thrushes and chats, wrens ; shrike tits, thick-
heads, and shrike robins; sun birds, bower birds,
rifle birds, grebes, albatrosses, finches, orioles, shining
starlings.
In the same way the importation of the following
is prohibited :
The birds of paradise, the hummingbirds, the
any one of the several species of Asiatic pheasants of the'
genus Lophophorus, as the Impeyan pheasant; the
Argus, any one of the several species of Asiatic pheasants
of the genus Argusianus, as the Argus pleasant; the
crowned pigeon, any of the several species of large
crested pigeons of the genus Goura inhabiting New
Guinea and the adjacent islands ; the rheas, the owls,
the kingfishers, the macaws, any parrot of the genus
Sittace or Macrocereus, the stork tribe, the heron tribe,
the ibises and spoonbills, the todies, the cock of the rock,
the quezal, or resplendent trogon.
I am learning the list off by heart, to recite at the
milliner before choosing my winter hat.
Calve, who likes her own way, and generally
gets it, had a fatiguing round with a couple of
American port-officials at the conclusion of her
voyage to America with Gasparri. An immigrant
officer declined to record her by the illustrious
name of Calv 4, but wrote her down as plain
“ Mrs. Gasparri.” Then he and the purser re-
fused to accept the age she gave, but added a
decade or two and wrote “44 ” ! Calve almost
burnt the two of them where they stood with eye-
lightning. But before they even commenced to
smoulder, the health officer arrived armed with
authority and frightful vaccinating implements.
He was a determined man, and nothing would
convince him that he and his apparatus were
totally unnecessary. Finally he was allowed to
make a sear where the stocking will cover it.
With the ball season approaching, prospective
hostesses are seeking for dancing novelties. The
hostess wants her party to be marked by a sensa-
tion equalling that of the harem skirts ; the guests
want to be able to look wise, > and suggest “the
latest thing we saw in Paris ” ; and last, but not
least, the dancing master is ardently seeking the
sweet uses of advertisement. And so the novelty
is pursued. At present one hears rumors that we
are to see the “ One-Step” ; but it would be well
if a heavy duty could be put on its importation, or
if the Customs officials can’t ban it that at least
the good taste of women will do so. The “ One-
Step ” is the rage of a certain section of Parisiana,
but it is not the section a wife would introduce to her
husband. It is an unfortunate development of the
“ Boston,” which became somewhat popular last
year, but is uglier and less graceful. As one sees
it at the Bal Tabarin, danced by either two girls
or a man and a girl, it is merely a wriggle, danced
to a cakewalk measure, the step short and rapid.
Altogether it is ungainly, with but a thin line
separating it from the vulgar and indecent.
Melba will shortly be within coo-ee. She is to
leave London immediately the Coronation frivols
are over, and will land in Australia in good time
to supervise the final preparations for her grand
opera season. About September the musical folk
will be in Heaven—or as close thereto as it is pos-
sible to get without suffering the unpleasantness
of being a corpse.
The marriage of Nellie Russell, the daughter of
racing-man Russell of Auckland (M.L.), and the
Earl of Hardwick is to happen this month in
England. Come to think of it, the best way of
securing a front seat at the George V. series of
entertainments is to marry into the peerage.
People bored by the rather tame play at the
cricket “final” at the M.C.C. Grounds on
Saturday were diverted by looking over into the
Richmond Ground. There a girls’ cricket match
was being furiously waged between “Cold-
streams ” and “ Thistles.” They played cricket
right enough—but the dress ! The girls wore pads
on their legs (cricketing pads, of course), but the
conventional skirt hung down over the sheathed
limbs; and when the players ran—and some of
them ran like deer—the dust was scuffled up in
clouds. The scoring was a great joy—the whole
batting side squealed and shrieked at each
run. The bowling was good, but the hair would
get in the eyes. Still it was the unserviceable
dress that struck all the peeping onlookers.
There is nothing quite so fetching as fencing
garb, and if that were adopted, the game might
be better worth watching than even a slow
“final” played by mere males.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The authorities are to “consider” the regulation of
street traffic—and flustered woman says : “ Not before it
was time ! ” The roads and footpaths (especially at mid-
day on Saturdays) seethe with people, and vehicles go
where they like and as they like; so that crossing
Collins-street or Pitt-street is an easier matter than
negotiating undisciplined King William-street.
Most of us are busy recovering from Easter, when we
tried, as usual, to cram a week’s doings into three days.
The Adelaide Polo Club abandoned its usual gymkhana,
and instead played a match against Strathalbyn at the
Plympton Ground on Saturday. Sassiety motored down
and drank tea, and cast casuai glances at the game, and
saw the home team win. The main reason of the con-
test was the Richard Smith Challenge Cup, which must
be won three times by the same club to guarantee per-
manent possession.
Onkaparinga had a long chapter of accidents to its
debit this year. At the last moment two South-Eastern
jockeys (Campbells) were recalled by the death of their
father ; the first race saw the death of Harry Holmes,
who was piloting Kanmantoo ; later in the day two
horses were killed and another badly knocked about.,
and both Steeples (the Great Eastern and the Amateur)
were responsible for a bunch of falls. Since then, Harry
Holmes’s five-months-old baby has died too. The two
amateurs to get a shaking were Neville Colley and
“Bob” Cudmore, both popular and good horsemen.
They’re on the road to recovery, but are still in the doc-
tor’s hands.
By the way, can nothing be done to keep imbeciles
from running across the track immediately in front of
the hhrses? It is said that one of the worst smashes on
Monday was caused by a woman (in this matter they’re
worse than the inevitable dogs!) who ran under the
horses’ noses, and in some miraculous way escaped injury
herself.
But for the smashes, the day was all one could wish.
Perfect weather; record crowd; good finishes. The
pack on the road was something to remember, and the
dust was such that sometimes you couldn’t see a yard
ahead ; Ltrt that’s part of the game. The S.P.C. A.
(which sounds like the S.P.C.K., but isn’t) had posted
inspectors along the road at intervals, whose business it
was to keep an open eye for underfed and overworked
beasts. Four fat women and six babies does seem a
heavy load for one half-starved horse, especially when
the way is long and hilly; but it takes outside opinion
to force this home to some people. The presence of in-
spectors must have acted as a strong hint, for there were
fewer glaring cases than usual. Some enterprising souls
seized the occasion to pret rid of various worthless sove-
reigns, and, finding- the tote too sharp for them, turned
their attention to the refreshment tents. The proprie-
tors’ lurid language seems to suggest that they succeeded.
X suppose you’ve heard that we’re waging war over ithe
question of Sunday entertainments. The Government
has Early Victorian morals, and says “ You shan’t.” The
purveyors of entertainments say “ We' will.” Owen
Smyth, Superintendent of Public Buildings, locked Fin-
kelstein and Marcus out of the Exhibition grounds, so
they sued him for damages and reaped a harvest of £2OO.
Now we wait for the next move.
Miss Walker, who has been selected by the Government
to preside over the outcomings of female emigrants from
England, will depart by the Mnoltan on May 11. (The
lady undertook the difficult task of collecting evidence
from mistress and maid as to the Mary Ann difficulty
which is rampant here, and the fact that she incurred the
goodwill of both parties speaks sufficiently for her tact..
S’Australians wish her well.
Speaking of the Mooltan, we’re still feeling suspicipus-
of her discharged freight, and trippers who returned by
her haven’t yet been rushed with callers or, dinner-
invitations.
Ruth, the handsome daughter of Speaker Cedes, has-
changed her maiden name for Trew, and will be taking
up her residence on some up-country station. And the
McFarlane-Hawker wedding removes one of our belles
from Mount Lofty to the comparatively remote region of
the lakes at the Murray mouth.
What myth will the new chum swallow next? A
selector t’other day found an old man kangarooin earnest

supial to distract its attention, he could deal it a knock
out blow with the other,” he tried the experiment ? Re’s

The servant girl is rapidly becoming ex-
tinct, and the question for women is, “ What -gre
we going to do,about it ? ” The problem is fully

A growl from up the line : '
No trucks for coal, no trucks for firewood, no trucks
for wheat, but lots of trucks for the conveyance of decent
white Australians ! Easter Monday I put on my best
suit, went to the railway station, and bought a ticket for
Bendigo and its Easter Fair. When the train arrived I
was hustled into a truck. That in a white man’s country,
and the best country on earth ! And, arrived at the
Fair," somebody told me that Deakin had just been in
Bendigo saying what a dreadful thing it would be if any
change were made in the control of the railways.
“R. 8.” : The Commonwealth Postal De-
partment is hereby advised to withdraw extra
postal facilities from the Japanese Club, jat
Broome. The Japs’ mailbag is sealed at the
Japanese Club by the Jap secretary, and taken
from club to steamer and from steamer to club
sealed. Australia is very like the kindly house-
holder who provided the burglar with a fire
escape so that he might not risk his life on a
ladder.


Present State of Beauty Culture
IT has been suggested to me to prepare
a special article dealing in a general
way with the present state of what is
known as “ Beauty Treatments.”
To do anything like justice to a sub-
ject which deals in ever so limited a
degree with Beauty of Women would
exhaust the space of a thousand volumes.
Shakspeare has sung it, and Petrarch
and Goethe ; but the combined efforts of
a score of such giants of the mind could
no more than touch the fringe of the sub-
ject.
The Cultivation of Beauty is a Science.
And if it has been so formulated and
systematised that it is now counted
among the sciences, it is due principally
to the efforts of Austrian, Russian and
German men of learning.
Therefore, it is to those countries that
we must now look for light and guidance
in the important business of preserving,
improving and restoring the beauty of
woman.
Now, concerning the conditions in
British countries: What, precisely, are
they ?
I am a foreigner, and must observe
discretion and tact.
But, frankly and reverently, British
Beauty Culture is really very, very elusive.
Many seem unable, to realise that the
skin of the face does, in point of fact,
require differeiit'care than is given to the
tegument of the rest of the body. When
one remembers that it is the only part of
the anatomy which is always exposed to
the vicissitudes of the atmosphere, it must
become clear that it should be treated
with a due regard to this important cir-
cumstance.
And so I am often asked : Is soap
uood for the complexion? Now, in the
form in whiPh it is put, this question is
unanswerable except by 'someone who is
particularly interested in the sale of soap.
If the inquirer would ask whether it is
good for her complexion, the question
would be properly framed. I would then
ascertain whether her skin is inclined to
be greasy, or is normal, or dry. In the
one case I would say : Yes, you should
use soap and water often, provided, of
course, you have the right kind of both.
In the other instance my answer would
be: You may use them, say, twice a
week. But in the last instance I would
reply : If you wish to have a complexion
left at all, shun soap and water as you
would the plague—and in their place use
a pure, scientific emollient cleanser or
skin food such as Valaze or Novena
Cerate. These will keep the skin in-
finitely cleaner than soap and water,
without drying, shrivelling and irritating
it. Moreover, Valaze possesses skin
clearing, toning and stimulating proper-
ties which give this speciality a unique
position among complexion correctives.
Some readers may here interpose that
they are unable to judge of the require-
ments of their skin, possessing no quali-
fication or knowledge of the subject.
My advice to these is : If you do not
know, ask.
To show how different scientific beauty
culture is from unscientific and slipshod
methbds, my book, “Beauty in the
Making,” has been translated and adapted
for circulation in all English-speaking
countries. As a contribution to the
literature of the Beauty of Woman it has
been acknowledged to be a book of in-
terest. It is a handbook which sums up
the very newest researches of a science
that should be dear to the heart of every
woman. Anyone who will write to me
for “Beauty in the Making” will receive

All my color left me, my faee grew thin and

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Church is becoming increasingly
shocked at the development of “ class conscious-
hess,” and in Melbourne the Anglican Synod in-
tends calling a conference to try to discover how
best religion may battle with the “growing evil.”
This sudden concern of the Church is very touch-
ing. When “ class consciousness ” was confined
to a very rich, powerful, dominant class, it was
scarcely noticeable from the height of a pulpit;
but now that the workers have realised that they
also belong to a class, and have thereupon set
about abolishing a few of the monopolies
and other privileges of the first-class, their
struggles and their triumphs suddenly become
evidences of an unrighteous and linbrotherly senti-
ment. Apparently perfect brotherly love con-
sists of one brother affectionately eating the other,
and the other submitting with fraternal sweetness.
MUTUAL SYMPATHY.
From a Melbourne paper:
The Acting-Premier, in comparing the Arbitration
Court with the Wages Board, said that in Maoriland the
workers were reluctant to go before a certain Judge
because that Judge was not favorable to the workers.
That was not the case in this country, unfortunately.
After thus implying his objection to Justice
Higgins, Willie Watt proceeded to describe his
Honor as a man who would give the workers jus-
tice. Well, what does Mr. Willie Watt want—
“unfortunately”?

Fat folks shouldn’t fast. To repress your
appetite is not at all necessary. If you wish to
reduce flesh rapidly and safely, take two teaspoon-
fuls after meals of this simple home mixture. One
half-ounce of Marmola, one ounce of fluid extract
of Gljcyrrhiza 8.P., one ounce of pure Glycerine
8.P., and Peppermint. Water to make six ounces.
This will not cause a wrinkle, nor will it necessi-
tate any interference with your ordinary diet.*

the above prescription has been condensed in the
form of a compact and perfect tablet known as

The building of the Warrego and the
1 kclihood of similar contracts being captured by
Sydney has moved the Weary Government to
make a start at establishing a shipbuilding yard
between Williamstown and the mouth of the
Yarra, at a spot bearing the wildly original name
of Greenwich. The Government’s present inten-
tion is to build two 600-ton hopper
dredges, needed for the “port im-
provement ” scheme. If lie had dune
that two years ago, instead of allow-
ing various public bodies to send
abroad for dredges that “couldn’t be
built here,” he might have had a
sizable shipyard in effective trim by
this time. Apparently it didn’t
matter, though, so long as the work
was being done in some foreign
country. It was only when a neigh-
boring State began to do things that
were talked about that Weary woke
up.
Boyd, ex-M.L.A., director of
the Jumbunna Coal Co. (Vic.), com-
plaining of the competition of the
State coal-mine, says that the latter
wrote off a deficit of £50,000. But,
added Boyd, it didn’t matter whether
it was written off or not —it was a
dead loss. This has given Peter
Mcßride a chance to say that it was
not £50,000, but £21,000; and even
that much wasn’t dead loss, because
the opening of the State mine had
added £300.000 to the land values of
the district in which Wonthaggi is
situated. And the special beauty
of that feat lies in the fact that this
increased value, like the mine itself,
belongs to the State, and not private
landowners. Which, of course, is the
annoying part of it. Fat doesn’t
mind the State spending twice or 20
times £50,000 as long as the money
adds to the value of his own private
property.

The Bulletin recently noted (30/3/’ll) that at a recent
referendum Geelong (Vic.) discarded its ancient, and
rotten Corporation Act by a majority of 645 votes ; but
nobody who knows anything of the Geelong Town Coun-
cil would fall into the error of supposing that any notice
will be taken of the people’s wishes by the fat fathers of
the hamlet. At a recent meeting of the council a motion
was brought forward that J. Weary be informed of the
result of the poll, and asked to arrange for a Bill to give
effect to the wishes of ratepayers. This motion was cast
into outer darkne-s, all the aldermen—who would cease
to exist under the Local Government Act—voting against
However, as acting-Premier Willie Watt is sus-
pected of being able to read, it seems not impos-
sible that he will find out the result of the local
referendum even if the council doesn’t tell him.
The “No’’-bodies in Melbourne must
have had a heap of somebody’s money to play
with. Never was so' much of the Devil’s oof
misapplied over a general election. The railway
stations and public places were plastered with
“No ” posters, and “No ” pictures and pamph-
lets were strevvn from one end of the State to the
other. Intelligent electors had only to turn to
those posters to see that the intellectual back-
number was fighting for “ No.” One vast colored
cartoon pictured that inspired novelty, the man
sawing off a tree limb on which he himself was
perched. Another daringly original conception
represented the dog dropping his bone to grab a
shadowy hunk of grub. A third was a fresh and
ingenious invention in the shape of an octopus.
These fire the blue-mouldy chestnuts of the car-
tooning art. and the party using them advertises
nothing but its mental poverty. Only people
with “No ” brains were influenced by these efforts
of the party of “No ” ideas.

The Argus, which used to believe that at
any rate it was respectable, threw even that
doubtful virtue overboard during the Referenda
campaign. At Perth Andrew Fisher said
1 believe that we will carry these proposals ivith a
majority that will make them law and part of the Con-
stitution, but I say, in the presence of two of my col-
leagues, that we will not despair even if they are not
carried. We, as a party, are going to; uphold the princi-
ples despite any rebuff.
Whereupon the Argus printed the yarn with the
headings:—
The Referendum, G
Mr. Fisher Fears Defeat.
No doubt Fisher did “ fear defeat” ; but he didn’t
expect it, though the Argus knew well enough
that that is what nine out of.'lo of its ignorant
readers would assume. If, in reply, Fisher had
scattered a few million “dodgers” to the
effect that “ The Argus Fears the Prosecution of
its Editor for Bigamy,” the old paper would pro-
bably be annoyed, and it might ev£n refer angrily
to the disgraceful way these lower orders fight.
Sample advt. screeched in Victoria during
the last days of the Referenda campaign “If
you don’t want to be taxed from N. S. Wales,
vote ‘No!’” Heaven only knows what the
Referenda had to do with taxation ; but it at least
shows that the “No ” appeal was an appeal to the
oldest and narrowest anti-Federal provincialism.
A WONDERFUL FAMILY LIKENESS.
“ Hie[?] ’S[?]use me, gentlemen, are you
brothers? ”
John McFarlane, of the Clarence, com-
pletes his quarter-century in the N.S.W. Assem-
bly this year. The only other sitting members
who were in the House when lie arrived are
R. H. Levien, C. A. Lee, Wm. McCourt, and
John Nohbs.
Australia didn’t always depend, as it does
now, on Ram Chunder for bags in which to put its
wheat. Victoria owns a veteran Scot (85 not out,
and still batting well) who can give evidence in
this matter. He is James Miller, who, away
back in ’53, founded the rope-making business
now carried on by Jas. Miller and Co. Pty., Ltd.
His firm in the ’6o’s started to make woolpaeks
and cornsaeks, spinning their own yarn and
weaving their own cloth, and for a time did very
well. That was before the manufacture was com-
menced in India; but as soon as that happened
the infant white industry went down before
“ black ” jute, and took a pile of capital with it.
J. M. Speed, a Gent One, put the
Referenda campaign to a quaint use in Wes-
tralia. He printed a “Yes, Yes” pamphlet
whose argument really hung on this hook
Mr. Deakin brought into 6ur world his pet twins—
measures against combines and in favor of New Protec-
tion. Now, because he and his friends ,are not to be
allowed to wet nurse ihem, he and they are trying their
utmost to strangle these same little offspring.
So lie titled his pamphlet “The Waifs and the
Referenda,” and announced, with keen appro-
priateness, that the money derived from its safe
would be devoted to the support of the Home for
abandoned infants at Parkerville.
The news is published that when the
Powerful is relieved by the Drake at the end of
the year, the floating ballroom of Farm Cove is
to roll home to England to be dismantled and
converted into a hulk. So the bad old Imperial
idea that the Australian Station is next door to
the naval scrapheap has endured to the very last.
The other subsidy ships, which hang themselves
about the harbor when they are not tramping
like sore-footed policemen around the Islands,
will probably go the same way when their
time comes to up anchor. The First Lord only
knows what the British Navy—especially the
part it hires out by the day to oversea “ Liberal ”
Governments —would look, like but for the eter-
nal paint brush. '• Anyway, the prompt ruling-out
of the ramshackle flagship on completion of its
present commission should encourage; all good
Australians to cheerfully face the bill for their own
modern ships, and to stand behind the Labor party
to whose efforts the change is due. “Liberalism’s”
idea was to defend Australia by increasing the
subsidy for whatever herd of lame ships J. Bull
liked to send us, and by purchasing one unmanned,
gunless battleship for Channel service with the
donations of title-hungry clubmen and the beer
and lolly money of smaller patriots. And it held
tenaciously to that, livery-stable ideal of defence
till the absence of public enthusiasm, the broad
hints of the Admiralty and the vigorous shoves of
the Labor Party made it crayfish along the straight
and obvious course towards responsibility and
safety. The well-meaning but futile person who
was willing, in the frenzied days of the newspaper
subscription lists, to defend the Empire with the
price of a bottle of lager, and the small, green
Gor’saver who was ready to Stick the Union Jack
to the mast with one cake of chewing gum, may
now be considered extinct.
When -T. C. Watson announced his inten-
tion of let iring from politics, the anti-Labor press
made endless capital of the assertion that the
Labor party was going too far for him, atid’ that
he had decided to retire rather than support an
extreme policy with which he was not in con-
currence. The extremists, whom he could no
longer check, were driving him out, it was said.
Then, to prove that the Tory.press was the same
old prevaricator, Watson put in strenuous work
at the Federal and State elections, and wound up
by leading the “Forward” section in the State
Labor Conference which gave Holman, McGowen
and the other ’bus-missers a bad hour or two.
There is a batch of political libel actions
incubating in Westralia. A while ago, pretry
soon after the Wilson Government had found out
that its reliable majority was something under
two, it was impressed with the urgency of redis-
tributing the Assembly seats.- A bill was accord-
ingly put through ; and when the mostly-Labor
goldfields constituencies saw how they were to he
treated the Kalgoorlie Minkr dropped in an
article on “ Three Rotten Sticks ” —the allegedly
rotten props being the three goldfields members
who were lassoed and harnessed into Wilson's
team. Now the three Anti-Sosh props—Gregory,
Connolly and McKenzie—have sent along in
timations that they are dissatisfied with the unfair
distribution of wealth in Westralia, and wantsome
of Hocking’s and Kirwan’s.
Before leaving for London lou Ward sold
many of his belongings (horses, carriages and the
like), and gave other indications that he desires a
rest from Maoriland politics. The job of Jones,
the present ailing and feeble High Commissioner,
would probably just about match his inclinations.
Lots of things considered, Joseph is possibly the
best man Maoriland could provide as its High
Commissioner. To discover another Premier in
his party will be the difficulty. Jimmy Carroll
(“Wait a Bit”), the Acting-Premier, would not
be able to last as head of ; the Ministry, and the
once-powerful Millar (Minister for Labor) is now
very doubtful. Fowlds, Minister of Education, is
perhaps the nearest approach the Cabinet con-
tains to a possible Premier; but he lacks experi-
ence, as well as a great many otlier things.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
So Sydney Kemp was not a dark horse,
after all. Or, perhaps, it might be fairer to say
that he was not sufficiently dark to douse the glim
of champion Pearce, who led the whole way, and
finished, looking as though he had not been fairly
extended, by the huge margin of 20 lengths. There
are some spectators who say that he won by only
12 lengths; but as the judge said 20, well, it was
20, and there’s an end on ’t. But although Kemp
was beaten, he pulled a game race and a fast one,
undeterred by the fact that the first mile had proved
to him that he was up against a snag. After that
distance it was obvious that, barring a miracle,
Kemp’s name was Slush—he had no hope unless the
champion suddenly contracted paralysis. In fact,
the race was so one-sided that it soon ceased to be
interesting, for a dog-walloping is not sport. There
must be some bad judges of sculling in charge of the
game when such an obviously hopeless chance is
traded off under the disguise of a championship race.
It only wants a few repeats of this sort of thing,
and sculling will be back at the dead-house.
Before Pearce landed after his victory, he was
informed that an ambitious youngster had challenged
him. Pearce remarked that the young man would
probably have “to wait.” Meantime the wireless
station was sending waves out over the Tasman Sea,
requesting a word with one Richard Arnst. And the
wandering wave, speeding eastward, bumped the
Ulimaroa, 920 miles away from the Hotel Aus-
tralia, and communicated the news to him. Arnst
was gratified, and in response said he would meet
Pearce for £5OO a-side and the championship, in 10
weeks. On this news being imparted to Pearce, he
said he would be delighted to row the champion—
in fact, there was no other champion he would sooner
row. So now all he has to do is raise £5OO, get
into condition, and do the trick. Thus history is
made. And, presumably, the smaller fry that put
£25 deposit to bind a match with Pearce as winner,
must feel properly and correctly crushed. Arnst
was on his way from Wellington (M.L.) to Sydney
when he received the aerogram.
Of the 24 races in the four days of the
A.J.O. meeting, only eight were won by horses which
could be labelled favorites, and amongst the other 16
were some of the wildest outsiders ever known. The
army of Maoriland bookmakers driven forth by a
sensible betting law made their first plunge into the
Sydney sea of flats, and so beautiful did they find it
that they wonder why they didn’t come before. The
saddling paddock betting fee for Randwick is £75
per annum, and Patrick Noses doesn’t mind paying a
mite like that to enter Tom Tiddler’s ground. Mean-
while, has anybody heard anything of Papa Levien
and his Tote Bill?
Comedy King endured two sound beat-
ings on Wednesday. A fat-headed holiday crowd
simply piled its money on him for the All-Aged
Stakes (one mile), despite the fact that he was in
the unproven class as a sprinter. In the final upshot
Sol Green’s crack finished a bad sixth, after being
backed down to 13 to eight on. He went out at
evens for the Cumberland Stakes, and succumbed to
both Trafalgar and Byplay. The beautiful litttle
Persimmon horse never looked better. Simply he
wasn’t class enough at the weights and distances he
was asked to negotiate. The joy of Israel was ac-
centuated by the fact that these two “skinners” dis-
posed of innumerable doubles.
On Saturday onlv two favorites rolled home, and
one of them (Trafalgar) was a seven to one on pro-
position. A tragic meeting for punters closed ap-
propriately with the victory of Reg, a 100 to one
chance, whose name was not so much as whispered
in the hearing of one Nose out of 50.
The books’ profits on these two days alone would,
if pooled, probably suffice to get all'N.S.W.’s finan-
cially-muddled hospitals out of debt, and build a few
new ones to boot. The cash would go a considerable
way towards paying for the Government’s proposed
“free” ferry service; invested, the interest on it
would provide pensions for an army of aged citizens.
All of which advertises yet again that N.S.W. is
deliberately foregoing a vast income because it
hearkens to the Wowser-caro-bookie clamor against
the Tote. The most righteous of “Liberal” Govern-
rpents have accepted the principle that the fool-
public must be encouraged to drop its hard-earned
cash somehow at race-meetings. Surely in the cir-
cumstances it might be allowed to drop the coin into
the State’s pockets, instead of into those of Private
Enterprise.
Apropos, how many bookmakers give a proportion
of the dead-easy money they make to hospitals or
any other sort of charity ? Painful as it is for a
White Australian paper to chronicle, the heathen
Chow disburses about 14 times more in this direction
than the typical frequenter of Tatt’s.
* * * *
The aged show-jumper, Skylark, holder of
the water-jump record at Sydney Royal Show, put
up his “final” last week, when he cleared 29ft. 3in.,
which was 3ft. more than the next best “lepper.”
Then he dropped dead. The judge tied the blue rib-
band round the corpse’s neck, and that was the
last of a fine animal, which has delighted thousands
of Show-goers.
* * * *
More than the customary crop of dream-
ers has arisen to claim victory and winnings over
the win. of Moorilla in the Sydney Cup. It is in-
dustriously spread about that, far from doing well
over the Sydney Cup, Christian Abrahams was in
reality fleeced by the seers of visions, one of whom
is said to have raked in £5OOO, another £2OOO,
and another £l5OO. This sort of guff is presumably
spread by the pcncillers to create an impression that
the meeting, which was generally considered to have
been as near a skinner of the public as history wots
of, was rather good for the knowing backers. The
amount of betting done was tremendous; the big
backers of horses had worked out a regular cam-
paign, which, had it come off, would have landed
some of them as much as £30,000. This country
is a land of big bets these days, and the profits of
those who bet against the propljpts are expanding
accordingly.
After the debacle at Randwick recently,
there is something tantalising about the Maoriland
tote records. At five of the chief meetings at
Easter-tide £165,782 passed through the machine,
while last year, with the bookmakers in opposition,
the amount was £102,644. This increase of £60,000
probably represents the share that Christian Noses
used to rake in from the old Conservatives and young
fools, and not an increase in the actual betting.
The chief point is, of course, that, barring the 11*
per cent, tax, all the money went back to the public
and not into the pockets of a ring small in numbers
but vast at the waist-line.
L. W. Pye. the Parramatta cricketer,
after being excluded from the Ma State’s representa-
tive 11, turned his cash and attention to motoring.
To-day he is the accredited prize-hogger in the
N.S.W. motor reliability contests, and might have
held the Tasmanian hoggership but for an accident.
Recently he conceived the inspiration of taking his
ear to the Apple Island and racing across the
orchard ; but on reaching Hobart the car was acci-
dentally dropped overboard between the steamer and
the wharf. It is Hobart’s boast that there is more
water alongside that wharf than at any other wharf
in the world, and whether that is true or not, Pye
found it deep enough to go on with.
* * * .
Though L. W. Pye thus struck ill-luck,
and had all his chances of winning the two days’
reliability Tasmanian trophy spoiled, Dr. Collins
gathered in the prize for the Ma State. So the
chemist’s hard luck was the doctor’s gain.
* * * *
H. A. Parker, the N.S.W. tennis player,
last week added two Tasmanian championships to
his numerous stock. In the Singles he defeated H.
Hunt for the title, but joined with him in the
doubles to smash up Manby and Brownell, who repre-
sented the State. Parker has now gathered in over
a century of prizes, championships, and other things
of that sort, and is seemingly good for any number
more.
* * * •
As everybody knows, hoof-ball is taken
mighty seriously in Maoriland, and the seriousness
is on the increase. In wet, windy, slushy Welling-
ton, which was created as a prison for Satan before
Sheol was thought of, the Rugby Union last year
had no less than 2000 players under its control.
This season it is said there will be nearly half as
many again, so, apparently, the windy city is yearn-
ing to kick something, and probably isn’t very par-
ticular what the something is. If the same progress
is general over the Ricketty Isles, the atmosphere
this winter will be simply filled with biff and flying
boots.
* ■* * *
The rush for a chance to get into the
English-going Rugby League team was, is, and
will be terrific. The officials are at bay like men
surrounded by wolves, and the wary secretary Larkin
proposes to arrange to die in his bed after a peace-
able old age, by sending a band across the Pacific
to missionise the Yankees. Of course, although the
nominal object is to teach the Yankees the new game
and thus widen the field of international matches,
Hie real obiect is to save the lives or minds of the
officials. They are having the time of their lives.
* * * *
The Sydney daily pressman who recently
wrote that enthusiasm was “running at fever heat
in the Rugby League,” wrote truthfully. It has
been running at fever heat; wherefore, although
only practice matches have so far been played, men
have been sent off the field and threatened with more
senenis punishments for displaying a tendency to use
the fist on the player instead of merely applying the
boot to the ball. One referee had evidently been
designed by the Almighty for a pressman, as he
boiled his yarn about the disturbances down to
something like this: “Smith v. Jones.—Ordered
both men off the field —equally to blame; but Smith
far and away the worst.” What a State politician
was lost in that man!
* * * *
Consider the mighty Rugby League and
how it eclipses Jonah’s gourd. This coming Satur-
day it will open its first-grade competition
with eight well-balanced over-manned clubs; on
Saturday week it will open the second-grade com-
petition with 16 teams, an increase of four on last
year’s number ; and the same day will also see the
start of the third-grade with 23 teams, an increase
of seven for this season. There were other entrants
banging on the mat and hammering at the panels,
but the League shooed them into the juniors or any-
where out of sight. The question is, Will it burst
—about the head ?
Next Saturday the “muddied oaf” will
get busy in Melbourne. The “reform” movement
has practically fizzled out, and the League has ar-
rived at a dead end re the question of open payment.
A majority of the delegates favors rescinding the “no
payment” rule —which is defiantly broken—and an-
other majority desires to fix the payments that can
be made to players; but as neither majority is equal
to the statutory three-fourths, the 1 motions of both
parties have lapsed. So the season starts much as
last year’s did, save that the League has admitted
the existence of a rule that it daren’t enforce.
As a test of grit and stamina, commend
us to d motor-bike reliabilitv ride. In a car one
can make himself fairly comfortable as long as the
cold can be kept out. But in a motor-bike there
is no shelter, and in bad weather, on bad roads, it
is just plain Sheol. The Motor Cycle Club of N.S.W.
held a run during Easter from Sydney to Nowra and
back. Only five managed to struggle through the
awful conditions to Nowra, where they found more
rain; and as the wire spoke of rain right along the
route, three of the five retired and made home by
train. The steadfast two were Norman Saunders, on
a _3*-h.p. Bradbury, and L. J, Astley, on a 3*-h.p.
Triumph. They left Nowra in blinding rain and a
torrent of expostulations. For six hours they battled
along the greasy roads, with the rain beating in
their faces and the machines skidding and slipping
all over the place. When they made Albion Park
they were “all out,” and there was nothing for it
but to retire.
“A Man Who Was There” : Hitherto the
übiquitous motor-car has swallowed the best of the
world’s rubber supplies, and the price of rubber has
run mad. A year ago it touched 12s. per lb., which
is a sinful sum of money to pay out on wheel-tires
weighing anywhere up to 251 b. apiece; and even now
the price is painful, unless your name is Rockefeller.
But this is going to be altered, and an Australian
is going to do it. A few days ago, at Manly, near
Sydney, three others and I witnessed a trial of the
new “Hyde” motor-wheel. The wheel we saw, and
looked over, and rode over, is about as unlike the
ordinary wheel as the modem golf-ball is unlike
the old feathers-and-leather contrivance. The car
runs on a number of turns of %-incli wire-rope,
forming a “tread,” between which and the felloe
are placed a number of spiral springs, attached,
radially, to a steel channel-rim fastened to the
felloe. The steel sections securing the springs are
held in position by rubber cushions, which prevent
displacement and take up the shock. The cost is
from one-tenth to one-eighth of the old wheel!
And the “life” is anywhere from three to six months.
The car- —an old-type Daimler, of 12-15-h.p.—has
been running for the last two months. It has covered
more than 1200 miles, and, so far, the wheel shows
little or no signs of wear and tear, beyond the
snapping of two or three strands on the outmost coil
of wire cable. As the rope is laid in the direction
of the wheel’s rotation, this snapping is, so far,
immaterial—and, anyway, wire rope doesn’t cost
much. And it runs as smoothly as one of Bruce
Smith’s speeches.
Saturday and a fairly big crowd saw the
end of Melbourne’s cricket season, when P’ran de-
feated Northcote by 69 runs on the first innings,
and collared, the pennant and whatever glory waves
at the tail of it. Previous week-end, in the semi-
finals, the same P’ran had walloped East Melbourne,
and Northcote had passed Carlton its first defeat
this season; and on Tuesday the pair met to con-
clude the discussion. P’ran won the toss, and
cautiously put together 208 (Stephens 77, and Bell
60). Northcote had an hour’s batting, and succeeded
in losing three for 37 —which left the rest of the
team much to ponder over while awaiting Saturday.
When they did resume they made almost as dis-
astrous a show, only F. Yeomans (40, not out) doing
anything notable, and the last stump fell for 139.
P’ran’s premiership win is mainly due to its pos-
session of a few good bowlers, notably Kyle, Bell
and Beath (five for 44 was Kyle’s record for the
final match) ; but irs far as consistent play goes,
Carlton is head and collar above the rest. Northcote,
the baby club of the V.C.A., did well to run-up.
Last year’s premiers, St. Kilda, adorned the bottom
of the list with a dozen or so points less than any.
A curious irregularity occurred during ’Cote’s
innings in this final game. One of the umpires
allowed ’Pran bowler Rogers to deliver 12 consecu-
tive balls. He got a wicket with the fifth, but the
second “over” or half of the over was a maiden.
Which circumstance fortunately saved much argu-
ment and a possible protest.
W. W. Naughton. the ex-Maorilander,
who is now one of the best known of United States
sporting Press writers, and in between times presi-
dent of the San Francisco Press Club, is on a visit to
Sydney after side-stepping it for 18 years. “Uncle
Bill” reckons to stop three weeks in the city; and as
he weighs about 18 stone, stands 6ft. and a few
inches, and is in the pink of health, he seems quite
capable of holding up, even under a flood of enter-
tainment, for that time.
* * * *
“Petramos”: Adelaide is beginning to be-
lieve that the “hope of the white race” will be
found in its local champion, young Ernest Waade-
witz, known in the ring as Waddy. Waadewitz has
the cool, calm style of Mistah Johnson; he stands
in the ring like a statue, till instinct tells him to
go in and slaughter. Recently he easily beat Tom
Dunn, who held the welterweight championship of
the State, and who fouled badly in the fifth round.
On Easter Saturday Waddy made Livingstone look
cheap, an'd knocked him out in the eighth round,
and as Livingstone is the next best thing to Waddy
the City of Churches can produce (he had never
been beaten previously), it is now up to some inter-
state gentleman to come along and be biffed. Waddy
has never yet suffered defeat. He is a baker and a
philosopher. Says he: “It’s a good game while
you’re winning;” “Micko” Walsh (of whom The
Bulletin once said: “Put lOoz. gloves on him, or
don’t let him fight—he’s out to kill”) has taught
Waddy much, and now says he can teach him no
more.
Bill Carrie, whose amateur successes
tempted him into the professional circle, had his
features still further maltreated in his mill with
“Nugget” Scanlon, at Ballarat, a few evenings ago.
Carrie went lOst. 101 b., and Scanlon list. 51b. The
big person jumped for William, “as mountain cat that
guards her young,” and Carrie bore up against his
troubles as best he could. Scanlon’s burst of
energy did not survive the judicious prods his rival
administered now and again, and the fifteenth round
saw-Carrie a lap ahead. William won on points,
and his hope of one day fighting for Australia in
U.S.A. is revived.
Lang’s story of his disqualification in the
Langford fight is pathetic. ■ According to his yarn,
the negro did not fall or slip, but was well and
truly sent to the boards with a good punch. Lang
stood over him ready td hit him as soon as he got
up, and when he came up Lang swung. Langford’s
manager yelled to his man to drop, and he dropped,
and as Lang couldn’t stop the blow, he was dis-
qualified. But what an amazingly quick individual
Langford must be • to get up and go down again
while Lang was hitting one biff. He evidently has
his goings up and down under more control than
Lang has his swipes.
“Eryander” : As one who has made prac-
tical experiment in the matter, 'this wrestler v.
boxer controversy interests me much. The question
is not at all a new one. Nearly 40 years ago, when
I was considered a pretty good “fistist,” I had a
friend who, being an excellent wrestler, maintained
that he could easily get away with any boxer of his
weight—three or four pounds over my then lOst. 71b.
We had frequently argued the point in Jem Mace’s
presence, and one day the old ’un suggested that we
should put the matter to actual test, in the presence
of a small, but knowing, spectatorate. My friend’s
style was Cumberland-and-Westmoreland, which
counts a fall when ground is touched by any part
of the body (even a finger-tip) except the feet.
Well, we tried it, and for five rounds I prevented
him from getting a hold. But it was a mighty hard
job, for he kept circling round to my left so rapidly
that I could never get a really loaded punch on to
him. But in the sixth round I suddenly wheeled
in the reverse direction, and met him with a right
to the nose that made him sit down with great sud-
denness. But, in the very act of rising, he flung
himself forward under my guard, got a good grip,
and threw me a cracker. When proceedings re-
commenced, my head was buzzing like a bee-hive,
but it remained clear, and I ‘ blocked the circus-
business by backing to a corner of the ring, away
from which I hit him again and again with straight
lefts. But my fall had hurt me severely ; the steam
went out of my blows, and presently the twister-man
got a chance and threw me another burster. Then
Mace called a halt, and “spoke a pieced” which
might almost have inspired some recent Bulletin
remarks on the subject. Quoth the redoubtable
James: “This ain’t a fair go. To even matters up,
there oughtn’t to be no gloves at the boxin’ end of
the show. There’s no gloves on them hard boards.”
“All right,” said my friend, now beginning to be a
bit cock-a-whoop, “let him take off the gloves. It
won’t make a scrap of difference.” But it made all
the difference. He got a comparatively light left
on the mouth, lost his temper, rushed recklessly in
to get hold, and received the same hand on the same
spot with a poundage that burst open both his lip?,
sent him staggering across the ring, and opened his
eyes to the possibilities of the stripped fist. He was
so astonished that I really believe he had never been
“hit naked” before. At all events, he would have
no more of it, and never “barracked” for wrestling
again.
N. S. Wales bowlers had a great time
last week, cleaning up Queensland and Victoria in
one swoop. Tasmania, of course, had been wiped
out previously, so the Australian championship stays
in Sydney. And the Sydney bowlers are hard at
work trying to get their hats on.
There was a remarkable finish for the
Barambah Plate at the recent Nanango (Q.) amateur
races. Six nags started, and the judge declared that
four of them dead-heated for second place.
Harry Holmes, killed on Easter Monday
at Oakbank (S.A.) “picnic” meeting (his wife and
three children Were part of the picnic), had been
talking of retiring for years, but every big event
saw him in the saddle. He went in the G. N.
Hurdles, an event he had won four times. Unlike
most jockeys, he hated wasting time between races,
so carried on business as a furniture remover.
The big Easter meeting of peds. at Stawell
(Vic.) was notable for the appearance of*champion
sprinter Jack Donaldson, and erst champion runner
Arthur Postle. Neither, however, annexed any of
the oof attached to the big events. In the Sprint
Handicap (75yds.), both failed to accomplish the
needful in their heats, Postle unhappily straining a
tendon in his hip. In the Easter Gift (130yds.,
first prize, £120), Postle lost his heat, and Donald-
son, who gained his by a brilliant run, missed secur-
ing a place in the'final scamper. The race was won
by D. 11. Devine in 11 2-ssec.
Suggested “motter” for Bill Lang in his
forthcoming fight with Lester in Sydney: “It is
better to give than to receive.”
The ■ Royal Life-saving- Society, whosp
awards cover the surface of the Empire, has expanded
into a huge concern. The society first saw light in
1892, with a membership (accepted only by axam.)
of 86; in 1910, the total reached 11,779, of which
3484 had been awarded bronze medallions, 308
awards of merit, 11 diplomas, and the balance various
grades of certificates. Australasia has a big hand in
the society, surf-bathing and life-saving proficiency
accounting for the bulk of the awards.
At the conclusion of the coming cricket
season in England, Albert Trott will probably re-
turn to the Commonwealth. He is still doing
well for Middlesex, though his glory isn’t what it
was a few seasons back.

Also at Melbourne Brisbane
“ Curse o’ Moses” : A line argument for
military training is being advanced m the S. M.
Herald by certain ancient volunteers who wore
armor in the Australian Middle Ages. One
veteran, in bis pride of years and service, wrote
to say that lie had good reason for thinking him-
self the oldest trailer of a rifle in the State.
Immediately a shoal of other antique persons
sent in letters stating that they had been in vari-
ous companies since the beginning of the nine-
teenth century, and knew others, still living, who
were there at the same time or even earlier. Ihe
general impression gathered from the correspond-
ence is that most of the old volunteers are still
hale and hearty, and that soldiering in Australia
is a marvellous preservative of health. With our
whole male population trained along better lines
tliMn were possible in the slipshod dciys of volun-
teering, centenarians should be as common as
colds; and it is just possible that here and there
we may have a patriarchal athlete challenging
Methuselah to an “ all in ” contest for the cham-
pionship in longevity—if the .Taps don’t come too
soon.
When John Weary has finished looking on
at the Coronation he and McLeod, the newly-
appointed Victorian immigration agent, are to
foregather with Agent-GeneralTaverner, and the
three are to look up some domestic servants and
select the “ right sort of girl,” as Fat’s missus puts
it. John is going to find it a worrying job; for
the “right sort of girl” is always dead—she
always died out about 30 years ago.
When Dig. Denham recently toured
Queensland,' he cried perpetually: “No, No!”
and referred to the blessings of Home Rule and
the curses of centralisation. While he was in
Townsville the Northerners took him at his word,
and, having pointed out that Townsville was fur-
ther from Brisbane than either Adelaide or Syd-
ney is from Melbourne, asked him to appoint a
Deputy-Railway Commissioner for the northern
system. Thereupon Dig-his-own-Grave Denham
promptly fell in by saying that he didn’t see any
necessity for it at all, and that a commissioner in
Brisbane, with a traffic manager at Townsville,
answered all purposes admirably.

The Point of View.
There are two ways of winning a cycle race—one is to come in first from a mark,
the other is to do the fastest time off scratch ; and the latter method carries with it
all the prestige.
At a recent test on Kowen Hill, Queanbeyan, a well-known motor-cycle got in
Erst in lmin 23secs. ; but THE REAL TRIUMPH was gained by Bennett & Wood’s
Speedwell Model “A”
'which negotiated the hill from scratch in linin'. 19$secs., and thus won the fastest
i itime. “The pace was truly terrific, and if the killing grade, round dangerous corners,
:rouky boulders and deep gutters be taken into account, just what such a ride means
■will be appreciated.”
The Speedwell Model “A” is h.p., and is the most reliable hill climber
on the road.
Come and have a look at it.
BENNETT & WOOD, ETD.
Corner of Pitt and Bathurst Streets, Sydney.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will b&
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany.
Old Sub.: The fact that the Labor party
held the balance of power in that Parliament was
proved by the fact that w'hen it transferred its sup-
port from Reid to Lyne, Lyne came in and Reid
went out. Neither had a majority without the
third party.... Bellerive : From the latest batch of

AFTER THE DANCE.
After the dance was over and the music ceased to
play,
A young couple along the sandy beach did stray ;
A gay rival had danced with the damsel fair
Which had aroused her lover to jealous Despair ;
lie slew her and consigned her corpse to the sea,
Then sped from the spot of his cruel Tragedy.
But ’twas witnessed by God. The remains of the
Dead
Was cast up by the waves for the burial dread.
Upon the murderer fell the hand of the Law
For his deed of the night on the bleak sea-shore.

THE BUSH-WOMAN.
She’s fresh from the hills.
Gaunt and thin, no frills,
Hungry : comes on market days.
One black tooth a smile betrays. , ,
Got religion young,
Overtight she clung.
Immured in a balmicide.
Down there her kind often slide.
But an old friend took her home,
Severely stopped her mind’s roam.
Love and sternness cured disease,
Where doctors procured no ease.
It was loneliness—as much:—
To someone woman must clutch,
Her husband failed, womanlike;
She clung to God, loverlike.
She’s mothered a family.
1 daresay that God will see
That her girls get strong husbands
Strong minded wives, her boys land.

“ Carmine ’’ wanders round :—-
Opitz’s Cafd, in Russell-street, Melbourne, is opposite
the Pekin Oafb. Opitz ooufageotisly sticks up:
“No Asiatics Served.” Supping tliere last night, I was
deliciously surprised with a painting by'John Hennings
on the plaster ot' the wall. It is 6ft. high and 12ft. long.
The subject is “ Sunset on the Yarra,” opposite the
Botanical Gardens. Painted in 1880, the picture was
damaged in 1896 by stabs from some idiot boozican, but
was skilfully repaired by artist Johnston, w'ho appends his
name with pride. The painting is a masterpiece. There
is a blood-red sunset, and the effect is splendid
when the afternoon sun shines in through a plate-
glass window. Hennings had evidently calculated on
that window. I fancy there is a little poetic license
with the spire of the Scots Church and Dr. Bevan’s Cam-
panile in the dreamy, hazy distance. Hennings was a
real artist —not a scene-painter by profession. He had
made a good start in DiLseldorf, but came to Melbourne,
under a delusion, like Summers, the sculptor of genius,
that there was an opening. Summers only got one
chance w'orth having—the Burke and Wills statue. Hen-
nings was luckier, though he had to go out of his higher
walk. Among his last works were the scenery for Miln’s
“Antony and Cleopatra,” and that of “Theodora” for
Sarah Bernhardt, the latter afterwards used for Tittell
Brurie. Mrs. Hennings was an excellent dancer. Hir
appeared seldom. Hans Phillips married their dam: in r.
“ Mosman ’’ and the Enterprise that “ can
always do the thing better ” :
On the evening of the day the dreadful news came out
that the Ma State’s Labor Government proposed to run
two lines of ferries to North Shore, I was trying to read
my paper on one of the homeward-bound arks. All about
me, people with some respect for their eyesight were
folding up their Evening Bloodstains and murmuring
against the sort of illumination provided by the Private
Enterprise co. At last one man groped his way out of a
recess and politely asked a shadowy engine-man if he
couldn’t turn on another volt or two. The engine-man,
whose feelings no doubt had been lacerated by the slight
fall in the co.’s shares, curtly replied that the boat
wasn’t a reading-room. The passenger thanked him and
withdrew. Some months ago 1 was travelling up the
hill in the Government tram which meets the Private
Enterprise boat, when another passenger close by was
handed some such impertinence by a numbered tram
collector. The passenger, without further argument,
produced a pencil and paper and carefully took a note of
the figures on the uncivil servant’s cap. I haven’t seen
that collector since. It is waste of time for the Ferries’
management to try and persuade the public that it is a
desirable monopoly by knocking a trifle off the periodica!
tickets if the public are to get heated remarks when they
respectfully ask for illumination. A heated remark
doesn’t throw any light on the evening paper.

that he came near dying. The rash Would
itch so that he would scratch till the blood
ran, and a thin yellowish stuff would be all
over his pillow in the morning. I had to put
mittens on his hands to prevent him tearing
his skin. He was so weak and run down that
he took fainting spells as if he were dying.
He was almost a skeleton and his little hands
were thin like claws.
“He was bad about eight months when we
tried Cuticura Remedies. I had not laid him
down in his cradle in the daytime for a long

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Silk”: The N.S.W., Full Court unloosed what
' looks like bad justice the other day in an appeal by
the Sydney Milk and Ice Co. against a verdict of
Judge Murray. Originally, a woman in a Sydney
suburb, peddling milk in a microscopic way, was
fined £5 and costs for selling watered goods. Seeing
that she sold the goods just as it was received from
the co.—not the cow—she sued the co. for damages,
and was awarded '£B 9s. for breach of warranty
and £ll 11s. for damage to her business. The co. ap-
pealed to the Full Court against this verdict;, it ad-
mitted the £8 95., but: resisted the £ll 11s. as
being bad in law—too “remote” to be calculated.
The Full Court upheld the point as to “remoteness,”
refused any damages except as to breach of warranty
(£8 95. ), and made no order as to costs. But
surely damage to business reputation through pur-
veying inferior or illegal' or murderous goods, which
have been bought and paid for as good goods, isn’t
so remote that it can’t be calculated. Anyhow, a
Judge, and sometimes a jury, assesses the damage
done or supposed to have been done to a person’s
reputation by a libel; and the Judge or jury that
can do that job should be able to do the other one
in its spare time, so easy is it by comparison.
* * * *
Lately the councillors of P’ran (Vic.) invited
tenders for wood-blocking one of the main thorough-
fares, a job that their engineer estimated worth
£l9BO. The lowest tender being for £2240, the
engineer was told to get busy himself. He did so,
and finished the’job for £1728. The difference of.
£512 represented the tribute which Private Enter-
prise expected to collect, from the ratepayers. No
wonder Private Enterprise says day labor is a great
mistake.
* * * *
A Melbourne citizen held the Law off the other
day, and dared it to do its worst, and presently left
it in a subdued < frame ;pf mind, with an awkward
conviction that it had been too precipitate and
couldn’t really do anything. To start with, one of
its minions noticed a man staggering along under a
double wire-mattress. He stopped the staggerer and
inquired the history of the couch. The man told
him to mind his own business, whereupon the intel-
ligent pfficer minded it by running him in. Being
subsequently asked by the-Law to explain his pos-
session of an article “reasonably supposed to have
been stolen,” he curtly repeated to the Law the ad-
vice about minding its own business—or, at any
rate he urged that it might leave his alone until it
had something substantial to build a suspicion on.
It nearly exploded at this; but as he firmly declined
to discuss the wire mattress, and as nobody could be
discovered who had lost such a utensil, the Law
1 frowned and told him he’d better go.

The N.S.W. country shop-assistants .are battling
for decent conditions, and- when the public reads
slabs of the, evidence given before the Wages Board
at Wollongong, it probably wonders why thev didn’t
kick before. The head carter at a" co-operative
South- Coast store told a startled Board that he
worked 86 hours a week for 455. (the Uuion is asking
for 555.). The second carter, who, presumably, bogs
in about as long as the first, staggers home with
a munificent 355. Another employee, after nine
years’ experience, toils 53 hours for the same
wages. The head of a grocery and ironmongery
department was overwhelmed with a weekly tW'«
guineas, while the manager of a branch had climbed
up to a dizzy £2 15s. and house rent. Several of
these witnesses were employed by a co-operative
store whose turnover for. the past four months
was over £15,000, and which paid a 10 per cent,
dividend. Altogether conditions revealed in the co-
operative stores gave the shareholders, manv of whom
.are unionists,; good reason fo feel ashamed .of them-,
selves.
“Wealsman”: Why is fruit sold retail by the lb.
in some places and by the dozen in others ? Two
cities, even in the same State, may, and do, adopt
different methods. For instance, Hobart sells its
apples by the dozen; Launceston, 130 miles away,
by the lb. Sydney quotes by the dozen, Melbourne
by weight. New Yorkers buy their table-fruit at
so many cents per .pippin; Londoners, at so many
pence, or “fardins” per' lb. 1 don’t think such
fruit as oranges or pineapples are sold by the lb.
in London or Melbourne or Launceston or anywhere
else, and I never heard of grapes or cherries being
retailed by the ’dozen, even in N p.iv York ;so there
are evidently exceptions to the general rule. In
Melbourne green peas are offered at so much per
lb. ; in Hobart and Adelaide they are invariably
sold by the peck or bushel. In four of the six Aus-
tralian capitals it is customary to purvey fish by
weight; Perth and Hobart fishmongers appear to
get along without scales. (Hobart fish-hawkers
don’t even carry a foot-rule of a tape-measure,
though it is not uncommon to hear ’em yell: “’Ere
y’ are, missis; a yard o’ barracoota f’r sixpence!”)
Isn’t it about -time we had uniformity of trading
terms and methods throughout Australia? The pre-
sent hotch-potch operates in restraint of trade.
Writer, who resides in Sydney when he isn’t living
in Brisbane or Hobart or Adelaide or Christchurch
or on the high seas, would often go to Melbourne
for sixpennorth of pears or similar vegetables if
he knew for certain beforehand how many Bon
Cretins he’d get for his coin. But who, not being
an expert weight-guesser, would risk making himself
[• look ridiculous by buying fruit by the lb. in a
i public place “Twopenee-ha’penny a pound” conveys
no notion to a fellow as to whether he will want
a microscope to find his six-pennorth or a Wheel-
barrow to cart it away.
A captured burglar breathed this in the ear of
a Melbourne pressman: “This habit of sleeping,
I out is playing the deuce with one of the most suc-
cessful branches of our business. You can’t climb
a balcony now with any certainty that you won’t
step on the stomach of a fat and greasy citizen
whose sudden grunt wakens the town. One dark
night- I scaled a St. liilda balcony, parted two
blinds, and clambered over the ornamental iron
work plump into a bed, and a stifled voice said
petulantly: ‘No you don’t, you brute! You can
sleep on the floor if you are not sober enough to
take your boots off.’ At the same moment a foot
planted in the small of my back' shot me into the
brick wall. I returned as I went, barring a swell-
ing the size of a fishball.”
THE CHAMPAGNE DELUSION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The object of a Bill passed by the French Senate
on February 16 is to prevent firms from put-
ting up into champagne bottles, and selling as
“champagne,” cheap and frequently “alien” pro-
ducts, the cost price of which is under a franc a
bottle. —News item.
What price the lotion termed champagne
In this far-distant region—
The “wine” of noodles, young and vain,
Which Gwendoline (erst Mary Jane)
Reveres as a religion?
The stuff that squattahs give their pals
Derives, its fizz from chemicals,
And helps to soften Gussie’s brain,
The while he wets his throttle;
What price our Extra-dry Champagne?
It costs one franc per bottle!
The rural “toff,” the city “nob,”
The cad who backs a winner,
The Jew who gets a “skinner,”
The brokers who the public rob,
The price they pay is fifteen bob,
For wine with caf6 dinner —
Imported fizz, from Godknowswhere;
To ’suage his thirst or drown his care,
To please his “tart” or dull his pain,
An unreflecting sot’ll
Pay fifteen bob for Dry Champagne—
Cost price, one franc a bottle!
Did’st ever drag an aching head
From brilliant “social function,”
Where visitors are wined and fed
By caterers who earn their bread
Without the least compunction?
The limekiln mouth, the burning eye
Of wedding guests who long to die
And sleep, ignoring Wind and rain,
Beneath the good old wattle —
These troubles come of choice Champagne ;
Cost price, one franc a bottle 1

One of Sydney’s old homes —The
Rangers, at Mosman—possesses an Oswald
Brierly picture painted on one of the dining-
room walls, the full width of the room, and
measuring 10ft. x 20ft. The subject is “ H.M.S.
Rattlesnake in a Squall off Timor,” and was pre-
sumably suggested by a cruise the artist took in
the vessel in 1848 when she was stationed in Aus-
tralian waters. The story goes that Brierly, who
was afterwards knighted and appointed Marine
Painter to Queen Victoria, painted the picture as
a return for the hospitality of Bloxsome, the
owner of the hou.se. Bloxsome, in 1844, bought
40 antes of North Shore ground for £lOO, and
built the quaint old Rangers. Subsequently
Henry Stuart Russell, one of the Darlimr Downs
pioneers, bought it, along with a further 129 acres,
and spent £lO,OOO on the place.

sonal” advt., excelled itself recently when the
dailies had an appeal, running to nearly half a
column, from an Adelaide husband. Samples :

displease, and be obedient to you as long as I live; and
as a bond to my promise I will put £5OO more in your
bank, and if I break that promise you can keep that
money end sa.v good-bye to me. . . Your sister [full
name given] has been brought to bed through worry, arid
is in a very critical state of health. If anything happened
to her it would be sin on your soul as long as you live.
Ou receipt of your letter, she forced Bill to come with me
and leave the horses idle. You can ask Bill yours If. ~
My dear Florence, if not on me, have mercy on your
sister,-as she is confined to her bed, and idle horses to
feed, and six childien.to keep, arid Bill is to stop here till
he finds you If you are determined, I can’t force you
to come to me ... For God’s sake, on receipt of this
news, give instructions at the police station or the Aus-
tralian Club Hotel to arrange an interview with both, or
Bill only first.
