BARRON GORGE.
MR. DONALD McDONALD (the well-known writer)
describes the trip as follows: —
the greater part of the journey on the Queensland coast the steamer runs between the
P Great Barrier Reef and the mainland, making it one of the smoothest of the sea trips of Aus-
tralasia. The route is dotted with picturesque islands, covered with foliage strange to
southern eyes, and the boat is ev'er rounding headlands, streaming into fine bays and estuaries. The
famous Whitsunday Passage, between Mackay and Bowen, is extremely picturesque.”

from debility with much loss of weight, and could not regain strength. The
doctor said I was in a decline and ordered me away. I determined instead to
give Angier’s Emulsion a trial, and I am very pleased that I did so, for the
result has been most gratifying. My friends are greatly astonished at the

better now than I have been for many years. Ido not wish you to publish my
name, but will gladly answer any enquiries that may come to you concerning
this testimonial. (Name and address furnished privately).
Angier's Emulsion is invaluable in all throat and lung affections ; also for digestive

IN BANANA LAND
The record rainfall of North Queens-
land was put up recently by Kuranda, at
the back o’ Cairns. Two yards in three
days did the weeping heavens bestow; and
Geraldton, that used to boast about a yard a
month, has turned green with envy.
It’s an ill wind that blows nobody good :
and the recent cyclone and floods up North
have served Port Douglas’s turn. That
hamlet has for years cherished a grievance,
that it and not Cairns—“the mud flat”—
should be the port for the Cairns hinter-
land and the mineral country uevond the
range. Now it is the port. The Cairns-
Ivuranda railroad having been washed away
once more, all mails and passengers are
being taken via Douglas.
The North is spoiling the record of a lot
of dismal prophets. When Premier Dig.
Denham was up at the top end the other
day, he was fed on apples which came from
an orchard at Herberton; and, according to
Dig., who ought to know something about
apples—he comes from Zummerzet—they
were as good as those grown at Stanthorpe,
the best apple-growing district in Banana-
land. And a climate which grows good
apples can generally be reckoned on to grow
good Adams and Eves.
_ Bananaland is trying to make its Univer-
sity something more than a Brisbane continu-
ation school for the sons of the Fat. Northern-
ers are asking the Government to add £2500
to the promised endowment, so that the Senate
may establish night classes and long-distance
correspondence courses. It is practically
settled, too, that Mt. Morgan, Maryborough
and; Ipswich, which have handy workshops,
shall be centres for engineering instruction,
so that the sooty-faced youth need not bolt
from his work to settle in Brisbane,
The luck of Edward Costello, of Thane’s
Creek (Warwick), knocks out even that of
the proverbial Chow. At a recent Land
Commissionei , ’s Court Costello made separate
applications for four portions of Talgai, all
adjoining, and went to the ballot, with a
number of other applicants. He won all
four ballots, and, thus securing 770 acres,
may consider himself a made man. But this
kind of contingency should be guarded
against by legislation. The ballot in this
case has substituted one budding monopolist
for four bona-fide selectors.
Freddy Brentnall, M.L.C., of the Brisbane
Telegraph, provoked huge amusement in
Queensland lately by writing to the Courier
and plaintively adjuring all li-ishmen and
Home Rulers to be true to their principles,
and vote “No” on Referendum Day. Con-
sidering that Ireland, the Pope and Home
Rule have for years been the prime abhor-
rence of the Brentnall-Cowlishaw crowd, this
appeal from Brentnall struck Brisbane as
being the “steepest” thing in a “steep” cam-
paign. It was as though Judkins had been
heard crying aloud to the boozers to be true
to themselves on local option day, and vote
Beer.
It is about time Queensland gave up pre-
tending that its “public” hospitals are main-
tained by private subscriptions, and should
therefore continue to be managed or mis-
managed by private committees. Some five
years ago the Brisbane repairing-house,
which was at that time in the usual finan-
cial straits, “borrowed” £lO,OOO from the
Morgan Government, to tide it over its diffi-
culties. Since then things have gone from
bad to worse, and now Premier Denham an-
nounces an increase in endowment to all
hospitals on a basis of £2 subsidy to every
£1 subscribed. Denham further hints at
giving the Brisbane institution an increase
also in its base-hospital subsidy, with the
unavowed object of helping it to pay off
the old debt to the Government. In other
words, the debt due to the Treasury is to be
met with money supplied by the Treasury.

Queensland Anglicans want to know why
the utterances of the Rev. Douglas Price
were dubbed rank heresy by Archbishop
Donaldson, while the same brand of senti-
ments uttered 200 miles further north attract
no episcopal censure whatever. Perhaps al-
lowance is made for the warmer climate, and
the archbishpp gives latitude-for latitude.

the following lovely “howlers” taken from
recent local examination papers:—
The German Emperor is called the Geysir.
Everyone needs a holiday from one year’s end to
another.

Rockhampton wool sales are now r practi-
cally a certainty. The local Harbor Board
has sent a representative amongst the squat-
ters to find out their feelings, and the report
is reassuring. Some memoers of the Board
will visit Longreach during Show week, when
Squatterdom has on its best frill, and the
support that would be given to the sales will
then be exactly determined. If, after the
Longreach Show, the members of the Board
are satisfied that they have a guarantee of
sufficient wool to create a market, the neces-
sary buildings will be erected on the town
wharf. And then in its dreams Rockhamp-
ton will see itself more clearly than ever
the capital of a new State. But the dream
won’t come true till there is unification and
reconstruction; and just at present a whole
lot of Rockhampton, like a whole lot of other
places, is misguided enough to think that
unification is the Devil.
“The Prodigal”: Zimmerle and Maurer,
who shore for the Australian championship
recently at Toowoomba, are now shearing at
the Ilfracombe wool-scour. The late rivals
are pulling out of the same pen, and their
highest tellies, lie between 170 and 180 a
day. Livingstone, who is travelling for Dal-
gety’s, visited Ilfracombe and had a yarn
with Zimmerle, and the near future may see
a contest between these two undoubted cham-
pions. Both of them are emphatic on the
point that a big tally for a day may mean
nothing, as easy sheep and the quality of
the work have to be considered. Zimmeile’s
highest tally for a day is 291, Livingstone’s
258, and Maurer’s 248 in Queensland, and
286 in N. S. Wales. Zimmerle is a 16-
stoner, Livingstone turns the beam at 13;
whilst Maurer is an ounce or so under 12st.
Diogenes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a Cynic teacher who existed in a
tub;
He never worried battling for his garments
or his grub ;
His feather bed was built of wood, his coun-
terpane of staves ;
He branded half of Greece for fools, the
other half for knaves.
He saw his fellows, money-mad, go by with
greedy roar,
He only turned within his cask, and did a
happy snore;
He answered them who gibed at him with
many a merry wheeze —-
“The King is lie who careth not,” said old
Diogenes.
He never shut his mansion on a debtor or a
dun,
Ilis door was always open till his little race
was run ;
And if his meal was scanty—just a,morsel of
his own—
In case the poor had nothing lie would give
them half his bone ;
His joy was in the moonlight and the sun-
light newly risen,
And ev’ry tie he thought a chain, and ev’ry
house a prison ;
His wine was in the brooklet and his nectar
in the breeze;
His friendly tub he made the hub of Earth —
Diogenes!
He lived within his barrel, and lie never
found it small;
\vith ease it gave him shelter, and contained
his little all;
In winter it was cosy, and in summer it
was cool.
And it served the local Cynics as a sort of
Sunday-school;
And he who was its tenant was the proudest
man in Greece,
He never feared a Tyrant, and he never
sought to please ;
He cried to Kings and Princes, and their
Parasitic Fleas —
“Away! The Tub sufiiceth for the old
Diogenes !”
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER F.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP
Brisbane, April 22, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Something like a thrill ran round the af-
ternoon tea cups' last Thursday when an
engagement was suddenly announced between
George B. Gray, eldest son of G. W.
Gray, M.L.C., and Muriel, the little daugh-
ter of Home Secretary Appel. And, as if
these young persons had not supplied enough
subject matter for our tea-talk, they
must next discover that long engagements
me absurd, and accordingly tixed the wed-
ding for the day this letter will walk abroad
in its pink frock. The marriage will prob-
ably take place at Darra, and it is quite
possible that, with a view to keeping up
their reputation for the unusual, the honey-
mooneis may join the Home Sec. rn a cruise
to the jewelled North on the Government
steamer Otter.
Next week it will simply rain orange
blossoms. Miss Joan Hart marries Mr.
Corfe, of Toowoomba, on the 27th; on the
day previous Miss Trot Hudson takes a
similar risk with station-man Moffatt. Miss
Doris de Winton and Mr. Haley (he is a
relative of Mrs. Campbell Praed) follow on,
and another sprig of an old Queensland
family, Mr. Ivory, manager of a western
station, marries Miss Somerville before many
more suns have fallen over the horizon.
At the races, the one theme of gossip was
the engagement, just unofficially announced,
between Cecil Strange Delpratt, manager for
the John Collins firm at Inkerman station,
ana Miss Jessie Persse, youngest daughter
of Mr. and Mrs. De Burgh Persse, of Tabra-
galba, Beaudesert. The very popular pair
were born and grew up on neighboring sta-
tions.
Frockmg at Ascot "was by no means smart.
As a matter of fact, the weather had been
so hot that most people had put off getting
their clothes till late, and when the cool
snap appeared the cupboard was bare of
anything but the left-over rags of summer.
So dressy folks mostly stayed at home, or
spent a day by the salt and sodden sea.
The Douglas Price presentation amounted
to a substantial sum, expressed in three
figures, a gold watch and an ornate address.
The good little Rev., Whose sin was that he
dared to think and reason, and 1 thus came
to loggerheads with the Archgaiters, departed
by the Omrah.
The news of the wedding at Nelson, Brit-
ish Columbia, of Miss Gladys Ralfe and Mr.
Holmes A’Court trickles along the cables.
The match produces some queer relation-
ships. The lady who is mother, of the
bride married the Hon. Holmes. A’Court,
who is father of the bridegroom; so that the
bride is step-sister by marriage to her hus-
band, and her step-father is also her father-
in-law.
Winter has been getting his cold-air plant
into working order lately, and the long night
and the short two-step descend upon us arm
in arm. The allied mayoresses are busily
arranging the big hop to take place during
Coronation week, on behalf of the hard-up
General Hospital. I hear also that the Com-
mercial Rowing men are to pull off a similar
event; but somehow the big ball as a popu-
lar form of amusement is getting out of
date, and the small private dance waltzes
more and more into public favor. Many of
these informal toe sprees are coming on, and
it is said that the Vision from Government
House will make her first “ball” appearance
in the State at one of them.
An old Queensland identity passed away
in Hobart last week, in the person of Mrs.
John Forth, a sprightly old lady, whose 76
years bore lightly upon her head. She came
from Wiltshire (Eng.), and remembered this
city in the days when you either journeyed
by bullock-waggon or walked.
Another of the Townsville Allens- —Chris
-—burned himself out in a Sydney hospital
-last week. Samuel Allen founded one of
the most affluent of Northern businesses,
and, though he worked hard, he lived on till
nearly 80. The sons, tremendous workers
of the nervous type, can’t follow in the old
man’s footsteps. Three years ago Sam died
at about 48; Chris, at 46, followed last
week.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.
Apropos the recent loss of the Yongala,
and the shockingly poor lighting of that part
of the dangerous Bananaland coast, 1 it trans
pires that between Cape Bowling Green
lighthouse and the one on Dent Island, there
is a stretch of 105 miles without a light; to
the south there is another grim 100 miles;
and between Percy Island and Cape Capri-
corn there is a black chasm of 125 miles.

WHEAT FARMS on
EASY TERMS
TO every man with good red blood in his veins the free, healthy, open
life of the country must stand out in marked and favorable contrast
to that spent amidst the drab and cramped surroundings and
monotonous routine of the office. Many men who are slaves to the desk
would gladly take up land if they had the means to do so, and if they
could avoid the physical hardships of the pioneer. Many would gladly
embark their sons on this career if they could do so at a reasonable outlay
and with a certain prospect of their ultimate success. It is for such as
these that we afford the preset opportunity of acquiring WHEAT
FARMS ON EASY TERMS. A very moderate outlay of capital is all
that is necessary. The blocks we offer now in many instances have
been under cultivation, and the remainder can be made ready for the
plough at an expenditure of about ss. per acre.
Homesteads have been erected in many instances, and tanks and dams
are ready for immediate use.
The reputation of the soil for wheat-growing is famous throughout
the State, and mixed-farming and stock-raising flourish in the district.
THERE IS BIG MONEY IN WHEAT-GROWING, and many
have found it a short cut to wealth and independence. The demand for
wheat never let's up —the market is always asking for more; and if you
work with a will you cannot help but win success.
RINGWOOD ESTATE is amongst the best
of the lahd in the Parkes-Condobolin District,
which produced last year over two million
bushels of wheat, and this season will show a
further increase on that great total. The wheat
produced in this district is of the finest quality,
and is much sought after by flour-millers. The
Estate consists of 17,120 acres of beautiful
chocolate loam, capable of growing an immense
crop. It has been subdivided into 30 blocks of
about 500 acres each. The property is fenced
in rabbit-proof netting, and many of the blocks
are practically complete in this respect.
RINGWOOD is some six miles from Ootha
Railway Siding, by good road, and half-way
between Bogan Gate and Condobolin, the ter-
minus of the line. Some 3500 acres have been
under cultivation. The balance is either open,
park-like land, or is lightly timbered. The trees
have been ringbarked for many years, and the
land can be cleared for the plough for about ss.
per acre. SOME OF THE BLOCKS HAVE
HOMESTEADS, AND ARE READY FOR
IMMEDIATE OCCUPATION.
THE RAINFALL is ample for successful
wheat-growing. Records taken at the home-
stead during the past five or six years show that
the rainfall has averaged about 20 inches. The
Estate is well served with tanks and dams, no
RINGWOOD.
fewer than eleven of the blocks being provided
for in this way.
THE TITLE.— The land is to be sold as
certificated conditional purchase, all conditions
fulfilled, and buyers may convert into Freehold
at any time. You get immediate possession,
and have all the advantages of a conditional
purchase WITHOUT A BALLOT.
The prices of this land as conditional
purchases range from 275. to 89s. per acre;
or Freehold prices, including all moneys
due to the Crown, 465. to 965. per acre.
WHERE CAN YOU GET SUCH LAND
AT SUCH A LOW PRICE ?
OUR EASY TERMS:
Ten per cent, deposit. No further pay-
ment for two years. The balance in 16
half-yearly payments or eight yearly pay-
ments, bearing interest at 5 per cent.,
payable half-yearly.
WHERE CAN YOU GET SUCH LAND
ON SUCH EASY TERMS ?

Mr. W. S. KEAST, M.L.A., of Dandenong,
Victoria, was interviewed recently by Sydney
Evening News reporter, on the question of
Land Settlement. Among other things, Mr.
Keast said :—“ In the Parkes district for
hundreds of miles, around such centres as
Forbes, Bogan Gate, Condobolin, etc., there are
millions of acres suitable for wheat farms. The
country is practically all cereal land, and adapted
for mixed-farming, with sheep-breeding. In
parts of Victoria, where the rainfall is 15 inches
(Ringwood averages 20), under dry-farming
methods we average 16 bushels of wheat to the
acre, and this district should produce more
than that.”
This is Convincing
Enough!
Send now for Plans, Schedule of Prices and
full particulars; or, better still, call and talk the
matter over.

As the yarns go round, the blue smoke
curling upwards in a fragrant clqud, the
smooth cool Havana flavor lingering on your

happiest hours of your life.

WHAT IS CATARRH ?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.
RAMEY'S MEDICATOR cures Catarrh,
Hay Fever, Catarrhal Deafness, Head-
ache, Neuralgia, Colds, Bronchitis, Asth-
ma. Write for booklet free, or send
Postal Note 10s. for Medicator complete,
treatment, post free anywhere.

Congo Roofing is a tough,
pliable water-proof felt that
will stand any amount of
wind and weather. It can
be laid by unskilled labor,
and with painting at intervals
of four years, will give ten
years of faultless service.
Nails and cement are sup-
plied free of charge. Each
roll contains 218 square feet.
Sample and prices on request.
Congo Damp Course is a
tough felt somewhat similar
to Congo Roofing, which is
immune from deterioration
by dampness or acids. It is
used for lining the exterior
foundations of buildings to
exclude dampness and ver-
min. It is made in various
widths and is easily applied.
It is the most economical
kind of underground water-
proofing.

Beautiful LIZETTE PARKES
EXTOLS
Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery
MISS LIZETTE PARKES is an Australasian Favorite. Her career is a triumph of
both beauty and brains. She recently won two beauty competitions in New
Zealand —one in Christchurch (a beautiful gold bangle), and the other in Wellington
(a silver jewel case, which was presented to her by Sir Joseph and Lady Ward). Her stage
career has been a singularly versatile and brilliant one, with the J. C. Williamson and
Meynell and Clarke firms. At present she is with the Hamilton-Plimmer-Denniston
Co., and is greatly delighting her admirers, particularly in the sweet character of Sim-
l
plicity Johnson in “Lover's
Lane.” New Zealand loves
Miss Parkes best as Peter
Pan—a r6le in which Aus-
tralians have not yet been
fortunate enough to see her.
“At the close of ‘Peter Pan’
at the Opera House,’’ said
the N.Z. Tjmics (28/8/To),
“ Miss Parkes had to re-
spond to the record number
of 12 curtain calls.” Re-
specting Miss Parkes in the
same role, the N.Z. Triad
said : “ She was a dearly
loved Peter Pan. She im-
pressively had the proper
spirit, the fairy grace, the
melody of voice, the lissom
feet.” Congratulating itself
on its foresight, the Sydney
Bulletin just recently said :
“Some years back The
Bulletin prophesied a big
future foy clevfcf Lizette
Parkes, and the prophecy is
materialising daily.” In the
following letter, Miss Lizette
Parkes points the way to per-
fect freedom from Coughs
and Colds to all sufferers
from Chest Complaints.

The Descent of Gadsbad.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
GADSBAD was tired of everything.
Gadsbad was a successful solicitor
with a nice, extravagant wife and a
handsome, extravagant daughter; and on his
50th birthday the fact struck him that he got
nothing much out of life for himself but a lot
of hard toil. He was not a brilliant man,
what success he had attained was due to
steady, conservative reputation and incessant
attention to his work. A lot of P^P 1 ® .“j
eluding his own family— were under the im-
pression that Gadsbad’s work was all he
wanted in this life or the next; whereas as
a matter of fact, the back of his brain was a
library of unfulfilled holiday dreams which
had been accumulating from the tar-back,
dusty year when lie had started piactice.
When a man or a machine goes too long
without a rest, the flesh or the steel takes a
rest of its own accord. So, in spite or his
grim determination to keep on, Gadsbad teit
that his grip on things was relaxing. He
realised that he was on the edge of some
sort of abyss, and that it would only take
a very slight push to send him over the
edge. He walked along the street thinking
about it, and, when a whining voice asked
him to buy a box of matches, he said No
roughly, and pushed on.
“Only a penny, boss!”
“No, I tell you !”
“Well, give’s a copper!”
Gadsbad stppped and looked angrily at the
slinking beggar at his elbow. “Why should
I give you anything?” he demanded. I
work hard for my living.”
The beggar shuffled and muttered some-
thing about having no work to do.
Gadsbad sneered. “ You don’t want work,
my man. Don’t tell me you want work.”
“Oo said I did? I said I ’adn’t got no
work t’ do!”
“You’re pretty frank, my friend,” said
Gadsbad. “Aren’t you ashamed of your-
self?”
“No!” answered the able-bodied vagrant.
“I don’t see no reason w’y I should be_! I
.don’t see w’y a man should work f’r ’is livin’
if ’e can get it without. S’pose you ’ad ten
thousan’ a year, would you work?”
“No, I wouldn’t!”
“Well, then, you’d be a loafer too if you
could. Buy a box o’ matches, Boss!”
But Gadsbad was not content to let the
.discussion drop at that point. He told the
man to follow him into a dingy hotel in a
small street close by, and, ordering a pint of
beer, called for a whisky and soda himself,
and attacked the vagrant as to his views on
life. , <
“Why don’t you work?” he inquired.
“Becos I’m no good at it, an’ I ’ate it!”
“And are you content to go on like this?”
“Yes, this sorter life soots me better’n
work. Work is ’arder t’ me than bein’ called
a dirty blackguard an’ a loafer, an’ gettin’
run in now and then, an’ ’avin’ t’ doss out.
I ’ate worry an’ bein’ bossed about; I carn’t
stan’ it! This is easier than bein’ re-spect-
able once ye’ve got used to it.” The vagrant
took a long pull at his beer, and wiped his
mouth on his dirty sleeve.
Gadsbad glanced at him with disgust.
“Were you ever respectable?”
“Yes, I was,” said the man defiantly, “but
I got tired of it. ’Ad a little grocer’s shop
an’ a good, naggin’ wife. I was 15 year try-
in’ t’ make up me mind t’ clear out. I took
five quid one day and left ’er. Useter ’avh
black fits now and then f’r a year or so;
useter worry about ’er an’ the’ little ’ome com-
forts, me friends, an’ so on. Me pride died
’ard, too! It useter ’urt me at first when
pleecemen pushed me about ’sif I was rub-
bish. I wasn’t quite rubbish then... But I
am now !... Oh ! it all took time ! Y’r carn’t
drop down t’ this way o’ livin’ all at once’;
it’s almos’ as ’ard as workin’ up if a man ’as
any pride in ’im. An’ I ’ad pride! I let
meself go, an’ w’en I found meself wantin’
t’ go back I fought agin it. I’m right down
now' —down as far as I can go. I ain’t man-
aged t’ forget everythin’—wish I could.. .Oh !
but I’m right outer that old, frettin’, worry-
in’ life —or underneath it. I don’t care about
nothin’, and I worry about nothin’ —what’s
th’ use? S’pose I gets sick, eh? Well, wot
if I do? Ain’t ther’ plenty o’ big, lazy
Johns as will wheel me t’ th’ ’orspital, an’
won’t pretty nurses with their noses in th’
air look after me? What are they there for,
eh? It’s a lot better’n graftin’ y’r soul-case
out, an’ worryin’ yerself almos’ mad tryin’
t’ scrape enough tergether t’ pay a doctor’s
bill. Oh! I know all about that! I was
re-sp‘ectable once, an’ paid me way like a
honest man. But th’ strain of it broke me !
. . , I carn’t tell yer, but I ’ad a lot o’ trouble
—nothin’ outer th’ ordin’ry—deaths, an’ sick-
ness, an’ general worry..../ dunno! I
s’pose there was somethin’ weak about me.,
but it got that way I knew it was th’ ’sylum
—or worse.... So I cleared out, an’ —an’ ’ere
I am! Seems mad, don’t it?” The vagrant
looked up at the family solicitor with a half-
defiant air, but the corners of his miserable
mouth were twitching, and his eyes were
moist with misery.
Gadsbad wanted to comfort him, for,
strangely enough, he felt sorry for the poor
wretcli who had gone under. But he saw
that any expression of sympathy would prob-
ably produce tears. The possibility was too.
awful to contemplate, and he hurriedly or-
dered more beer, with some dim idea of
swamping the emotion which the recital had
caused.
While the wreck was greedily sucking at
his tankard, Gadsbad pressed a half-crown
into his hand, with several muttered lies.
“It’s a waste of money, but you’ve interested
me. I haven’t a bit of sympathy for you,
mind! I think you are a cur to go down as
you have, and a d —d fool, too. Anyhow,
there you are! Now, you’d better clear
out.”
The outcast hitched at his belt and sneeredi
“Orright,” he muttered, “I’m goin’! < It
ain’t orfen I gets ’arf a dollar f’r tellin’ tIF
g).spel truth about meself! Good-night,
oss ! An’ thank yer !” .
r £h ( e awing doors closed with a bang, and
he #as gone like an uficlean spirit, the empty
tankard on the counter the only tangible
proof that he had ever been.
Gadsbad left the hotel a minute later, and
walked home thinking hard about the
vagrant. He found himself to a certain ex-
tent sympathising with the coward who had
deliberately relinquished all effort, and gone
—down the hill. It was conceivably that .a
% ’ man of small courage and'low brain capacity
might find the burden of life too Heavy.
Why, of course, wasn’t suicide a common-
place thing? Didn’t men and women kill
themselves every day? Suppose a man had
reached the stage of utter exhaustion, yet
still was unwilling to end his life, what
alternative had he? Clearly, there was no-
thing for him to do but go under as this poor
wretch had done. It was disgusting that
any human being should prefer that sort of
thing to clean, decisive death; but the weak-
ling who chose it was entitled to the useless
and helpless sympathy which all extended to
the . suicide. One could do nothing for a
dead body except bury it; a live body could at
least spend a sympathetic half-crown on
beer. Gadsbad smiled cynically at the
thought as he entered his front gate and
walked slowly up the path towards the front
door. He was feeling very tired.
Mrs. Gadsbad was sitting in the morn-
ing-room reading when he entered, and
Phyllis, his daughter, was writing an ac-
ceptance to an invitation received that
“How white you look!” His wife low-
ered her book for an instant with a frown
of annoyance, and the tinkle of her num-
erous bracelets made an irritating accom-
paniment for her voice. And how wor-
ried !” .
“Do I?” muttered the tired man.
“I don’t know why you should look so,”
said Mrs. Gadsbad fretfully. “It’s hor-
ribly depressing to see anyone going about
like a ghost. So far as I can see, there
is no necessity for it!” She resumed her
book with an impatient movement, whilst
Gadsbad leant wearily on the mantelpiece
and stared at the fire.
Phyllis suspended writing and surveyed
him critically. Most people said that
Phyllis “took after” her mother; if she
didn’t, then her mother took before Phyllis.
They were both tiresome, comfortless wo-
men. . . ,
“Father!” the voice was very clear and
distinct, and very — very patient. “Father,
why do you work so hard?”
Instead of demurring helplessly, as was'
his wont when such queries were flung at
him, Gadsbad made a new, bright and orig-
inal reply. He said, “I really don’t know,
Phyllis.”' Then he moaned slightly, as if
in sudden pain, and fell fainting into the
fender.
Utter confusion supervened. The house-
maid and handy man were summoned with
all haste, and, between them, they lifted
the insensible man on to the sofa, while
Mrs. Gadsbad and Phyllis looked on, mute
and helpless with terror. Gadsbad, a cruel,
jagged wound over one eye, and bis face
streaked with blood —lay practically un-
attended until the doctor arrived with a
nurse in his motor. Then the wound was
dressed, and he was carried upstairs still
unconscious, and put to bed. His wife sat
by his bedside waiting for him to recover
his senses; but it was well on towards 11
o’clock before he opened his eyes, and said
in a drowsy voice, “Buy er box er matches,
Boss !”
“M'illiam!” Mrs. Gadsbad started up
half-asleep and looked wildly into her hus-
band’s face. “Do you feel better, Wil-
liam?”
“Buy ; er box er matches, Boss !” whined
the injured man, his voice a dreadful echo
of the ’ beggar’s'.
"“William! Don’t you know me? Mrs.
Gndsbad shook him slightly in her agita
tion.
“No! ’Oo are yer?”
. “Your wife.”

*dages. “No, yer don’t!” he sneered. “I
ain’t got no wife. Don’t you try any of

I didn’t come inter ’orspital to be worried !”
The distracted woman shook him again.
“You are wandering! This isn’t a hospi-

“Look ’ere, Missus, vou shut up or I’ll

Gli, nonsense! Don’t you remember?

Scalp Troubles.
MOTHERS find it very difficult to
guard against unsightly and dis-
tressing diseases of the scalp among
the little ones, especially as some such
troubles are very catching; and once they
have developed it is more difficult still to
find a cure.
Eczema (in the familiar form of scald-
head), honey-scab, dandruff and ring-
worm are all scalp affections for which
Zam-Buk has proved most efficacious. Whether the form of the eruption be
running sores, dry scabs or scales, or inflamed patches, Zam-Buk is alike
soothing and curative.
In cases of ringworm Zam-Buk has achieved remarkable success. Rubbed
gently into the itchy red patches, this pure balm soaks through the diseased
tissue, and kills the microscopic fungus-plants which are the cause of the trouble.
Mothers should keep Zam-Buk handy, and dress daily any part of the skin or
scalp which presents the least suspicion of disease.

A month passed away, and Gadsbad was
walking about the garden. At intervals he
asked truculently when was he “goin’ t’ be
discharged frotn this ’ere ’orspital.” All at-
tempts to convince him that he was in his
own home only served to put him in a pas-
sion, so the specialist strongly advised Mrs.
Gadsbad to pose as the matron, and leave it
to Nature to put things right. However,
Nature seemed disinclined to take on the
contract. Gadsbad gradually grew strong
and well in body, but his brain retained its
madness. He . would not, or could not, speak
decent English, he remembered nothing, and,
worst of all, his old habits cf personal clean-
liness began to be forgotten.
It was a long time before Mrs. Gadsbad
could be brought to understand that by no-
thing less than an out-size in miracles could
her husband ever be able to go to the office
again. And the necessary miracle didn’t
look like happening. Instead, Gadsbad the
family solicitor sank rapidly into the miser-
able state of the chronic loafer. He had
not the necessary qualifications either for a
public hospital or a lunatic asylum, even had
Mrs. Gadsbad been willing to contemplate
such a solution of the dreadful problem. He
certainly was not ill, and he wasn’t mad
in the ordinary sense. Simply his fall had
put out of action the brain department which
keeps a man alive and stirring whilst life is
in him. All efforts to awaken some recollec-
tions of his old life—and many were made by
liis wife—were in vain. The habits, train-
ing, memories and ambitions of a lifetime
had been wiped out the instant the fender
edge dug into his head. So, after six
months had gone, Mrs. Gadsbad began to
realise that it would have been better had
her husband died that tragic night. The
fearful caricature that lounged about the
garden represented something worse than
death both to itself and to those who, if they
had not loved the original, at least had re-
spected it.
People—especially selfish people— must
live; so, After the first shock was over,
Mrs. Gadsbad and Phyllis resumed to a great
extent the old social round. This necessi-
tated all sorts of mean expedients to keep
Gadsbad out of evidence. When callers were
expected Gadsbad became the, care of his
own gardener—a small, tyrannical man who
keenly resented being made the keeper of a
lunatic. He made poor Gadsbad so wretch-
edly unhappy that the poor wreck cleared out
one night, and was only found by the police
after a week’s search. When discovered,
he was sleeping happily underneath a wharf
and he objected strongly to being disturbed.
They conveyed him home, where he remained
under protest for three days. At the end
of that time he made off again, and was not
recaptured for a fortnight. Once more he
was forcibly returned to the home, which was
kept going on a very reduced scale by his
wife, who, fortunately, had a small income
of her own. But he escaped again, and this
time no effort was made to bring him back.
They had, so they argued, barely sufficient
for themselves; and, even supposing they
had more, the poor man didn’t seem, happy
with then!? So of what vise was it worry-
ing? Thus it came to pass that Gadsbad
dropped out of, sight of his wife and child,
and went down to the depths.
Now, the story of Gadsbad would have
ended there had it not been for a legacy and
a newspaper man. The newspaper man
didn’t get the legacy (newspaper men never
do), but a vindictive Providence selected
from its armory these two totally dissimilar
instruments to re-open Gadsbad’s case, and
to wreak vengeance on his wife and daughter.
The.blow did not fall for two years after
Gadsbad’s third and final disappearance.
During the interval, Mrs. Gadsbad and
Phyllis settleu down to a steady two-handed
game of Society, for small stakes, and they
were getting along nicely when something
died unexpectedly and left Mrs. Gadsbad the
stupendous amount of £200,000. Wealth is
almost as hard to suppress as murder; the
news of the windfall swept all over the place,
and congratulations were pelting in by post
and wire, when a grislv article bobbed up on
the surface of an evening paper. It was
labelled “A Romance of Real Life,” and it
told the story of Gadsbad, with an atten-
tion to detail that was inhuman. It
contrasted the future comfort of the wife and
-daughter with the utter destitution of the
■afflicted husband, and it left Mrs. Gadsbad
;and Phyllis no alternative but to go and res-
cue the man who had worked for them for so
many years. They were spared the trouble
<of searching for the lost one —the evening
paper man had basely located him for them.
Gadsbad had become a minstrel of the slums,
and sang for pennies in the streets, mostly
outside dingy hotels where the prevailing
brand of beer made people feel benevolent.
Two days after the publication of the ar-
ticle, a cab with two white-faced women in
it came slowly down a street in Woolloo-
mooloo towards a small hotel, in front of
which a battered object was hoarsely shout-
ing a song. The words echoed unheeded
in the dingy thoroughfare:
I know she thinks abart ’im,
And hindwardly she frets.

“What is ut ye’re wantin, ma’am?” asked

Mrs. Gadsbad nodded at Gadsbad’s back

The man made a gesture of disgust. “Don’t
give th’ dhirty loafer yer good money, ma’am.
He’s a nuisance, so he is !”
“But I merely want to speak”
“Don’t spake to him, ma’am! He isn’t

“Ere, what’s it go’ t’ do with you?”
Bozzy wheeled round and approached the
cab cautiously. Bozzy was the match-seller
who had approached Gadsbad on that fateful

patron one night in the Domain, and they
had formed a sort of travelling partnership.
Bozzy was Gadsbad’s manager; he arranged
Gadsbad’s suburban concert tours, and did
the collecting. As a business man of sorts,
he recognised that money might be made out
of the strange lady’s interest in his star. So
he approached the cab with almost as much
confidence as a dog well accustomed to be
kicked.
“What is it, lady?” he asked hungrily.
“I want to speak to that man, please.”
Bozzy coughed slightly, and came a step
closer. “T’ Billo, lady?”
“Yes —to Billo !”
Bozzy shook his head and looked doubt-
fully at Gadsbad. “’E ain’t too strong'in
the ’ead, lady. Me an’ ’im’s together—p.n'
I kin tell yer anythin’ y’ want t’ know....
Would y’ rather speak t’ ’im yerself, lady?”
“Yes; will you bring him here, please ?”
Bozzy turned round obediently. “Billo,
yer wanted !”
Gadsbad stepped off the kerb and brushed
past the indignant and dumbfounded publi-
can. He walked towards the cab, but he
kept his eyes on Bozzy’s face. “Does she
want me to sing, Bozzy?”
“Do you remember me, William?” asked
the wife.
Gadsbad looked her straight in the eyes
and answered respectfully, “No, missus.”
But Bozzy, in the background, suddenly
made a frenzied dive at his breast-pocket,
and pulled out a sheet of newspaper with
jam stains on it. He unfolded it with trem-
bling fingers, and, pushing Gadsbad to one ,
side, extended it in front of the fashionably-
dressed woman. “Is—is this ’ere true?”
he demanded breathlessly. “Is Billo th’
missin’ one?”
Mrs. Gadsbad nodded miserably.
Bozzy turned to the publican and waved
the account of the “Gadsbad Romance” at
him. “That’s one in yer duck-’ouse, Riley !
This lady 'ere is Billo’s missus!!”
Then the publican temporarily lost his rea-
son altogether. He stamped alternately
with both feet, spat in the gutter and
shouted. “Ye’re a pair av dhirty black-
guards !” After which he rushed inside and
slammed the side door after him with a crash
that shook the whole shanty.
And outside, Mrs. Gadsbad and Bozzy
spent several minutes inducing Gadsbad to
get into the cab. He was very reluctant to
do so, and Bozzy had to explain that he too
was going for a ride before he would consent
to climb in. Finally they all drove off to-
gether, and the publican watched them from
a top window, and requested Heaven to tell
him whether he was mad or not.
Gadsbad and Bozzy now have a small cot-
tage to themselves somewhere down in Wool-
loomooloo. There is no necessity for Gads-
bad to sing for pennies, but he thinks there
is, and. as it is quite useless to argue with
him, Mrs. Gadsbad has simply to try and
avoid seeing or hearing him. But Gadsbad
wanders far and wide, and sings pretty well
all round the clock, so it is impossible to
avoid him altogether. He only sings the
one awful song, and, when you examine it
critically and remember the circumstances of
the case, you can’t help feeling sorry for
Mrs. Gadsbad.

KODAK.
Moonshine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ALL the yellow hair of night,
Tumbled in the golden moon,
Ripples into waves of light,
Touched with melody and tune.
Olden links of broken song
In a sleepy whisper die
Where the timid shadows throng,
Hiding from the splendid sky.
This full hour is mine and thine ;
Ours to hold, in cups of dre^m,
All that vintage of clear wine
Eldest summers set astream.
Lotus kisses set afloat,
Living lips, and eyes that smile,
Lapping waters where the boat
Breaks the mirror of the Nile.
Jewel-wrought, a sandal flames,
Pearl enwoven robes go by,
Youth and love have tender names
’Neath the unforgetting sky.
Sighing towards the city wall,
Wakes a strain that trembles on,
Where the sloping shadows fall
O’er the gold of Babylon.
Clear the pathway of long fire
On Levantine waves that swell,
Where a balcony of Tyre
Breathes the name of Isabel.
Grecian meadows dewy sweet,
Dells where fountain music drips,
Youngest gods have lucent feet,
Oreads have rosy lips.
Kentish orchards rich with bloom,-
Whispered converse in the shade,
Castle arch and vaulted room,
• Norman speech and Saxon maid.
All the world is mine and thine,
Gathered in the cups of dream,
Crystal vintage of full wine,
Eldest moonlight set astream.
Yellow hair of laughing night,
Tumbled in the windy gold—
Girl, your lips of red delight
Would have made a hermit bold !
« N.S.W.
DAVID MCKEE WRIGHT.
